461	

462	PREFACE

463	

464	

465	Most of the adventures recorded in this book really occurred; one or two

466	were experiences of my own, the rest those of boys who were schoolmates

467	of mine. Huck Finn is drawn from life; Tom Sawyer also, but not from an

468	individual—he is a combination of the characteristics of three boys whom

469	I knew, and therefore belongs to the composite order of architecture.

470	

471	The odd superstitions touched upon were all prevalent among children and

472	slaves in the West at the period of this story—that is to say, thirty or

473	forty years ago.

474	

475	Although my book is intended mainly for the entertainment of boys and

476	girls, I hope it will not be shunned by men and women on that account,

477	for part of my plan has been to try to pleasantly remind adults of what

478	they once were themselves, and of how they felt and thought and talked,

479	and what queer enterprises they sometimes engaged in.

480	

481	THE AUTHOR.

482	

483	HARTFORD, 1876.

484	

485	

486	

487	

488	CHAPTER I

489	

490	

491	“TOM!”

492	

493	No answer.

494	

495	“TOM!”

496	

497	No answer.

498	

499	“What’s gone with that boy, I wonder? You TOM!”

500	

501	No answer.

502	

503	The old lady pulled her spectacles down and looked over them about the

504	room; then she put them up and looked out under them. She seldom or

505	never looked _through_ them for so small a thing as a boy; they were

506	her state pair, the pride of her heart, and were built for “style,” not

507	service—she could have seen through a pair of stove-lids just as well.

508	She looked perplexed for a moment, and then said, not fiercely, but

509	still loud enough for the furniture to hear:

510	

511	“Well, I lay if I get hold of you I’ll—”

512	

513	She did not finish, for by this time she was bending down and punching

514	under the bed with the broom, and so she needed breath to punctuate the

515	punches with. She resurrected nothing but the cat.

516	

517	“I never did see the beat of that boy!”

518	

519	She went to the open door and stood in it and looked out among the

520	tomato vines and “jimpson” weeds that constituted the garden. No Tom. So

521	she lifted up her voice at an angle calculated for distance and shouted:

522	

523	“Y-o-u-u TOM!”

524	

525	There was a slight noise behind her and she turned just in time to seize

526	a small boy by the slack of his roundabout and arrest his flight.

527	

528	“There! I might ’a’ thought of that closet. What you been doing in

529	there?”

530	

531	“Nothing.”

532	

533	“Nothing! Look at your hands. And look at your mouth. What _is_ that

534	truck?”

535	

536	“I don’t know, aunt.”

537	

538	“Well, I know. It’s jam—that’s what it is. Forty times I’ve said if you

539	didn’t let that jam alone I’d skin you. Hand me that switch.”

540	

541	The switch hovered in the air—the peril was desperate—

542	

543	“My! Look behind you, aunt!”

544	

545	The old lady whirled round, and snatched her skirts out of danger.

546	The lad fled on the instant, scrambled up the high board-fence, and

547	disappeared over it.

548	

549	His aunt Polly stood surprised a moment, and then broke into a gentle

550	laugh.

551	

552	“Hang the boy, can’t I never learn anything? Ain’t he played me tricks

553	enough like that for me to be looking out for him by this time? But old

554	fools is the biggest fools there is. Can’t learn an old dog new tricks,

555	as the saying is. But my goodness, he never plays them alike, two days,

556	and how is a body to know what’s coming? He ’pears to know just how long

557	he can torment me before I get my dander up, and he knows if he can make

558	out to put me off for a minute or make me laugh, it’s all down again and

559	I can’t hit him a lick. I ain’t doing my duty by that boy, and that’s

560	the Lord’s truth, goodness knows. Spare the rod and spile the child,

561	as the Good Book says. I’m a laying up sin and suffering for us both,

562	I know. He’s full of the Old Scratch, but laws-a-me! he’s my own

563	dead sister’s boy, poor thing, and I ain’t got the heart to lash him,

564	somehow. Every time I let him off, my conscience does hurt me so, and

565	every time I hit him my old heart most breaks. Well-a-well, man that is

566	born of woman is of few days and full of trouble, as the Scripture

567	says, and I reckon it’s so. He’ll play hookey this evening, * and [*

568	Southwestern for “afternoon”] I’ll just be obleeged to make him work,

569	tomorrow, to punish him. It’s mighty hard to make him work Saturdays,

570	when all the boys is having holiday, but he hates work more than he

571	hates anything else, and I’ve _got_ to do some of my duty by him, or

572	I’ll be the ruination of the child.”

573	

574	Tom did play hookey, and he had a very good time. He got back home

575	barely in season to help Jim, the small colored boy, saw next-day’s wood

576	and split the kindlings before supper—at least he was there in time

577	to tell his adventures to Jim while Jim did three-fourths of the work.

578	Tom’s younger brother (or rather half-brother) Sid was already through

579	with his part of the work (picking up chips), for he was a quiet boy,

580	and had no adventurous, trouble-some ways.

581	

582	While Tom was eating his supper, and stealing sugar as opportunity

583	offered, Aunt Polly asked him questions that were full of guile, and

584	very deep—for she wanted to trap him into damaging revealments. Like

585	many other simple-hearted souls, it was her pet vanity to believe she

586	was endowed with a talent for dark and mysterious diplomacy, and she

587	loved to contemplate her most transparent devices as marvels of low

588	cunning. Said she:

589	

590	“Tom, it was middling warm in school, warn’t it?”

591	

592	“Yes’m.”

593	

594	“Powerful warm, warn’t it?”

595	

596	“Yes’m.”

597	

598	“Didn’t you want to go in a-swimming, Tom?”

599	

600	A bit of a scare shot through Tom—a touch of uncomfortable suspicion. He

601	searched Aunt Polly’s face, but it told him nothing. So he said:

602	

603	“No’m—well, not very much.”

604	

605	The old lady reached out her hand and felt Tom’s shirt, and said:

606	

607	“But you ain’t too warm now, though.” And it flattered her to reflect

608	that she had discovered that the shirt was dry without anybody knowing

609	that that was what she had in her mind. But in spite of her, Tom knew

610	where the wind lay, now. So he forestalled what might be the next move:

611	

612	“Some of us pumped on our heads—mine’s damp yet. See?”

613	

614	Aunt Polly was vexed to think she had overlooked that bit of

615	circumstantial evidence, and missed a trick. Then she had a new

616	inspiration:

617	

618	“Tom, you didn’t have to undo your shirt collar where I sewed it, to

619	pump on your head, did you? Unbutton your jacket!”

620	

621	The trouble vanished out of Tom’s face. He opened his jacket. His shirt

622	collar was securely sewed.

623	

624	“Bother! Well, go ’long with you. I’d made sure you’d played hookey

625	and been a-swimming. But I forgive ye, Tom. I reckon you’re a kind of a

626	singed cat, as the saying is—better’n you look. _This_ time.”

627	

628	She was half sorry her sagacity had miscarried, and half glad that Tom

629	had stumbled into obedient conduct for once.

630	

631	But Sidney said:

632	

633	“Well, now, if I didn’t think you sewed his collar with white thread,

634	but it’s black.”

635	

636	“Why, I did sew it with white! Tom!”

637	

638	But Tom did not wait for the rest. As he went out at the door he said:

639	

640	“Siddy, I’ll lick you for that.”

641	

642	In a safe place Tom examined two large needles which were thrust into

643	the lapels of his jacket, and had thread bound about them—one needle

644	carried white thread and the other black. He said:

645	

646	“She’d never noticed if it hadn’t been for Sid. Confound it! sometimes

647	she sews it with white, and sometimes she sews it with black. I wish to

648	gee-miny she’d stick to one or t’other—I can’t keep the run of ’em. But

649	I bet you I’ll lam Sid for that. I’ll learn him!”

650	

651	He was not the Model Boy of the village. He knew the model boy very well

652	though—and loathed him.

653	

654	Within two minutes, or even less, he had forgotten all his troubles. Not

655	because his troubles were one whit less heavy and bitter to him than a

656	man’s are to a man, but because a new and powerful interest bore

657	them down and drove them out of his mind for the time—just as men’s

658	misfortunes are forgotten in the excitement of new enterprises. This new

659	interest was a valued novelty in whistling, which he had just acquired

660	from a negro, and he was suffering to practise it un-disturbed. It

661	consisted in a peculiar bird-like turn, a sort of liquid warble,

662	produced by touching the tongue to the roof of the mouth at short

663	intervals in the midst of the music—the reader probably remembers how to

664	do it, if he has ever been a boy. Diligence and attention soon gave him

665	the knack of it, and he strode down the street with his mouth full of

666	harmony and his soul full of gratitude. He felt much as an astronomer

667	feels who has discovered a new planet—no doubt, as far as strong, deep,

668	unalloyed pleasure is concerned, the advantage was with the boy, not the

669	astronomer.

670	

671	The summer evenings were long. It was not dark, yet. Presently Tom

672	checked his whistle. A stranger was before him—a boy a shade larger

673	than himself. A new-comer of any age or either sex was an im-pressive

674	curiosity in the poor little shabby village of St. Petersburg. This boy

675	was well dressed, too—well dressed on a week-day. This was simply

676	astounding. His cap was a dainty thing, his close-buttoned blue cloth

677	roundabout was new and natty, and so were his pantaloons. He had shoes

678	on—and it was only Friday. He even wore a necktie, a bright bit of

679	ribbon. He had a citified air about him that ate into Tom’s vitals. The

680	more Tom stared at the splendid marvel, the higher he turned up his nose

681	at his finery and the shabbier and shabbier his own outfit seemed to

682	him to grow. Neither boy spoke. If one moved, the other moved—but only

683	sidewise, in a circle; they kept face to face and eye to eye all the

684	time. Finally Tom said:

685	

686	“I can lick you!”

687	

688	“I’d like to see you try it.”

689	

690	“Well, I can do it.”

691	

692	“No you can’t, either.”

693	

694	“Yes I can.”

695	

696	“No you can’t.”

697	

698	“I can.”

699	

700	“You can’t.”

701	

702	“Can!”

703	

704	“Can’t!”

705	

706	An uncomfortable pause. Then Tom said:

707	

708	“What’s your name?”

709	

710	“’Tisn’t any of your business, maybe.”

711	

712	“Well I ’low I’ll _make_ it my business.”

713	

714	“Well why don’t you?”

715	

716	“If you say much, I will.”

717	

718	“Much—much—_much_. There now.”

719	

720	“Oh, you think you’re mighty smart, _don’t_ you? I could lick you with

721	one hand tied behind me, if I wanted to.”

722	

723	“Well why don’t you _do_ it? You _say_ you can do it.”

724	

725	“Well I _will_, if you fool with me.”

726	

727	“Oh yes—I’ve seen whole families in the same fix.”

728	

729	“Smarty! You think you’re _some_, now, _don’t_ you? Oh, what a hat!”

730	

731	“You can lump that hat if you don’t like it. I dare you to knock it

732	off—and anybody that’ll take a dare will suck eggs.”

733	

734	“You’re a liar!”

735	

736	“You’re another.”

737	

738	“You’re a fighting liar and dasn’t take it up.”

739	

740	“Aw—take a walk!”

741	

742	“Say—if you give me much more of your sass I’ll take and bounce a rock

743	off’n your head.”

744	

745	“Oh, of _course_ you will.”

746	

747	“Well I _will_.”

748	

749	“Well why don’t you _do_ it then? What do you keep _saying_ you will

750	for? Why don’t you _do_ it? It’s because you’re afraid.”

751	

752	“I _ain’t_ afraid.”

753	

754	“You are.”

755	

756	“I ain’t.”

757	

758	“You are.”

759	

760	Another pause, and more eying and sidling around each other. Presently

761	they were shoulder to shoulder. Tom said:

762	

763	“Get away from here!”

764	

765	“Go away yourself!”

766	

767	“I won’t.”

768	

769	“I won’t either.”

770	

771	So they stood, each with a foot placed at an angle as a brace, and both

772	shoving with might and main, and glowering at each other with hate. But

773	neither could get an advantage. After struggling till both were hot and

774	flushed, each relaxed his strain with watchful caution, and Tom said:

775	

776	“You’re a coward and a pup. I’ll tell my big brother on you, and he can

777	thrash you with his little finger, and I’ll make him do it, too.”

778	

779	“What do I care for your big brother? I’ve got a brother that’s bigger

780	than he is—and what’s more, he can throw him over that fence, too.”

781	 [Both brothers were imaginary.]

782	

783	“That’s a lie.”

784	

785	“_Your_ saying so don’t make it so.”

786	

787	Tom drew a line in the dust with his big toe, and said:

788	

789	“I dare you to step over that, and I’ll lick you till you can’t stand

790	up. Anybody that’ll take a dare will steal sheep.”

791	

792	The new boy stepped over promptly, and said:

793	

794	“Now you said you’d do it, now let’s see you do it.”

795	

796	“Don’t you crowd me now; you better look out.”

797	

798	“Well, you _said_ you’d do it—why don’t you do it?”

799	

800	“By jingo! for two cents I _will_ do it.”

801	

802	The new boy took two broad coppers out of his pocket and held them out

803	with derision. Tom struck them to the ground. In an instant both boys

804	were rolling and tumbling in the dirt, gripped together like cats; and

805	for the space of a minute they tugged and tore at each other’s hair and

806	clothes, punched and scratched each other’s nose, and covered themselves

807	with dust and glory. Presently the confusion took form, and through the

808	fog of battle Tom appeared, seated astride the new boy, and pounding him

809	with his fists. “Holler ’nuff!” said he.

810	

811	The boy only struggled to free himself. He was crying—mainly from rage.

812	

813	“Holler ’nuff!”—and the pounding went on.

814	

815	At last the stranger got out a smothered “’Nuff!” and Tom let him up and

816	said:

817	

818	“Now that’ll learn you. Better look out who you’re fooling with next

819	time.”

820	

821	The new boy went off brushing the dust from his clothes, sobbing,

822	snuffling, and occasionally looking back and shaking his head and

823	threatening what he would do to Tom the “next time he caught him out.”

824	 To which Tom responded with jeers, and started off in high feather, and

825	as soon as his back was turned the new boy snatched up a stone, threw it

826	and hit him between the shoulders and then turned tail and ran like

827	an antelope. Tom chased the traitor home, and thus found out where he

828	lived. He then held a position at the gate for some time, daring the

829	enemy to come outside, but the enemy only made faces at him through the

830	window and declined. At last the enemy’s mother appeared, and called Tom

831	a bad, vicious, vulgar child, and ordered him away. So he went away; but

832	he said he “’lowed” to “lay” for that boy.

833	

834	He got home pretty late that night, and when he climbed cautiously in

835	at the window, he uncovered an ambuscade, in the person of his aunt; and

836	when she saw the state his clothes were in her resolution to turn his

837	Saturday holiday into captivity at hard labor became adamantine in its

838	firmness.

839	

840	

841	

842	

843	CHAPTER II

844	

845	

846	SATURDAY morning was come, and all the summer world was bright and

847	fresh, and brimming with life. There was a song in every heart; and if

848	the heart was young the music issued at the lips. There was cheer in

849	every face and a spring in every step. The locust-trees were in bloom

850	and the fragrance of the blossoms filled the air. Cardiff Hill, beyond

851	the village and above it, was green with vegetation and it lay just far

852	enough away to seem a Delectable Land, dreamy, reposeful, and inviting.

853	

854	Tom appeared on the sidewalk with a bucket of whitewash and a

855	long-handled brush. He surveyed the fence, and all gladness left him and

856	a deep melancholy settled down upon his spirit. Thirty yards of board

857	fence nine feet high. Life to him seemed hollow, and existence but a

858	burden. Sighing, he dipped his brush and passed it along the topmost

859	plank; repeated the operation; did it again; compared the insignificant

860	whitewashed streak with the far-reaching continent of unwhitewashed

861	fence, and sat down on a tree-box discouraged. Jim came skipping out at

862	the gate with a tin pail, and singing Buffalo Gals. Bringing water from

863	the town pump had always been hateful work in Tom’s eyes, before, but

864	now it did not strike him so. He remembered that there was company at

865	the pump. White, mulatto, and negro boys and girls were always there

866	waiting their turns, resting, trading playthings, quarrelling, fighting,

867	skylarking. And he remembered that although the pump was only a hundred

868	and fifty yards off, Jim never got back with a bucket of water under an

869	hour—and even then somebody generally had to go after him. Tom said:

870	

871	“Say, Jim, I’ll fetch the water if you’ll whitewash some.”

872	

873	Jim shook his head and said:

874	

875	“Can’t, Mars Tom. Ole missis, she tole me I got to go an’ git dis water

876	an’ not stop foolin’ roun’ wid anybody. She say she spec’ Mars Tom gwine

877	to ax me to whitewash, an’ so she tole me go ’long an’ ’tend to my own

878	business—she ’lowed _she’d_ ’tend to de whitewashin’.”

879	

880	“Oh, never you mind what she said, Jim. That’s the way she always talks.

881	Gimme the bucket—I won’t be gone only a a minute. _She_ won’t ever

882	know.”

883	

884	“Oh, I dasn’t, Mars Tom. Ole missis she’d take an’ tar de head off’n me.

885	’Deed she would.”

886	

887	“_She_! She never licks anybody—whacks ’em over the head with her

888	thimble—and who cares for that, I’d like to know. She talks awful, but

889	talk don’t hurt—anyways it don’t if she don’t cry. Jim, I’ll give you a

890	marvel. I’ll give you a white alley!”

891	

892	Jim began to waver.

893	

894	“White alley, Jim! And it’s a bully taw.”

895	

896	“My! Dat’s a mighty gay marvel, I tell you! But Mars Tom I’s powerful

897	’fraid ole missis—”

898	

899	“And besides, if you will I’ll show you my sore toe.”

900	

901	Jim was only human—this attraction was too much for him. He put down

902	his pail, took the white alley, and bent over the toe with absorbing

903	interest while the bandage was being unwound. In another moment he

904	was flying down the street with his pail and a tingling rear, Tom was

905	whitewashing with vigor, and Aunt Polly was retiring from the field with

906	a slipper in her hand and triumph in her eye.

907	

908	But Tom’s energy did not last. He began to think of the fun he had

909	planned for this day, and his sorrows multiplied. Soon the free boys

910	would come tripping along on all sorts of delicious expeditions, and

911	they would make a world of fun of him for having to work—the very

912	thought of it burnt him like fire. He got out his worldly wealth and

913	examined it—bits of toys, marbles, and trash; enough to buy an exchange

914	of _work_, maybe, but not half enough to buy so much as half an hour

915	of pure freedom. So he returned his straitened means to his pocket, and

916	gave up the idea of trying to buy the boys. At this dark and hopeless

917	moment an inspiration burst upon him! Nothing less than a great,

918	magnificent inspiration.

919	

920	He took up his brush and went tranquilly to work. Ben Rogers hove in

921	sight presently—the very boy, of all boys, whose ridicule he had been

922	dreading. Ben’s gait was the hop-skip-and-jump—proof enough that his

923	heart was light and his anticipations high. He was eating an apple, and

924	giving a long, melodious whoop, at intervals, followed by a deep-toned

925	ding-dong-dong, ding-dong-dong, for he was personating a steamboat. As

926	he drew near, he slackened speed, took the middle of the street, leaned

927	far over to starboard and rounded to ponderously and with laborious pomp

928	and circumstance—for he was personating the Big Missouri, and considered

929	himself to be drawing nine feet of water. He was boat and captain and

930	engine-bells combined, so he had to imagine himself standing on his own

931	hurricane-deck giving the orders and executing them:

932	

933	“Stop her, sir! Ting-a-ling-ling!” The headway ran almost out, and he

934	drew up slowly toward the sidewalk.

935	

936	“Ship up to back! Ting-a-ling-ling!” His arms straightened and stiffened

937	down his sides.

938	

939	“Set her back on the stabboard! Ting-a-ling-ling! Chow! ch-chow-wow!

940	Chow!” His right hand, mean-time, describing stately circles—for it was

941	representing a forty-foot wheel.

942	

943	“Let her go back on the labboard! Ting-a-ling-ling! Chow-ch-chow-chow!”

944	 The left hand began to describe circles.

945	

946	“Stop the stabboard! Ting-a-ling-ling! Stop the labboard! Come ahead on

947	the stabboard! Stop her! Let your outside turn over slow! Ting-a-ling-ling!

948	Chow-ow-ow! Get out that head-line! _lively_ now! Come—out with

949	your spring-line—what’re you about there! Take a turn round that stump

950	with the bight of it! Stand by that stage, now—let her go! Done with

951	the engines, sir! Ting-a-ling-ling! SH’T! S’H’T! SH’T!” (trying the

952	gauge-cocks).

953	

954	Tom went on whitewashing—paid no attention to the steamboat. Ben stared

955	a moment and then said: “_Hi-Yi! You’re_ up a stump, ain’t you!”

956	

957	No answer. Tom surveyed his last touch with the eye of an artist, then

958	he gave his brush another gentle sweep and surveyed the result, as

959	before. Ben ranged up alongside of him. Tom’s mouth watered for the

960	apple, but he stuck to his work. Ben said:

961	

962	“Hello, old chap, you got to work, hey?”

963	

964	Tom wheeled suddenly and said:

965	

966	“Why, it’s you, Ben! I warn’t noticing.”

967	

968	“Say—I’m going in a-swimming, I am. Don’t you wish you could? But of

969	course you’d druther _work_—wouldn’t you? Course you would!”

970	

971	Tom contemplated the boy a bit, and said:

972	

973	“What do you call work?”

974	

975	“Why, ain’t _that_ work?”

976	

977	Tom resumed his whitewashing, and answered carelessly:

978	

979	“Well, maybe it is, and maybe it ain’t. All I know, is, it suits Tom

980	Sawyer.”

981	

982	“Oh come, now, you don’t mean to let on that you _like_ it?”

983	

984	The brush continued to move.

985	

986	“Like it? Well, I don’t see why I oughtn’t to like it. Does a boy get a

987	chance to whitewash a fence every day?”

988	

989	That put the thing in a new light. Ben stopped nibbling his apple.

990	Tom swept his brush daintily back and forth—stepped back to note the

991	effect—added a touch here and there—criticised the effect again—Ben

992	watching every move and getting more and more interested, more and more

993	absorbed. Presently he said:

994	

995	“Say, Tom, let _me_ whitewash a little.”

996	

997	Tom considered, was about to consent; but he altered his mind:

998	

999	“No—no—I reckon it wouldn’t hardly do, Ben. You see, Aunt Polly’s awful

1000	particular about this fence—right here on the street, you know—but if it

1001	was the back fence I wouldn’t mind and _she_ wouldn’t. Yes, she’s awful

1002	particular about this fence; it’s got to be done very careful; I reckon

1003	there ain’t one boy in a thousand, maybe two thousand, that can do it

1004	the way it’s got to be done.”

1005	

1006	“No—is that so? Oh come, now—lemme just try. Only just a little—I’d let

1007	_you_, if you was me, Tom.”

1008	

1009	“Ben, I’d like to, honest injun; but Aunt Polly—well, Jim wanted to do

1010	it, but she wouldn’t let him; Sid wanted to do it, and she wouldn’t let

1011	Sid. Now don’t you see how I’m fixed? If you was to tackle this fence

1012	and anything was to happen to it—”

1013	

1014	“Oh, shucks, I’ll be just as careful. Now lemme try. Say—I’ll give you

1015	the core of my apple.”

1016	

1017	“Well, here—No, Ben, now don’t. I’m afeard—”

1018	

1019	“I’ll give you _all_ of it!”

1020	

1021	Tom gave up the brush with reluctance in his face, but alacrity in his

1022	heart. And while the late steamer Big Missouri worked and sweated in the

1023	sun, the retired artist sat on a barrel in the shade close by,

1024	dangled his legs, munched his apple, and planned the slaughter of more

1025	innocents. There was no lack of material; boys happened along every

1026	little while; they came to jeer, but remained to whitewash. By the time

1027	Ben was fagged out, Tom had traded the next chance to Billy Fisher for

1028	a kite, in good repair; and when he played out, Johnny Miller bought in

1029	for a dead rat and a string to swing it with—and so on, and so on, hour

1030	after hour. And when the middle of the afternoon came, from being a

1031	poor poverty-stricken boy in the morning, Tom was literally rolling in

1032	wealth. He had besides the things before mentioned, twelve marbles, part

1033	of a jews-harp, a piece of blue bottle-glass to look through, a spool

1034	cannon, a key that wouldn’t unlock anything, a fragment of chalk, a

1035	glass stopper of a decanter, a tin soldier, a couple of tadpoles,

1036	six fire-crackers, a kitten with only one eye, a brass door-knob, a

1037	dog-collar—but no dog—the handle of a knife, four pieces of orange-peel,

1038	and a dilapidated old window sash.

1039	

1040	He had had a nice, good, idle time all the while—plenty of company—and

1041	the fence had three coats of whitewash on it! If he hadn’t run out of

1042	whitewash he would have bankrupted every boy in the village.

1043	

1044	Tom said to himself that it was not such a hollow world, after all. He

1045	had discovered a great law of human action, without knowing it—namely,

1046	that in order to make a man or a boy covet a thing, it is only necessary

1047	to make the thing difficult to attain. If he had been a great and

1048	wise philosopher, like the writer of this book, he would now have

1049	comprehended that Work consists of whatever a body is _obliged_ to do,

1050	and that Play consists of whatever a body is not obliged to do. And

1051	this would help him to understand why constructing artificial flowers or

1052	performing on a tread-mill is work, while rolling ten-pins or climbing

1053	Mont Blanc is only amusement. There are wealthy gentlemen in England

1054	who drive four-horse passenger-coaches twenty or thirty miles on a

1055	daily line, in the summer, because the privilege costs them considerable

1056	money; but if they were offered wages for the service, that would turn

1057	it into work and then they would resign.

1058	

1059	The boy mused awhile over the substantial change which had taken place

1060	in his worldly circumstances, and then wended toward headquarters to

1061	report.

1062	

1063	

1064	

1065	

1066	CHAPTER III

1067	

1068	

1069	TOM presented himself before Aunt Polly, who was sitting by an

1070	open window in a pleasant rearward apartment, which was bedroom,

1071	breakfast-room, dining-room, and library, combined. The balmy summer

1072	air, the restful quiet, the odor of the flowers, and the drowsing

1073	murmur of the bees had had their effect, and she was nodding over her

1074	knitting—for she had no company but the cat, and it was asleep in her

1075	lap. Her spectacles were propped up on her gray head for safety. She had

1076	thought that of course Tom had deserted long ago, and she wondered at

1077	seeing him place himself in her power again in this intrepid way. He

1078	said: “Mayn’t I go and play now, aunt?”

1079	

1080	“What, a’ready? How much have you done?”

1081	

1082	“It’s all done, aunt.”

1083	

1084	“Tom, don’t lie to me—I can’t bear it.”

1085	

1086	“I ain’t, aunt; it _is_ all done.”

1087	

1088	Aunt Polly placed small trust in such evidence. She went out to see for

1089	herself; and she would have been content to find twenty per cent. of

1090	Tom’s statement true. When she found the entire fence white-washed, and

1091	not only whitewashed but elaborately coated and recoated, and even a

1092	streak added to the ground, her astonishment was almost unspeakable. She

1093	said:

1094	

1095	“Well, I never! There’s no getting round it, you can work when you’re a

1096	mind to, Tom.” And then she diluted the compliment by adding, “But it’s

1097	powerful seldom you’re a mind to, I’m bound to say. Well, go ’long and

1098	play; but mind you get back some time in a week, or I’ll tan you.”

1099	

1100	She was so overcome by the splendor of his achievement that she took

1101	him into the closet and selected a choice apple and delivered it to him,

1102	along with an improving lecture upon the added value and flavor a treat

1103	took to itself when it came without sin through virtuous effort.

1104	And while she closed with a happy Scriptural flourish, he “hooked” a

1105	doughnut.

1106	

1107	Then he skipped out, and saw Sid just starting up the outside stairway

1108	that led to the back rooms on the second floor. Clods were handy and

1109	the air was full of them in a twinkling. They raged around Sid like a

1110	hail-storm; and before Aunt Polly could collect her surprised faculties

1111	and sally to the rescue, six or seven clods had taken personal effect,

1112	and Tom was over the fence and gone. There was a gate, but as a general

1113	thing he was too crowded for time to make use of it. His soul was at

1114	peace, now that he had settled with Sid for calling attention to his

1115	black thread and getting him into trouble.

1116	

1117	Tom skirted the block, and came round into a muddy alley that led by the

1118	back of his aunt’s cow-stable. He presently got safely beyond the reach

1119	of capture and punishment, and hastened toward the public square of the

1120	village, where two “military” companies of boys had met for conflict,

1121	according to previous appointment. Tom was General of one of these

1122	armies, Joe Harper (a bosom friend) General of the other. These two

1123	great commanders did not condescend to fight in person—that being better

1124	suited to the still smaller fry—but sat together on an eminence

1125	and conducted the field operations by orders delivered through

1126	aides-de-camp. Tom’s army won a great victory, after a long and

1127	hard-fought battle. Then the dead were counted, prisoners exchanged,

1128	the terms of the next disagreement agreed upon, and the day for the

1129	necessary battle appointed; after which the armies fell into line and

1130	marched away, and Tom turned homeward alone.

1131	

1132	As he was passing by the house where Jeff Thatcher lived, he saw a new

1133	girl in the garden—a lovely little blue-eyed creature with yellow

1134	hair plaited into two long-tails, white summer frock and embroidered

1135	pan-talettes. The fresh-crowned hero fell without firing a shot. A

1136	certain Amy Lawrence vanished out of his heart and left not even a

1137	memory of herself behind. He had thought he loved her to distraction;

1138	he had regarded his passion as adoration; and behold it was only a poor

1139	little evanescent partiality. He had been months winning her; she had

1140	confessed hardly a week ago; he had been the happiest and the proudest

1141	boy in the world only seven short days, and here in one instant of time

1142	she had gone out of his heart like a casual stranger whose visit is

1143	done.

1144	

1145	He worshipped this new angel with furtive eye, till he saw that she had

1146	discovered him; then he pretended he did not know she was present, and

1147	began to “show off” in all sorts of absurd boyish ways, in order to win

1148	her admiration. He kept up this grotesque foolishness for some time;

1149	but by-and-by, while he was in the midst of some dangerous gymnastic

1150	performances, he glanced aside and saw that the little girl was wending

1151	her way toward the house. Tom came up to the fence and leaned on it,

1152	grieving, and hoping she would tarry yet awhile longer. She halted a

1153	moment on the steps and then moved toward the door. Tom heaved a great

1154	sigh as she put her foot on the threshold. But his face lit up,

1155	right away, for she tossed a pansy over the fence a moment before she

1156	disappeared.

1157	

1158	The boy ran around and stopped within a foot or two of the flower, and

1159	then shaded his eyes with his hand and began to look down street as

1160	if he had discovered something of interest going on in that direction.

1161	Presently he picked up a straw and began trying to balance it on his

1162	nose, with his head tilted far back; and as he moved from side to side,

1163	in his efforts, he edged nearer and nearer toward the pansy; finally his

1164	bare foot rested upon it, his pliant toes closed upon it, and he hopped

1165	away with the treasure and disappeared round the corner. But only for a

1166	minute—only while he could button the flower inside his jacket, next

1167	his heart—or next his stomach, possibly, for he was not much posted in

1168	anatomy, and not hypercritical, anyway.

1169	

1170	He returned, now, and hung about the fence till nightfall, “showing

1171	off,” as before; but the girl never exhibited herself again, though Tom

1172	comforted himself a little with the hope that she had been near some

1173	window, meantime, and been aware of his attentions. Finally he strode

1174	home reluctantly, with his poor head full of visions.

1175	

1176	All through supper his spirits were so high that his aunt wondered “what

1177	had got into the child.” He took a good scolding about clodding Sid, and

1178	did not seem to mind it in the least. He tried to steal sugar under his

1179	aunt’s very nose, and got his knuckles rapped for it. He said:

1180	

1181	“Aunt, you don’t whack Sid when he takes it.”

1182	

1183	“Well, Sid don’t torment a body the way you do. You’d be always into

1184	that sugar if I warn’t watching you.”

1185	

1186	Presently she stepped into the kitchen, and Sid, happy in his immunity,

1187	reached for the sugar-bowl—a sort of glorying over Tom which was

1188	wellnigh unbearable. But Sid’s fingers slipped and the bowl dropped and

1189	broke. Tom was in ecstasies. In such ecstasies that he even controlled

1190	his tongue and was silent. He said to himself that he would not speak a

1191	word, even when his aunt came in, but would sit perfectly still till she

1192	asked who did the mischief; and then he would tell, and there would be

1193	nothing so good in the world as to see that pet model “catch it.” He was

1194	so brimful of exultation that he could hardly hold himself when the old

1195	lady came back and stood above the wreck discharging lightnings of wrath

1196	from over her spectacles. He said to himself, “Now it’s coming!” And the

1197	next instant he was sprawling on the floor! The potent palm was uplifted

1198	to strike again when Tom cried out:

1199	

1200	“Hold on, now, what ’er you belting _me_ for?—Sid broke it!”

1201	

1202	Aunt Polly paused, perplexed, and Tom looked for healing pity. But when

1203	she got her tongue again, she only said:

1204	

1205	“Umf! Well, you didn’t get a lick amiss, I reckon. You been into some

1206	other audacious mischief when I wasn’t around, like enough.”

1207	

1208	Then her conscience reproached her, and she yearned to say something

1209	kind and loving; but she judged that this would be construed into a

1210	confession that she had been in the wrong, and discipline forbade that.

1211	So she kept silence, and went about her affairs with a troubled heart.

1212	Tom sulked in a corner and exalted his woes. He knew that in her heart

1213	his aunt was on her knees to him, and he was morosely gratified by the

1214	consciousness of it. He would hang out no signals, he would take notice

1215	of none. He knew that a yearning glance fell upon him, now and then,

1216	through a film of tears, but he refused recognition of it. He pictured

1217	himself lying sick unto death and his aunt bending over him beseeching

1218	one little forgiving word, but he would turn his face to the wall, and

1219	die with that word unsaid. Ah, how would she feel then? And he pictured

1220	himself brought home from the river, dead, with his curls all wet, and

1221	his sore heart at rest. How she would throw herself upon him, and how

1222	her tears would fall like rain, and her lips pray God to give her back

1223	her boy and she would never, never abuse him any more! But he would

1224	lie there cold and white and make no sign—a poor little sufferer, whose

1225	griefs were at an end. He so worked upon his feelings with the pathos of

1226	these dreams, that he had to keep swallowing, he was so like to choke;

1227	and his eyes swam in a blur of water, which overflowed when he winked,

1228	and ran down and trickled from the end of his nose. And such a luxury to

1229	him was this petting of his sorrows, that he could not bear to have any

1230	worldly cheeriness or any grating delight intrude upon it; it was too

1231	sacred for such contact; and so, presently, when his cousin Mary danced

1232	in, all alive with the joy of seeing home again after an age-long visit

1233	of one week to the country, he got up and moved in clouds and darkness

1234	out at one door as she brought song and sunshine in at the other.

1235	

1236	He wandered far from the accustomed haunts of boys, and sought desolate

1237	places that were in harmony with his spirit. A log raft in the river

1238	invited him, and he seated himself on its outer edge and contemplated

1239	the dreary vastness of the stream, wishing, the while, that he could

1240	only be drowned, all at once and unconsciously, without undergoing the

1241	uncomfortable routine devised by nature. Then he thought of his flower.

1242	He got it out, rumpled and wilted, and it mightily increased his dismal

1243	felicity. He wondered if she would pity him if she knew? Would she

1244	cry, and wish that she had a right to put her arms around his neck and

1245	comfort him? Or would she turn coldly away like all the hollow world?

1246	This picture brought such an agony of pleasurable suffering that he

1247	worked it over and over again in his mind and set it up in new and

1248	varied lights, till he wore it threadbare. At last he rose up sighing

1249	and departed in the darkness.

1250	

1251	About half-past nine or ten o’clock he came along the deserted street to

1252	where the Adored Unknown lived; he paused a moment; no sound fell upon

1253	his listening ear; a candle was casting a dull glow upon the curtain

1254	of a second-story window. Was the sacred presence there? He climbed the

1255	fence, threaded his stealthy way through the plants, till he stood under

1256	that window; he looked up at it long, and with emotion; then he laid him

1257	down on the ground under it, disposing himself upon his back, with his

1258	hands clasped upon his breast and holding his poor wilted flower.

1259	And thus he would die—out in the cold world, with no shelter over his

1260	homeless head, no friendly hand to wipe the death-damps from his brow,

1261	no loving face to bend pityingly over him when the great agony came. And

1262	thus _she_ would see him when she looked out upon the glad morning, and

1263	oh! would she drop one little tear upon his poor, lifeless form, would

1264	she heave one little sigh to see a bright young life so rudely blighted,

1265	so untimely cut down?

1266	

1267	The window went up, a maid-servant’s discordant voice profaned the holy

1268	calm, and a deluge of water drenched the prone martyr’s remains!

1269	

1270	The strangling hero sprang up with a relieving snort. There was a whiz

1271	as of a missile in the air, mingled with the murmur of a curse, a sound

1272	as of shivering glass followed, and a small, vague form went over the

1273	fence and shot away in the gloom.

1274	

1275	Not long after, as Tom, all undressed for bed, was surveying his

1276	drenched garments by the light of a tallow dip, Sid woke up; but if he

1277	had any dim idea of making any “references to allusions,” he thought

1278	better of it and held his peace, for there was danger in Tom’s eye.

1279	

1280	Tom turned in without the added vexation of prayers, and Sid made mental

1281	note of the omission.

1282	

1283	

1284	

1285	

1286	CHAPTER IV

1287	

1288	

1289	THE sun rose upon a tranquil world, and beamed down upon the peaceful

1290	village like a benediction. Breakfast over, Aunt Polly had family

1291	worship: it began with a prayer built from the ground up of solid

1292	courses of Scriptural quotations, welded together with a thin mortar of

1293	originality; and from the summit of this she delivered a grim chapter of

1294	the Mosaic Law, as from Sinai.

1295	

1296	Then Tom girded up his loins, so to speak, and went to work to “get

1297	his verses.” Sid had learned his lesson days before. Tom bent all his

1298	energies to the memorizing of five verses, and he chose part of the

1299	Sermon on the Mount, because he could find no verses that were shorter.

1300	At the end of half an hour Tom had a vague general idea of his lesson,

1301	but no more, for his mind was traversing the whole field of human

1302	thought, and his hands were busy with distracting recreations. Mary took

1303	his book to hear him recite, and he tried to find his way through the

1304	fog:

1305	

1306	“Blessed are the—a—a—”

1307	

1308	“Poor”—

1309	

1310	“Yes—poor; blessed are the poor—a—a—”

1311	

1312	“In spirit—”

1313	

1314	“In spirit; blessed are the poor in spirit, for they—they—”

1315	

1316	“_Theirs_—”

1317	

1318	“For _theirs_. Blessed are the poor in spirit, for theirs is the kingdom

1319	of heaven. Blessed are they that mourn, for they—they—”

1320	

1321	“Sh—”

1322	

1323	“For they—a—”

1324	

1325	“S, H, A—”

1326	

1327	“For they S, H—Oh, I don’t know what it is!”

1328	

1329	“_Shall_!”

1330	

1331	“Oh, _shall_! for they shall—for they shall—a—a—shall

1332	mourn—a—a—blessed are they that shall—they that—a—they that

1333	shall mourn, for they shall—a—shall _what_? Why don’t you tell me,

1334	Mary?—what do you want to be so mean for?”

1335	

1336	“Oh, Tom, you poor thick-headed thing, I’m not teasing you. I wouldn’t

1337	do that. You must go and learn it again. Don’t you be discouraged, Tom,

1338	you’ll manage it—and if you do, I’ll give you something ever so nice.

1339	There, now, that’s a good boy.”

1340	

1341	“All right! What is it, Mary, tell me what it is.”

1342	

1343	“Never you mind, Tom. You know if I say it’s nice, it is nice.”

1344	

1345	“You bet you that’s so, Mary. All right, I’ll tackle it again.”

1346	

1347	And he did “tackle it again”—and under the double pressure of curiosity

1348	and prospective gain he did it with such spirit that he accomplished a

1349	shining success. Mary gave him a brand-new “Barlow” knife worth twelve

1350	and a half cents; and the convulsion of delight that swept his system

1351	shook him to his foundations. True, the knife would not cut anything,

1352	but it was a “sure-enough” Barlow, and there was inconceivable grandeur

1353	in that—though where the Western boys ever got the idea that such a

1354	weapon could possibly be counterfeited to its injury is an imposing

1355	mystery and will always remain so, perhaps. Tom contrived to scarify the

1356	cupboard with it, and was arranging to begin on the bureau, when he was

1357	called off to dress for Sunday-school.

1358	

1359	Mary gave him a tin basin of water and a piece of soap, and he went

1360	outside the door and set the basin on a little bench there; then he

1361	dipped the soap in the water and laid it down; turned up his sleeves;

1362	poured out the water on the ground, gently, and then entered the kitchen

1363	and began to wipe his face diligently on the towel behind the door. But

1364	Mary removed the towel and said:

1365	

1366	“Now ain’t you ashamed, Tom. You mustn’t be so bad. Water won’t hurt

1367	you.”

1368	

1369	Tom was a trifle disconcerted. The basin was refilled, and this time he

1370	stood over it a little while, gathering resolution; took in a big breath

1371	and began. When he entered the kitchen presently, with both eyes shut

1372	and groping for the towel with his hands, an honorable testimony of

1373	suds and water was dripping from his face. But when he emerged from

1374	the towel, he was not yet satisfactory, for the clean territory stopped

1375	short at his chin and his jaws, like a mask; below and beyond this line

1376	there was a dark expanse of unirrigated soil that spread downward in

1377	front and backward around his neck. Mary took him in hand, and when she

1378	was done with him he was a man and a brother, without distinction of

1379	color, and his saturated hair was neatly brushed, and its short curls

1380	wrought into a dainty and symmetrical general effect. [He privately

1381	smoothed out the curls, with labor and difficulty, and plastered his

1382	hair close down to his head; for he held curls to be effeminate, and his

1383	own filled his life with bitterness.] Then Mary got out a suit of his

1384	clothing that had been used only on Sundays during two years—they were

1385	simply called his “other clothes”—and so by that we know the size of his

1386	wardrobe. The girl “put him to rights” after he had dressed himself;

1387	she buttoned his neat roundabout up to his chin, turned his vast shirt

1388	collar down over his shoulders, brushed him off and crowned him with

1389	his speckled straw hat. He now looked exceedingly improved and

1390	uncomfortable. He was fully as uncomfortable as he looked; for there

1391	was a restraint about whole clothes and cleanliness that galled him. He

1392	hoped that Mary would forget his shoes, but the hope was blighted; she

1393	coated them thoroughly with tallow, as was the custom, and brought

1394	them out. He lost his temper and said he was always being made to do

1395	everything he didn’t want to do. But Mary said, persuasively:

1396	

1397	“Please, Tom—that’s a good boy.”

1398	

1399	So he got into the shoes snarling. Mary was soon ready, and the three

1400	children set out for Sunday-school—a place that Tom hated with his whole

1401	heart; but Sid and Mary were fond of it.

1402	

1403	Sabbath-school hours were from nine to half-past ten; and then church

1404	service. Two of the children always remained for the sermon voluntarily,

1405	and the other always remained too—for stronger reasons. The church’s

1406	high-backed, uncushioned pews would seat about three hundred persons;

1407	the edifice was but a small, plain affair, with a sort of pine board

1408	tree-box on top of it for a steeple. At the door Tom dropped back a step

1409	and accosted a Sunday-dressed comrade:

1410	

1411	“Say, Billy, got a yaller ticket?”

1412	

1413	“Yes.”

1414	

1415	“What’ll you take for her?”

1416	

1417	“What’ll you give?”

1418	

1419	“Piece of lickrish and a fish-hook.”

1420	

1421	“Less see ’em.”

1422	

1423	Tom exhibited. They were satisfactory, and the property changed hands.

1424	Then Tom traded a couple of white alleys for three red tickets, and some

1425	small trifle or other for a couple of blue ones. He waylaid other

1426	boys as they came, and went on buying tickets of various colors ten

1427	or fifteen minutes longer. He entered the church, now, with a swarm

1428	of clean and noisy boys and girls, proceeded to his seat and started

1429	a quarrel with the first boy that came handy. The teacher, a grave,

1430	elderly man, interfered; then turned his back a moment and Tom pulled a

1431	boy’s hair in the next bench, and was absorbed in his book when the boy

1432	turned around; stuck a pin in another boy, presently, in order to hear

1433	him say “Ouch!” and got a new reprimand from his teacher. Tom’s whole

1434	class were of a pattern—restless, noisy, and troublesome. When they came

1435	to recite their lessons, not one of them knew his verses perfectly, but

1436	had to be prompted all along. However, they worried through, and each

1437	got his reward—in small blue tickets, each with a passage of Scripture

1438	on it; each blue ticket was pay for two verses of the recitation. Ten

1439	blue tickets equalled a red one, and could be exchanged for it; ten red

1440	tickets equalled a yellow one; for ten yellow tickets the superintendent

1441	gave a very plainly bound Bible (worth forty cents in those easy

1442	times) to the pupil. How many of my readers would have the industry and

1443	application to memorize two thousand verses, even for a Dore Bible? And

1444	yet Mary had acquired two Bibles in this way—it was the patient work of

1445	two years—and a boy of German parentage had won four or five. He once

1446	recited three thousand verses without stopping; but the strain upon his

1447	mental faculties was too great, and he was little better than an idiot

1448	from that day forth—a grievous misfortune for the school, for on great

1449	occasions, before company, the superintendent (as Tom expressed it)

1450	had always made this boy come out and “spread himself.” Only the older

1451	pupils managed to keep their tickets and stick to their tedious work

1452	long enough to get a Bible, and so the delivery of one of these prizes

1453	was a rare and noteworthy circumstance; the successful pupil was so

1454	great and conspicuous for that day that on the spot every scholar’s

1455	heart was fired with a fresh ambition that often lasted a couple

1456	of weeks. It is possible that Tom’s mental stomach had never really

1457	hungered for one of those prizes, but unquestionably his entire being

1458	had for many a day longed for the glory and the eclat that came with it.

1459	

1460	In due course the superintendent stood up in front of the pulpit, with

1461	a closed hymn-book in his hand and his forefinger inserted between its

1462	leaves, and commanded attention. When a Sunday-school superintendent

1463	makes his customary little speech, a hymn-book in the hand is as

1464	necessary as is the inevitable sheet of music in the hand of a singer

1465	who stands forward on the platform and sings a solo at a concert—though

1466	why, is a mystery: for neither the hymn-book nor the sheet of music

1467	is ever referred to by the sufferer. This superintendent was a slim

1468	creature of thirty-five, with a sandy goatee and short sandy hair; he

1469	wore a stiff standing-collar whose upper edge almost reached his ears

1470	and whose sharp points curved forward abreast the corners of his mouth—a

1471	fence that compelled a straight lookout ahead, and a turning of the

1472	whole body when a side view was required; his chin was propped on a

1473	spreading cravat which was as broad and as long as a bank-note, and had

1474	fringed ends; his boot toes were turned sharply up, in the fashion

1475	of the day, like sleigh-runners—an effect patiently and laboriously

1476	produced by the young men by sitting with their toes pressed against a

1477	wall for hours together. Mr. Walters was very earnest of mien, and very

1478	sincere and honest at heart; and he held sacred things and places

1479	in such reverence, and so separated them from worldly matters, that

1480	unconsciously to himself his Sunday-school voice had acquired a peculiar

1481	intonation which was wholly absent on week-days. He began after this

1482	fashion:

1483	

1484	“Now, children, I want you all to sit up just as straight and pretty as

1485	you can and give me all your attention for a minute or two. There—that

1486	is it. That is the way good little boys and girls should do. I see one

1487	little girl who is looking out of the window—I am afraid she thinks I

1488	am out there somewhere—perhaps up in one of the trees making a speech

1489	to the little birds. [Applausive titter.] I want to tell you how good it

1490	makes me feel to see so many bright, clean little faces assembled in a

1491	place like this, learning to do right and be good.” And so forth and so

1492	on. It is not necessary to set down the rest of the oration. It was of a

1493	pattern which does not vary, and so it is familiar to us all.

1494	

1495	The latter third of the speech was marred by the resumption of fights

1496	and other recreations among certain of the bad boys, and by fidgetings

1497	and whisperings that extended far and wide, washing even to the bases of

1498	isolated and incorruptible rocks like Sid and Mary. But now every sound

1499	ceased suddenly, with the subsidence of Mr. Walters’ voice, and the

1500	conclusion of the speech was received with a burst of silent gratitude.

1501	

1502	A good part of the whispering had been occasioned by an event which was

1503	more or less rare—the entrance of visitors: lawyer Thatcher, accompanied

1504	by a very feeble and aged man; a fine, portly, middle-aged gentleman

1505	with iron-gray hair; and a dignified lady who was doubtless the latter’s

1506	wife. The lady was leading a child. Tom had been restless and full of

1507	chafings and repinings; conscience-smitten, too—he could not meet Amy

1508	Lawrence’s eye, he could not brook her loving gaze. But when he saw this

1509	small newcomer his soul was all ablaze with bliss in a moment. The next

1510	moment he was “showing off” with all his might—cuffing boys, pulling

1511	hair, making faces—in a word, using every art that seemed likely to

1512	fascinate a girl and win her applause. His exaltation had but one

1513	alloy—the memory of his humiliation in this angel’s garden—and that

1514	record in sand was fast washing out, under the waves of happiness that

1515	were sweeping over it now.

1516	

1517	The visitors were given the highest seat of honor, and as soon as Mr.

1518	Walters’ speech was finished, he introduced them to the school. The

1519	middle-aged man turned out to be a prodigious personage—no less a one

1520	than the county judge—altogether the most august creation these children

1521	had ever looked upon—and they wondered what kind of material he was made

1522	of—and they half wanted to hear him roar, and were half afraid he might,

1523	too. He was from Constantinople, twelve miles away—so he had travelled,

1524	and seen the world—these very eyes had looked upon the county

1525	court-house—which was said to have a tin roof. The awe which these

1526	reflections inspired was attested by the impressive silence and the

1527	ranks of staring eyes. This was the great Judge Thatcher, brother of

1528	their own lawyer. Jeff Thatcher immediately went forward, to be familiar

1529	with the great man and be envied by the school. It would have been music

1530	to his soul to hear the whisperings:

1531	

1532	“Look at him, Jim! He’s a going up there. Say—look! he’s a going to

1533	shake hands with him—he _is_ shaking hands with him! By jings, don’t you

1534	wish you was Jeff?”

1535	

1536	Mr. Walters fell to “showing off,” with all sorts of official bustlings

1537	and activities, giving orders, delivering judgments, discharging

1538	directions here, there, everywhere that he could find a target. The

1539	librarian “showed off”—running hither and thither with his arms full of

1540	books and making a deal of the splutter and fuss that insect authority

1541	delights in. The young lady teachers “showed off”—bending sweetly over

1542	pupils that were lately being boxed, lifting pretty warning fingers

1543	at bad little boys and patting good ones lovingly. The young gentlemen

1544	teachers “showed off” with small scoldings and other little displays of

1545	authority and fine attention to discipline—and most of the teachers, of

1546	both sexes, found business up at the library, by the pulpit; and it was

1547	business that frequently had to be done over again two or three times

1548	(with much seeming vexation). The little girls “showed off” in various

1549	ways, and the little boys “showed off” with such diligence that the air

1550	was thick with paper wads and the murmur of scufflings. And above it

1551	all the great man sat and beamed a majestic judicial smile upon all

1552	the house, and warmed himself in the sun of his own grandeur—for he was

1553	“showing off,” too.

1554	

1555	There was only one thing wanting to make Mr. Walters’ ecstasy complete,

1556	and that was a chance to deliver a Bible-prize and exhibit a prodigy.

1557	Several pupils had a few yellow tickets, but none had enough—he had been

1558	around among the star pupils inquiring. He would have given worlds, now,

1559	to have that German lad back again with a sound mind.

1560	

1561	And now at this moment, when hope was dead, Tom Sawyer came forward with

1562	nine yellow tickets, nine red tickets, and ten blue ones, and demanded

1563	a Bible. This was a thunderbolt out of a clear sky. Walters was not

1564	expecting an application from this source for the next ten years. But

1565	there was no getting around it—here were the certified checks, and they

1566	were good for their face. Tom was therefore elevated to a place with

1567	the Judge and the other elect, and the great news was announced from

1568	headquarters. It was the most stunning surprise of the decade, and

1569	so profound was the sensation that it lifted the new hero up to the

1570	judicial one’s altitude, and the school had two marvels to gaze upon

1571	in place of one. The boys were all eaten up with envy—but those that

1572	suffered the bitterest pangs were those who perceived too late that they

1573	themselves had contributed to this hated splendor by trading tickets to

1574	Tom for the wealth he had amassed in selling whitewashing privileges.

1575	These despised themselves, as being the dupes of a wily fraud, a

1576	guileful snake in the grass.

1577	

1578	The prize was delivered to Tom with as much effusion as the

1579	superintendent could pump up under the circumstances; but it lacked

1580	somewhat of the true gush, for the poor fellow’s instinct taught him

1581	that there was a mystery here that could not well bear the light,

1582	perhaps; it was simply preposterous that this boy had warehoused two

1583	thousand sheaves of Scriptural wisdom on his premises—a dozen would

1584	strain his capacity, without a doubt.

1585	

1586	Amy Lawrence was proud and glad, and she tried to make Tom see it in

1587	her face—but he wouldn’t look. She wondered; then she was just a grain

1588	troubled; next a dim suspicion came and went—came again; she watched;

1589	a furtive glance told her worlds—and then her heart broke, and she was

1590	jealous, and angry, and the tears came and she hated everybody. Tom most

1591	of all (she thought).

1592	

1593	Tom was introduced to the Judge; but his tongue was tied, his breath

1594	would hardly come, his heart quaked—partly because of the awful

1595	greatness of the man, but mainly because he was her parent. He would

1596	have liked to fall down and worship him, if it were in the dark. The

1597	Judge put his hand on Tom’s head and called him a fine little man, and

1598	asked him what his name was. The boy stammered, gasped, and got it out:

1599	

1600	“Tom.”

1601	

1602	“Oh, no, not Tom—it is—”

1603	

1604	“Thomas.”

1605	

1606	“Ah, that’s it. I thought there was more to it, maybe. That’s very well.

1607	But you’ve another one I daresay, and you’ll tell it to me, won’t you?”

1608	

1609	“Tell the gentleman your other name, Thomas,” said Walters, “and say

1610	sir. You mustn’t forget your manners.”

1611	

1612	“Thomas Sawyer—sir.”

1613	

1614	“That’s it! That’s a good boy. Fine boy. Fine, manly little fellow. Two

1615	thousand verses is a great many—very, very great many. And you never can

1616	be sorry for the trouble you took to learn them; for knowledge is worth

1617	more than anything there is in the world; it’s what makes great men

1618	and good men; you’ll be a great man and a good man yourself, some

1619	day, Thomas, and then you’ll look back and say, It’s all owing to the

1620	precious Sunday-school privileges of my boyhood—it’s all owing to

1621	my dear teachers that taught me to learn—it’s all owing to the good

1622	superintendent, who encouraged me, and watched over me, and gave me a

1623	beautiful Bible—a splendid elegant Bible—to keep and have it all for my

1624	own, always—it’s all owing to right bringing up! That is what you will

1625	say, Thomas—and you wouldn’t take any money for those two thousand

1626	verses—no indeed you wouldn’t. And now you wouldn’t mind telling me and

1627	this lady some of the things you’ve learned—no, I know you wouldn’t—for

1628	we are proud of little boys that learn. Now, no doubt you know the names

1629	of all the twelve disciples. Won’t you tell us the names of the first

1630	two that were appointed?”

1631	

1632	Tom was tugging at a button-hole and looking sheepish. He blushed,

1633	now, and his eyes fell. Mr. Walters’ heart sank within him. He said

1634	to himself, it is not possible that the boy can answer the simplest

1635	question—why _did_ the Judge ask him? Yet he felt obliged to speak up

1636	and say:

1637	

1638	“Answer the gentleman, Thomas—don’t be afraid.”

1639	

1640	Tom still hung fire.

1641	

1642	“Now I know you’ll tell me,” said the lady. “The names of the first two

1643	disciples were—”

1644	

1645	“_David And Goliah!_”

1646	

1647	Let us draw the curtain of charity over the rest of the scene.

1648	

1649	

1650	

1651	

1652	CHAPTER V

1653	

1654	

1655	ABOUT half-past ten the cracked bell of the small church began to ring,

1656	and presently the people began to gather for the morning sermon. The

1657	Sunday-school children distributed themselves about the house and

1658	occupied pews with their parents, so as to be under supervision. Aunt

1659	Polly came, and Tom and Sid and Mary sat with her—Tom being placed next

1660	the aisle, in order that he might be as far away from the open window

1661	and the seductive outside summer scenes as possible. The crowd filed up

1662	the aisles: the aged and needy postmaster, who had seen better days;

1663	the mayor and his wife—for they had a mayor there, among other

1664	unnecessaries; the justice of the peace; the widow Douglas, fair,

1665	smart, and forty, a generous, good-hearted soul and well-to-do, her hill

1666	mansion the only palace in the town, and the most hospitable and much

1667	the most lavish in the matter of festivities that St. Petersburg could

1668	boast; the bent and venerable Major and Mrs. Ward; lawyer Riverson, the

1669	new notable from a distance; next the belle of the village, followed by

1670	a troop of lawn-clad and ribbon-decked young heart-breakers; then all

1671	the young clerks in town in a body—for they had stood in the vestibule

1672	sucking their cane-heads, a circling wall of oiled and simpering

1673	admirers, till the last girl had run their gantlet; and last of all came

1674	the Model Boy, Willie Mufferson, taking as heedful care of his mother as

1675	if she were cut glass. He always brought his mother to church, and was

1676	the pride of all the matrons. The boys all hated him, he was so

1677	good. And besides, he had been “thrown up to them” so much. His

1678	white handkerchief was hanging out of his pocket behind, as usual on

1679	Sundays—accidentally. Tom had no handkerchief, and he looked upon boys

1680	who had as snobs.

1681	

1682	The congregation being fully assembled, now, the bell rang once more,

1683	to warn laggards and stragglers, and then a solemn hush fell upon the

1684	church which was only broken by the tittering and whispering of the

1685	choir in the gallery. The choir always tittered and whispered all

1686	through service. There was once a church choir that was not ill-bred,

1687	but I have forgotten where it was, now. It was a great many years ago,

1688	and I can scarcely remember anything about it, but I think it was in

1689	some foreign country.

1690	

1691	The minister gave out the hymn, and read it through with a relish, in a

1692	peculiar style which was much admired in that part of the country. His

1693	voice began on a medium key and climbed steadily up till it reached a

1694	certain point, where it bore with strong emphasis upon the topmost word

1695	and then plunged down as if from a spring-board:

1696	

1697	Shall I be car-ri-ed toe the skies, on flow’ry _beds_ of ease,

1698	

1699	Whilst others fight to win the prize, and sail thro’ _blood_-y seas?

1700	

1701	He was regarded as a wonderful reader. At church “sociables” he was

1702	always called upon to read poetry; and when he was through, the ladies

1703	would lift up their hands and let them fall helplessly in their laps,

1704	and “wall” their eyes, and shake their heads, as much as to say, “Words

1705	cannot express it; it is too beautiful, TOO beautiful for this mortal

1706	earth.”

1707	

1708	After the hymn had been sung, the Rev. Mr. Sprague turned himself into

1709	a bulletin-board, and read off “notices” of meetings and societies and

1710	things till it seemed that the list would stretch out to the crack of

1711	doom—a queer custom which is still kept up in America, even in cities,

1712	away here in this age of abundant newspapers. Often, the less there is

1713	to justify a traditional custom, the harder it is to get rid of it.

1714	

1715	And now the minister prayed. A good, generous prayer it was, and went

1716	into details: it pleaded for the church, and the little children of the

1717	church; for the other churches of the village; for the village itself;

1718	for the county; for the State; for the State officers; for the United

1719	States; for the churches of the United States; for Congress; for the

1720	President; for the officers of the Government; for poor sailors, tossed

1721	by stormy seas; for the oppressed millions groaning under the heel of

1722	European monarchies and Oriental despotisms; for such as have the light

1723	and the good tidings, and yet have not eyes to see nor ears to hear

1724	withal; for the heathen in the far islands of the sea; and closed with

1725	a supplication that the words he was about to speak might find grace

1726	and favor, and be as seed sown in fertile ground, yielding in time a

1727	grateful harvest of good. Amen.

1728	

1729	There was a rustling of dresses, and the standing congregation sat down.

1730	The boy whose history this book relates did not enjoy the prayer, he

1731	only endured it—if he even did that much. He was restive all through it;

1732	he kept tally of the details of the prayer, unconsciously—for he was not

1733	listening, but he knew the ground of old, and the clergyman’s regular

1734	route over it—and when a little trifle of new matter was interlarded,

1735	his ear detected it and his whole nature resented it; he considered

1736	additions unfair, and scoundrelly. In the midst of the prayer a fly had

1737	lit on the back of the pew in front of him and tortured his spirit by

1738	calmly rubbing its hands together, embracing its head with its arms, and

1739	polishing it so vigorously that it seemed to almost part company with

1740	the body, and the slender thread of a neck was exposed to view; scraping

1741	its wings with its hind legs and smoothing them to its body as if they

1742	had been coat-tails; going through its whole toilet as tranquilly as if

1743	it knew it was perfectly safe. As indeed it was; for as sorely as Tom’s

1744	hands itched to grab for it they did not dare—he believed his soul would

1745	be instantly destroyed if he did such a thing while the prayer was going

1746	on. But with the closing sentence his hand began to curve and steal

1747	forward; and the instant the “Amen” was out the fly was a prisoner of

1748	war. His aunt detected the act and made him let it go.

1749	

1750	The minister gave out his text and droned along monotonously through an

1751	argument that was so prosy that many a head by and by began to nod—and

1752	yet it was an argument that dealt in limitless fire and brimstone and

1753	thinned the predestined elect down to a company so small as to be hardly

1754	worth the saving. Tom counted the pages of the sermon; after church he

1755	always knew how many pages there had been, but he seldom knew anything

1756	else about the discourse. However, this time he was really interested

1757	for a little while. The minister made a grand and moving picture of the

1758	assembling together of the world’s hosts at the millennium when the lion

1759	and the lamb should lie down together and a little child should lead

1760	them. But the pathos, the lesson, the moral of the great spectacle

1761	were lost upon the boy; he only thought of the conspicuousness of the

1762	principal character before the on-looking nations; his face lit with the

1763	thought, and he said to himself that he wished he could be that child,

1764	if it was a tame lion.

1765	

1766	Now he lapsed into suffering again, as the dry argument was resumed.

1767	Presently he bethought him of a treasure he had and got it out. It was

1768	a large black beetle with formidable jaws—a “pinchbug,” he called it. It

1769	was in a percussion-cap box. The first thing the beetle did was to

1770	take him by the finger. A natural fillip followed, the beetle went

1771	floundering into the aisle and lit on its back, and the hurt finger went

1772	into the boy’s mouth. The beetle lay there working its helpless legs,

1773	unable to turn over. Tom eyed it, and longed for it; but it was safe out

1774	of his reach. Other people uninterested in the sermon found relief in

1775	the beetle, and they eyed it too. Presently a vagrant poodle dog came

1776	idling along, sad at heart, lazy with the summer softness and the

1777	quiet, weary of captivity, sighing for change. He spied the beetle; the

1778	drooping tail lifted and wagged. He surveyed the prize; walked around

1779	it; smelt at it from a safe distance; walked around it again; grew

1780	bolder, and took a closer smell; then lifted his lip and made a gingerly

1781	snatch at it, just missing it; made another, and another; began to enjoy

1782	the diversion; subsided to his stomach with the beetle between his paws,

1783	and continued his experiments; grew weary at last, and then indifferent

1784	and absent-minded. His head nodded, and little by little his chin

1785	descended and touched the enemy, who seized it. There was a sharp yelp,

1786	a flirt of the poodle’s head, and the beetle fell a couple of yards

1787	away, and lit on its back once more. The neighboring spectators

1788	shook with a gentle inward joy, several faces went behind fans and

1789	hand-kerchiefs, and Tom was entirely happy. The dog looked foolish,

1790	and probably felt so; but there was resentment in his heart, too, and a

1791	craving for revenge. So he went to the beetle and began a wary attack on

1792	it again; jumping at it from every point of a circle, lighting with his

1793	fore-paws within an inch of the creature, making even closer snatches at

1794	it with his teeth, and jerking his head till his ears flapped again. But

1795	he grew tired once more, after a while; tried to amuse himself with a

1796	fly but found no relief; followed an ant around, with his nose close

1797	to the floor, and quickly wearied of that; yawned, sighed, forgot the

1798	beetle entirely, and sat down on it. Then there was a wild yelp of agony

1799	and the poodle went sailing up the aisle; the yelps continued, and so

1800	did the dog; he crossed the house in front of the altar; he flew

1801	down the other aisle; he crossed before the doors; he clamored up the

1802	home-stretch; his anguish grew with his progress, till presently he was

1803	but a woolly comet moving in its orbit with the gleam and the speed of

1804	light. At last the frantic sufferer sheered from its course, and sprang

1805	into its master’s lap; he flung it out of the window, and the voice of

1806	distress quickly thinned away and died in the distance.

1807	

1808	By this time the whole church was red-faced and suffocating with

1809	suppressed laughter, and the sermon had come to a dead standstill.

1810	The discourse was resumed presently, but it went lame and halting, all

1811	possibility of impressiveness being at an end; for even the gravest

1812	sentiments were constantly being received with a smothered burst of

1813	unholy mirth, under cover of some remote pew-back, as if the poor parson

1814	had said a rarely facetious thing. It was a genuine relief to the whole

1815	congregation when the ordeal was over and the benediction pronounced.

1816	

1817	Tom Sawyer went home quite cheerful, thinking to himself that there was

1818	some satisfaction about divine service when there was a bit of variety

1819	in it. He had but one marring thought; he was willing that the dog

1820	should play with his pinchbug, but he did not think it was upright in

1821	him to carry it off.

1822	

1823	

1824	

1825	

1826	CHAPTER VI

1827	

1828	

1829	MONDAY morning found Tom Sawyer miserable. Monday morning always found

1830	him so—because it began another week’s slow suffering in school. He

1831	generally began that day with wishing he had had no intervening holiday,

1832	it made the going into captivity and fetters again so much more odious.

1833	

1834	Tom lay thinking. Presently it occurred to him that he wished he was

1835	sick; then he could stay home from school. Here was a vague possibility.

1836	He canvassed his system. No ailment was found, and he investigated

1837	again. This time he thought he could detect colicky symptoms, and he

1838	began to encourage them with considerable hope. But they soon grew

1839	feeble, and presently died wholly away. He reflected further. Suddenly

1840	he discovered something. One of his upper front teeth was loose. This

1841	was lucky; he was about to begin to groan, as a “starter,” as he

1842	called it, when it occurred to him that if he came into court with that

1843	argument, his aunt would pull it out, and that would hurt. So he thought

1844	he would hold the tooth in reserve for the present, and seek further.

1845	Nothing offered for some little time, and then he remembered hearing

1846	the doctor tell about a certain thing that laid up a patient for two or

1847	three weeks and threatened to make him lose a finger. So the boy eagerly

1848	drew his sore toe from under the sheet and held it up for inspection.

1849	But now he did not know the necessary symptoms. However, it seemed

1850	well worth while to chance it, so he fell to groaning with considerable

1851	spirit.

1852	

1853	But Sid slept on unconscious.

1854	

1855	Tom groaned louder, and fancied that he began to feel pain in the toe.

1856	

1857	No result from Sid.

1858	

1859	Tom was panting with his exertions by this time. He took a rest and then

1860	swelled himself up and fetched a succession of admirable groans.

1861	

1862	Sid snored on.

1863	

1864	Tom was aggravated. He said, “Sid, Sid!” and shook him. This course

1865	worked well, and Tom began to groan again. Sid yawned, stretched, then

1866	brought himself up on his elbow with a snort, and began to stare at Tom.

1867	Tom went on groaning. Sid said:

1868	

1869	“Tom! Say, Tom!” [No response.] “Here, Tom! TOM! What is the matter,

1870	Tom?” And he shook him and looked in his face anxiously.

1871	

1872	Tom moaned out:

1873	

1874	“Oh, don’t, Sid. Don’t joggle me.”

1875	

1876	“Why, what’s the matter, Tom? I must call auntie.”

1877	

1878	“No—never mind. It’ll be over by and by, maybe. Don’t call anybody.”

1879	

1880	“But I must! _Don’t_ groan so, Tom, it’s awful. How long you been this

1881	way?”

1882	

1883	“Hours. Ouch! Oh, don’t stir so, Sid, you’ll kill me.”

1884	

1885	“Tom, why didn’t you wake me sooner? Oh, Tom, _don’t!_ It makes my flesh

1886	crawl to hear you. Tom, what is the matter?”

1887	

1888	“I forgive you everything, Sid. [Groan.] Everything you’ve ever done to

1889	me. When I’m gone—”

1890	

1891	“Oh, Tom, you ain’t dying, are you? Don’t, Tom—oh, don’t. Maybe—”

1892	

1893	“I forgive everybody, Sid. [Groan.] Tell ’em so, Sid. And Sid, you give

1894	my window-sash and my cat with one eye to that new girl that’s come to

1895	town, and tell her—”

1896	

1897	But Sid had snatched his clothes and gone. Tom was suffering in reality,

1898	now, so handsomely was his imagination working, and so his groans had

1899	gathered quite a genuine tone.

1900	

1901	Sid flew downstairs and said:

1902	

1903	“Oh, Aunt Polly, come! Tom’s dying!”

1904	

1905	“Dying!”

1906	

1907	“Yes’m. Don’t wait—come quick!”

1908	

1909	“Rubbage! I don’t believe it!”

1910	

1911	But she fled upstairs, nevertheless, with Sid and Mary at her heels.

1912	And her face grew white, too, and her lip trembled. When she reached the

1913	bedside she gasped out:

1914	

1915	“You, Tom! Tom, what’s the matter with you?”

1916	

1917	“Oh, auntie, I’m—”

1918	

1919	“What’s the matter with you—what is the matter with you, child?”

1920	

1921	“Oh, auntie, my sore toe’s mortified!”

1922	

1923	The old lady sank down into a chair and laughed a little, then cried a

1924	little, then did both together. This restored her and she said:

1925	

1926	“Tom, what a turn you did give me. Now you shut up that nonsense and

1927	climb out of this.”

1928	

1929	The groans ceased and the pain vanished from the toe. The boy felt a

1930	little foolish, and he said:

1931	

1932	“Aunt Polly, it _seemed_ mortified, and it hurt so I never minded my

1933	tooth at all.”

1934	

1935	“Your tooth, indeed! What’s the matter with your tooth?”

1936	

1937	“One of them’s loose, and it aches perfectly awful.”

1938	

1939	“There, there, now, don’t begin that groaning again. Open your mouth.

1940	Well—your tooth _is_ loose, but you’re not going to die about that.

1941	Mary, get me a silk thread, and a chunk of fire out of the kitchen.”

1942	

1943	Tom said:

1944	

1945	“Oh, please, auntie, don’t pull it out. It don’t hurt any more. I wish

1946	I may never stir if it does. Please don’t, auntie. I don’t want to stay

1947	home from school.”

1948	

1949	“Oh, you don’t, don’t you? So all this row was because you thought you’d

1950	get to stay home from school and go a-fishing? Tom, Tom, I love you so,

1951	and you seem to try every way you can to break my old heart with your

1952	outrageousness.” By this time the dental instruments were ready. The old

1953	lady made one end of the silk thread fast to Tom’s tooth with a loop

1954	and tied the other to the bedpost. Then she seized the chunk of fire and

1955	suddenly thrust it almost into the boy’s face. The tooth hung dangling

1956	by the bedpost, now.

1957	

1958	But all trials bring their compensations. As Tom wended to school after

1959	breakfast, he was the envy of every boy he met because the gap in his

1960	upper row of teeth enabled him to expectorate in a new and admirable

1961	way. He gathered quite a following of lads interested in the exhibition;

1962	and one that had cut his finger and had been a centre of fascination and
