1963	homage up to this time, now found himself suddenly without an adherent,

1964	and shorn of his glory. His heart was heavy, and he said with a disdain

1965	which he did not feel that it wasn’t anything to spit like Tom Sawyer;

1966	but another boy said, “Sour grapes!” and he wandered away a dismantled

1967	hero.

1968	

1969	Shortly Tom came upon the juvenile pariah of the village, Huckleberry

1970	Finn, son of the town drunkard. Huckleberry was cordially hated and

1971	dreaded by all the mothers of the town, because he was idle and lawless

1972	and vulgar and bad—and because all their children admired him so, and

1973	delighted in his forbidden society, and wished they dared to be like

1974	him. Tom was like the rest of the respectable boys, in that he envied

1975	Huckleberry his gaudy outcast condition, and was under strict orders

1976	not to play with him. So he played with him every time he got a chance.

1977	Huckleberry was always dressed in the cast-off clothes of full-grown

1978	men, and they were in perennial bloom and fluttering with rags. His hat

1979	was a vast ruin with a wide crescent lopped out of its brim; his coat,

1980	when he wore one, hung nearly to his heels and had the rearward buttons

1981	far down the back; but one suspender supported his trousers; the seat of

1982	the trousers bagged low and contained nothing, the fringed legs dragged

1983	in the dirt when not rolled up.

1984	

1985	Huckleberry came and went, at his own free will. He slept on doorsteps

1986	in fine weather and in empty hogsheads in wet; he did not have to go to

1987	school or to church, or call any being master or obey anybody; he could

1988	go fishing or swimming when and where he chose, and stay as long as it

1989	suited him; nobody forbade him to fight; he could sit up as late as he

1990	pleased; he was always the first boy that went barefoot in the spring

1991	and the last to resume leather in the fall; he never had to wash, nor

1992	put on clean clothes; he could swear wonderfully. In a word, everything

1993	that goes to make life precious that boy had. So thought every harassed,

1994	hampered, respectable boy in St. Petersburg.

1995	

1996	Tom hailed the romantic outcast:

1997	

1998	“Hello, Huckleberry!”

1999	

2000	“Hello yourself, and see how you like it.”

2001	

2002	“What’s that you got?”

2003	

2004	“Dead cat.”

2005	

2006	“Lemme see him, Huck. My, he’s pretty stiff. Where’d you get him?”

2007	

2008	“Bought him off’n a boy.”

2009	

2010	“What did you give?”

2011	

2012	“I give a blue ticket and a bladder that I got at the slaughter-house.”

2013	

2014	“Where’d you get the blue ticket?”

2015	

2016	“Bought it off’n Ben Rogers two weeks ago for a hoop-stick.”

2017	

2018	“Say—what is dead cats good for, Huck?”

2019	

2020	“Good for? Cure warts with.”

2021	

2022	“No! Is that so? I know something that’s better.”

2023	

2024	“I bet you don’t. What is it?”

2025	

2026	“Why, spunk-water.”

2027	

2028	“Spunk-water! I wouldn’t give a dern for spunk-water.”

2029	

2030	“You wouldn’t, wouldn’t you? D’you ever try it?”

2031	

2032	“No, I hain’t. But Bob Tanner did.”

2033	

2034	“Who told you so!”

2035	

2036	“Why, he told Jeff Thatcher, and Jeff told Johnny Baker, and Johnny

2037	told Jim Hollis, and Jim told Ben Rogers, and Ben told a nigger, and the

2038	nigger told me. There now!”

2039	

2040	“Well, what of it? They’ll all lie. Leastways all but the nigger. I

2041	don’t know _him_. But I never see a nigger that _wouldn’t_ lie. Shucks!

2042	Now you tell me how Bob Tanner done it, Huck.”

2043	

2044	“Why, he took and dipped his hand in a rotten stump where the rain-water

2045	was.”

2046	

2047	“In the daytime?”

2048	

2049	“Certainly.”

2050	

2051	“With his face to the stump?”

2052	

2053	“Yes. Least I reckon so.”

2054	

2055	“Did he say anything?”

2056	

2057	“I don’t reckon he did. I don’t know.”

2058	

2059	“Aha! Talk about trying to cure warts with spunk-water such a blame fool

2060	way as that! Why, that ain’t a-going to do any good. You got to go all

2061	by yourself, to the middle of the woods, where you know there’s a

2062	spunk-water stump, and just as it’s midnight you back up against the stump

2063	and jam your hand in and say:

2064	

2065	‘Barley-corn, barley-corn, injun-meal shorts, Spunk-water, spunk-water,

2066	swaller these warts,’

2067	

2068	and then walk away quick, eleven steps, with your eyes shut, and then

2069	turn around three times and walk home without speaking to anybody.

2070	Because if you speak the charm’s busted.”

2071	

2072	“Well, that sounds like a good way; but that ain’t the way Bob Tanner

2073	done.”

2074	

2075	“No, sir, you can bet he didn’t, becuz he’s the wartiest boy in this

2076	town; and he wouldn’t have a wart on him if he’d knowed how to work

2077	spunk-water. I’ve took off thousands of warts off of my hands that way,

2078	Huck. I play with frogs so much that I’ve always got considerable many

2079	warts. Sometimes I take ’em off with a bean.”

2080	

2081	“Yes, bean’s good. I’ve done that.”

2082	

2083	“Have you? What’s your way?”

2084	

2085	“You take and split the bean, and cut the wart so as to get some blood,

2086	and then you put the blood on one piece of the bean and take and dig

2087	a hole and bury it ’bout midnight at the crossroads in the dark of the

2088	moon, and then you burn up the rest of the bean. You see that piece

2089	that’s got the blood on it will keep drawing and drawing, trying to

2090	fetch the other piece to it, and so that helps the blood to draw the

2091	wart, and pretty soon off she comes.”

2092	

2093	“Yes, that’s it, Huck—that’s it; though when you’re burying it if you

2094	say ‘Down bean; off wart; come no more to bother me!’ it’s better.

2095	That’s the way Joe Harper does, and he’s been nearly to Coonville and

2096	most everywheres. But say—how do you cure ’em with dead cats?”

2097	

2098	“Why, you take your cat and go and get in the grave-yard ’long about

2099	midnight when somebody that was wicked has been buried; and when it’s

2100	midnight a devil will come, or maybe two or three, but you can’t see

2101	’em, you can only hear something like the wind, or maybe hear ’em talk;

2102	and when they’re taking that feller away, you heave your cat after ’em

2103	and say, ‘Devil follow corpse, cat follow devil, warts follow cat, I’m

2104	done with ye!’ That’ll fetch _any_ wart.”

2105	

2106	“Sounds right. D’you ever try it, Huck?”

2107	

2108	“No, but old Mother Hopkins told me.”

2109	

2110	“Well, I reckon it’s so, then. Becuz they say she’s a witch.”

2111	

2112	“Say! Why, Tom, I _know_ she is. She witched pap. Pap says so his own

2113	self. He come along one day, and he see she was a-witching him, so he

2114	took up a rock, and if she hadn’t dodged, he’d a got her. Well, that

2115	very night he rolled off’n a shed wher’ he was a layin drunk, and broke

2116	his arm.”

2117	

2118	“Why, that’s awful. How did he know she was a-witching him?”

2119	

2120	“Lord, pap can tell, easy. Pap says when they keep looking at you right

2121	stiddy, they’re a-witching you. Specially if they mumble. Becuz when

2122	they mumble they’re saying the Lord’s Prayer backards.”

2123	

2124	“Say, Hucky, when you going to try the cat?”

2125	

2126	“To-night. I reckon they’ll come after old Hoss Williams to-night.”

2127	

2128	“But they buried him Saturday. Didn’t they get him Saturday night?”

2129	

2130	“Why, how you talk! How could their charms work till midnight?—and

2131	_then_ it’s Sunday. Devils don’t slosh around much of a Sunday, I don’t

2132	reckon.”

2133	

2134	“I never thought of that. That’s so. Lemme go with you?”

2135	

2136	“Of course—if you ain’t afeard.”

2137	

2138	“Afeard! ’Tain’t likely. Will you meow?”

2139	

2140	“Yes—and you meow back, if you get a chance. Last time, you kep’ me

2141	a-meowing around till old Hays went to throwing rocks at me and says

2142	‘Dern that cat!’ and so I hove a brick through his window—but don’t you

2143	tell.”

2144	

2145	“I won’t. I couldn’t meow that night, becuz auntie was watching me, but

2146	I’ll meow this time. Say—what’s that?”

2147	

2148	“Nothing but a tick.”

2149	

2150	“Where’d you get him?”

2151	

2152	“Out in the woods.”

2153	

2154	“What’ll you take for him?”

2155	

2156	“I don’t know. I don’t want to sell him.”

2157	

2158	“All right. It’s a mighty small tick, anyway.”

2159	

2160	“Oh, anybody can run a tick down that don’t belong to them. I’m

2161	satisfied with it. It’s a good enough tick for me.”

2162	

2163	“Sho, there’s ticks a plenty. I could have a thousand of ’em if I wanted

2164	to.”

2165	

2166	“Well, why don’t you? Becuz you know mighty well you can’t. This is a

2167	pretty early tick, I reckon. It’s the first one I’ve seen this year.”

2168	

2169	“Say, Huck—I’ll give you my tooth for him.”

2170	

2171	“Less see it.”

2172	

2173	Tom got out a bit of paper and carefully unrolled it. Huckleberry viewed

2174	it wistfully. The temptation was very strong. At last he said:

2175	

2176	“Is it genuwyne?”

2177	

2178	Tom lifted his lip and showed the vacancy.

2179	

2180	“Well, all right,” said Huckleberry, “it’s a trade.”

2181	

2182	Tom enclosed the tick in the percussion-cap box that had lately been the

2183	pinchbug’s prison, and the boys separated, each feeling wealthier than

2184	before.

2185	

2186	When Tom reached the little isolated frame school-house, he strode in

2187	briskly, with the manner of one who had come with all honest speed. He

2188	hung his hat on a peg and flung himself into his seat with business-like

2189	alacrity. The master, throned on high in his great splint-bottom

2190	arm-chair, was dozing, lulled by the drowsy hum of study. The

2191	interruption roused him.

2192	

2193	“Thomas Sawyer!”

2194	

2195	Tom knew that when his name was pronounced in full, it meant trouble.

2196	

2197	“Sir!”

2198	

2199	“Come up here. Now, sir, why are you late again, as usual?”

2200	

2201	Tom was about to take refuge in a lie, when he saw two long tails of

2202	yellow hair hanging down a back that he recognized by the electric

2203	sympathy of love; and by that form was _the only vacant place_ on the

2204	girls’ side of the school-house. He instantly said:

2205	

2206	“_I stopped to talk with Huckleberry Finn!_”

2207	

2208	The master’s pulse stood still, and he stared helplessly. The buzz of

2209	study ceased. The pupils wondered if this foolhardy boy had lost his

2210	mind. The master said:

2211	

2212	“You—you did what?”

2213	

2214	“Stopped to talk with Huckleberry Finn.”

2215	

2216	There was no mistaking the words.

2217	

2218	“Thomas Sawyer, this is the most astounding confession I have ever

2219	listened to. No mere ferule will answer for this offence. Take off your

2220	jacket.”

2221	

2222	The master’s arm performed until it was tired and the stock of switches

2223	notably diminished. Then the order followed:

2224	

2225	“Now, sir, go and sit with the girls! And let this be a warning to you.”

2226	

2227	The titter that rippled around the room appeared to abash the boy, but

2228	in reality that result was caused rather more by his worshipful awe

2229	of his unknown idol and the dread pleasure that lay in his high good

2230	fortune. He sat down upon the end of the pine bench and the girl hitched

2231	herself away from him with a toss of her head. Nudges and winks and

2232	whispers traversed the room, but Tom sat still, with his arms upon the

2233	long, low desk before him, and seemed to study his book.

2234	

2235	By and by attention ceased from him, and the accustomed school murmur

2236	rose upon the dull air once more. Presently the boy began to steal

2237	furtive glances at the girl. She observed it, “made a mouth” at him

2238	and gave him the back of her head for the space of a minute. When she

2239	cautiously faced around again, a peach lay before her. She thrust it

2240	away. Tom gently put it back. She thrust it away again, but with less

2241	animosity. Tom patiently returned it to its place. Then she let it

2242	remain. Tom scrawled on his slate, “Please take it—I got more.” The

2243	girl glanced at the words, but made no sign. Now the boy began to draw

2244	something on the slate, hiding his work with his left hand. For a time

2245	the girl refused to notice; but her human curiosity presently began

2246	to manifest itself by hardly perceptible signs. The boy worked on,

2247	apparently unconscious. The girl made a sort of non-committal attempt

2248	to see, but the boy did not betray that he was aware of it. At last she

2249	gave in and hesitatingly whispered:

2250	

2251	“Let me see it.”

2252	

2253	Tom partly uncovered a dismal caricature of a house with two gable ends

2254	to it and a corkscrew of smoke issuing from the chimney. Then the girl’s

2255	interest began to fasten itself upon the work and she forgot everything

2256	else. When it was finished, she gazed a moment, then whispered:

2257	

2258	“It’s nice—make a man.”

2259	

2260	The artist erected a man in the front yard, that resembled a derrick. He

2261	could have stepped over the house; but the girl was not hypercritical;

2262	she was satisfied with the monster, and whispered:

2263	

2264	“It’s a beautiful man—now make me coming along.”

2265	

2266	Tom drew an hour-glass with a full moon and straw limbs to it and armed

2267	the spreading fingers with a portentous fan. The girl said:

2268	

2269	“It’s ever so nice—I wish I could draw.”

2270	

2271	“It’s easy,” whispered Tom, “I’ll learn you.”

2272	

2273	“Oh, will you? When?”

2274	

2275	“At noon. Do you go home to dinner?”

2276	

2277	“I’ll stay if you will.”

2278	

2279	“Good—that’s a whack. What’s your name?”

2280	

2281	“Becky Thatcher. What’s yours? Oh, I know. It’s Thomas Sawyer.”

2282	

2283	“That’s the name they lick me by. I’m Tom when I’m good. You call me

2284	Tom, will you?”

2285	

2286	“Yes.”

2287	

2288	Now Tom began to scrawl something on the slate, hiding the words from

2289	the girl. But she was not backward this time. She begged to see. Tom

2290	said:

2291	

2292	“Oh, it ain’t anything.”

2293	

2294	“Yes it is.”

2295	

2296	“No it ain’t. You don’t want to see.”

2297	

2298	“Yes I do, indeed I do. Please let me.”

2299	

2300	“You’ll tell.”

2301	

2302	“No I won’t—deed and deed and double deed won’t.”

2303	

2304	“You won’t tell anybody at all? Ever, as long as you live?”

2305	

2306	“No, I won’t ever tell _any_body. Now let me.”

2307	

2308	“Oh, _you_ don’t want to see!”

2309	

2310	“Now that you treat me so, I _will_ see.” And she put her small hand

2311	upon his and a little scuffle ensued, Tom pretending to resist in

2312	earnest but letting his hand slip by degrees till these words were

2313	revealed: “_I love you_.”

2314	

2315	“Oh, you bad thing!” And she hit his hand a smart rap, but reddened and

2316	looked pleased, nevertheless.

2317	

2318	Just at this juncture the boy felt a slow, fateful grip closing on his

2319	ear, and a steady lifting impulse. In that wise he was borne across the

2320	house and deposited in his own seat, under a peppering fire of giggles

2321	from the whole school. Then the master stood over him during a few awful

2322	moments, and finally moved away to his throne without saying a word. But

2323	although Tom’s ear tingled, his heart was jubilant.

2324	

2325	As the school quieted down Tom made an honest effort to study, but

2326	the turmoil within him was too great. In turn he took his place in the

2327	reading class and made a botch of it; then in the geography class and

2328	turned lakes into mountains, mountains into rivers, and rivers into

2329	continents, till chaos was come again; then in the spelling class, and

2330	got “turned down,” by a succession of mere baby words, till he brought

2331	up at the foot and yielded up the pewter medal which he had worn with

2332	ostentation for months.

2333	

2334	

2335	

2336	

2337	CHAPTER VII

2338	

2339	

2340	THE harder Tom tried to fasten his mind on his book, the more his ideas

2341	wandered. So at last, with a sigh and a yawn, he gave it up. It seemed

2342	to him that the noon recess would never come. The air was utterly dead.

2343	There was not a breath stirring. It was the sleepiest of sleepy days.

2344	The drowsing murmur of the five and twenty studying scholars soothed

2345	the soul like the spell that is in the murmur of bees. Away off in the

2346	flaming sunshine, Cardiff Hill lifted its soft green sides through a

2347	shimmering veil of heat, tinted with the purple of distance; a few birds

2348	floated on lazy wing high in the air; no other living thing was visible

2349	but some cows, and they were asleep. Tom’s heart ached to be free, or

2350	else to have something of interest to do to pass the dreary time.

2351	His hand wandered into his pocket and his face lit up with a glow of

2352	gratitude that was prayer, though he did not know it. Then furtively

2353	the percussion-cap box came out. He released the tick and put him on

2354	the long flat desk. The creature probably glowed with a gratitude that

2355	amounted to prayer, too, at this moment, but it was premature: for when

2356	he started thankfully to travel off, Tom turned him aside with a pin and

2357	made him take a new direction.

2358	

2359	Tom’s bosom friend sat next him, suffering just as Tom had been, and

2360	now he was deeply and gratefully interested in this entertainment in

2361	an instant. This bosom friend was Joe Harper. The two boys were sworn

2362	friends all the week, and embattled enemies on Saturdays. Joe took a

2363	pin out of his lapel and began to assist in exercising the prisoner.

2364	The sport grew in interest momently. Soon Tom said that they were

2365	interfering with each other, and neither getting the fullest benefit

2366	of the tick. So he put Joe’s slate on the desk and drew a line down the

2367	middle of it from top to bottom.

2368	

2369	“Now,” said he, “as long as he is on your side you can stir him up and

2370	I’ll let him alone; but if you let him get away and get on my side,

2371	you’re to leave him alone as long as I can keep him from crossing over.”

2372	

2373	“All right, go ahead; start him up.”

2374	

2375	The tick escaped from Tom, presently, and crossed the equator. Joe

2376	harassed him awhile, and then he got away and crossed back again. This

2377	change of base occurred often. While one boy was worrying the tick with

2378	absorbing interest, the other would look on with interest as strong, the

2379	two heads bowed together over the slate, and the two souls dead to all

2380	things else. At last luck seemed to settle and abide with Joe. The

2381	tick tried this, that, and the other course, and got as excited and as

2382	anxious as the boys themselves, but time and again just as he would

2383	have victory in his very grasp, so to speak, and Tom’s fingers would

2384	be twitching to begin, Joe’s pin would deftly head him off, and keep

2385	possession. At last Tom could stand it no longer. The temptation was too

2386	strong. So he reached out and lent a hand with his pin. Joe was angry in

2387	a moment. Said he:

2388	

2389	“Tom, you let him alone.”

2390	

2391	“I only just want to stir him up a little, Joe.”

2392	

2393	“No, sir, it ain’t fair; you just let him alone.”

2394	

2395	“Blame it, I ain’t going to stir him much.”

2396	

2397	“Let him alone, I tell you.”

2398	

2399	“I won’t!”

2400	

2401	“You shall—he’s on my side of the line.”

2402	

2403	“Look here, Joe Harper, whose is that tick?”

2404	

2405	“I don’t care whose tick he is—he’s on my side of the line, and you

2406	sha’n’t touch him.”

2407	

2408	“Well, I’ll just bet I will, though. He’s my tick and I’ll do what I

2409	blame please with him, or die!”

2410	

2411	A tremendous whack came down on Tom’s shoulders, and its duplicate on

2412	Joe’s; and for the space of two minutes the dust continued to fly from

2413	the two jackets and the whole school to enjoy it. The boys had been

2414	too absorbed to notice the hush that had stolen upon the school awhile

2415	before when the master came tiptoeing down the room and stood over them.

2416	He had contemplated a good part of the performance before he contributed

2417	his bit of variety to it.

2418	

2419	When school broke up at noon, Tom flew to Becky Thatcher, and whispered

2420	in her ear:

2421	

2422	“Put on your bonnet and let on you’re going home; and when you get to

2423	the corner, give the rest of ’em the slip, and turn down through the

2424	lane and come back. I’ll go the other way and come it over ’em the same

2425	way.”

2426	

2427	So the one went off with one group of scholars, and the other with

2428	another. In a little while the two met at the bottom of the lane, and

2429	when they reached the school they had it all to themselves. Then they

2430	sat together, with a slate before them, and Tom gave Becky the pencil

2431	and held her hand in his, guiding it, and so created another surprising

2432	house. When the interest in art began to wane, the two fell to talking.

2433	Tom was swimming in bliss. He said:

2434	

2435	“Do you love rats?”

2436	

2437	“No! I hate them!”

2438	

2439	“Well, I do, too—_live_ ones. But I mean dead ones, to swing round your

2440	head with a string.”

2441	

2442	“No, I don’t care for rats much, anyway. What I like is chewing-gum.”

2443	

2444	“Oh, I should say so! I wish I had some now.”

2445	

2446	“Do you? I’ve got some. I’ll let you chew it awhile, but you must give

2447	it back to me.”

2448	

2449	That was agreeable, so they chewed it turn about, and dangled their legs

2450	against the bench in excess of contentment.

2451	

2452	“Was you ever at a circus?” said Tom.

2453	

2454	“Yes, and my pa’s going to take me again some time, if I’m good.”

2455	

2456	“I been to the circus three or four times—lots of times. Church ain’t

2457	shucks to a circus. There’s things going on at a circus all the time.

2458	I’m going to be a clown in a circus when I grow up.”

2459	

2460	“Oh, are you! That will be nice. They’re so lovely, all spotted up.”

2461	

2462	“Yes, that’s so. And they get slathers of money—most a dollar a day, Ben

2463	Rogers says. Say, Becky, was you ever engaged?”

2464	

2465	“What’s that?”

2466	

2467	“Why, engaged to be married.”

2468	

2469	“No.”

2470	

2471	“Would you like to?”

2472	

2473	“I reckon so. I don’t know. What is it like?”

2474	

2475	“Like? Why it ain’t like anything. You only just tell a boy you won’t

2476	ever have anybody but him, ever ever ever, and then you kiss and that’s

2477	all. Anybody can do it.”

2478	

2479	“Kiss? What do you kiss for?”

2480	

2481	“Why, that, you know, is to—well, they always do that.”

2482	

2483	“Everybody?”

2484	

2485	“Why, yes, everybody that’s in love with each other. Do you remember

2486	what I wrote on the slate?”

2487	

2488	“Ye—yes.”

2489	

2490	“What was it?”

2491	

2492	“I sha’n’t tell you.”

2493	

2494	“Shall I tell _you_?”

2495	

2496	“Ye—yes—but some other time.”

2497	

2498	“No, now.”

2499	

2500	“No, not now—to-morrow.”

2501	

2502	“Oh, no, _now_. Please, Becky—I’ll whisper it, I’ll whisper it ever so

2503	easy.”

2504	

2505	Becky hesitating, Tom took silence for consent, and passed his arm about

2506	her waist and whispered the tale ever so softly, with his mouth close to

2507	her ear. And then he added:

2508	

2509	“Now you whisper it to me—just the same.”

2510	

2511	She resisted, for a while, and then said:

2512	

2513	“You turn your face away so you can’t see, and then I will. But you

2514	mustn’t ever tell anybody—_will_ you, Tom? Now you won’t, _will_ you?”

2515	

2516	“No, indeed, indeed I won’t. Now, Becky.”

2517	

2518	He turned his face away. She bent timidly around till her breath stirred

2519	his curls and whispered, “I—love—you!”

2520	

2521	Then she sprang away and ran around and around the desks and benches,

2522	with Tom after her, and took refuge in a corner at last, with her little

2523	white apron to her face. Tom clasped her about her neck and pleaded:

2524	

2525	“Now, Becky, it’s all done—all over but the kiss. Don’t you be afraid

2526	of that—it ain’t anything at all. Please, Becky.” And he tugged at her

2527	apron and the hands.

2528	

2529	By and by she gave up, and let her hands drop; her face, all glowing

2530	with the struggle, came up and submitted. Tom kissed the red lips and

2531	said:

2532	

2533	“Now it’s all done, Becky. And always after this, you know, you ain’t

2534	ever to love anybody but me, and you ain’t ever to marry anybody but me,

2535	ever never and forever. Will you?”

2536	

2537	“No, I’ll never love anybody but you, Tom, and I’ll never marry anybody

2538	but you—and you ain’t to ever marry anybody but me, either.”

2539	

2540	“Certainly. Of course. That’s _part_ of it. And always coming to school

2541	or when we’re going home, you’re to walk with me, when there ain’t

2542	anybody looking—and you choose me and I choose you at parties, because

2543	that’s the way you do when you’re engaged.”

2544	

2545	“It’s so nice. I never heard of it before.”

2546	

2547	“Oh, it’s ever so gay! Why, me and Amy Lawrence—”

2548	

2549	The big eyes told Tom his blunder and he stopped, confused.

2550	

2551	“Oh, Tom! Then I ain’t the first you’ve ever been engaged to!”

2552	

2553	The child began to cry. Tom said:

2554	

2555	“Oh, don’t cry, Becky, I don’t care for her any more.”

2556	

2557	“Yes, you do, Tom—you know you do.”

2558	

2559	Tom tried to put his arm about her neck, but she pushed him away and

2560	turned her face to the wall, and went on crying. Tom tried again, with

2561	soothing words in his mouth, and was repulsed again. Then his pride was

2562	up, and he strode away and went outside. He stood about, restless and

2563	uneasy, for a while, glancing at the door, every now and then, hoping

2564	she would repent and come to find him. But she did not. Then he began

2565	to feel badly and fear that he was in the wrong. It was a hard struggle

2566	with him to make new advances, now, but he nerved himself to it and

2567	entered. She was still standing back there in the corner, sobbing, with

2568	her face to the wall. Tom’s heart smote him. He went to her and stood a

2569	moment, not knowing exactly how to proceed. Then he said hesitatingly:

2570	

2571	“Becky, I—I don’t care for anybody but you.”

2572	

2573	No reply—but sobs.

2574	

2575	“Becky”—pleadingly. “Becky, won’t you say something?”

2576	

2577	More sobs.

2578	

2579	Tom got out his chiefest jewel, a brass knob from the top of an andiron,

2580	and passed it around her so that she could see it, and said:

2581	

2582	“Please, Becky, won’t you take it?”

2583	

2584	She struck it to the floor. Then Tom marched out of the house and over

2585	the hills and far away, to return to school no more that day. Presently

2586	Becky began to suspect. She ran to the door; he was not in sight; she

2587	flew around to the play-yard; he was not there. Then she called:

2588	

2589	“Tom! Come back, Tom!”

2590	

2591	She listened intently, but there was no answer. She had no companions

2592	but silence and loneliness. So she sat down to cry again and upbraid

2593	herself; and by this time the scholars began to gather again, and she

2594	had to hide her griefs and still her broken heart and take up the cross

2595	of a long, dreary, aching afternoon, with none among the strangers about

2596	her to exchange sorrows with.

2597	

2598	

2599	

2600	

2601	CHAPTER VIII

2602	

2603	

2604	TOM dodged hither and thither through lanes until he was well out of the

2605	track of returning scholars, and then fell into a moody jog. He crossed

2606	a small “branch” two or three times, because of a prevailing juvenile

2607	superstition that to cross water baffled pursuit. Half an hour later

2608	he was disappearing behind the Douglas mansion on the summit of Cardiff

2609	Hill, and the school-house was hardly distinguishable away off in the

2610	valley behind him. He entered a dense wood, picked his pathless way to

2611	the centre of it, and sat down on a mossy spot under a spreading oak.

2612	There was not even a zephyr stirring; the dead noonday heat had even

2613	stilled the songs of the birds; nature lay in a trance that was broken

2614	by no sound but the occasional far-off hammering of a wood-pecker, and

2615	this seemed to render the pervading silence and sense of loneliness the

2616	more profound. The boy’s soul was steeped in melancholy; his feelings

2617	were in happy accord with his surroundings. He sat long with his elbows

2618	on his knees and his chin in his hands, meditating. It seemed to him

2619	that life was but a trouble, at best, and he more than half envied Jimmy

2620	Hodges, so lately released; it must be very peaceful, he thought, to lie

2621	and slumber and dream forever and ever, with the wind whispering through

2622	the trees and caressing the grass and the flowers over the grave, and

2623	nothing to bother and grieve about, ever any more. If he only had a

2624	clean Sunday-school record he could be willing to go, and be done with

2625	it all. Now as to this girl. What had he done? Nothing. He had meant

2626	the best in the world, and been treated like a dog—like a very dog. She

2627	would be sorry some day—maybe when it was too late. Ah, if he could only

2628	die _temporarily_!

2629	

2630	But the elastic heart of youth cannot be compressed into one constrained

2631	shape long at a time. Tom presently began to drift insensibly back into

2632	the concerns of this life again. What if he turned his back, now, and

2633	disappeared mysteriously? What if he went away—ever so far away, into

2634	unknown countries beyond the seas—and never came back any more! How

2635	would she feel then! The idea of being a clown recurred to him now, only

2636	to fill him with disgust. For frivolity and jokes and spotted tights

2637	were an offense, when they intruded themselves upon a spirit that was

2638	exalted into the vague august realm of the romantic. No, he would be

2639	a soldier, and return after long years, all war-worn and illustrious.

2640	No—better still, he would join the Indians, and hunt buffaloes and go on

2641	the warpath in the mountain ranges and the trackless great plains of the

2642	Far West, and away in the future come back a great chief, bristling with

2643	feathers, hideous with paint, and prance into Sunday-school, some drowsy

2644	summer morning, with a blood-curdling war-whoop, and sear the eyeballs

2645	of all his companions with unappeasable envy. But no, there was

2646	something gaudier even than this. He would be a pirate! That was it!

2647	_now_ his future lay plain before him, and glowing with unimaginable

2648	splendor. How his name would fill the world, and make people shudder!

2649	How gloriously he would go plowing the dancing seas, in his long, low,

2650	black-hulled racer, the Spirit of the Storm, with his grisly flag flying

2651	at the fore! And at the zenith of his fame, how he would suddenly appear

2652	at the old village and stalk into church, brown and weather-beaten, in

2653	his black velvet doublet and trunks, his great jack-boots, his crimson

2654	sash, his belt bristling with horse-pistols, his crime-rusted cutlass

2655	at his side, his slouch hat with waving plumes, his black flag unfurled,

2656	with the skull and crossbones on it, and hear with swelling ecstasy

2657	the whisperings, “It’s Tom Sawyer the Pirate!—the Black Avenger of the

2658	Spanish Main!”

2659	

2660	Yes, it was settled; his career was determined. He would run away from

2661	home and enter upon it. He would start the very next morning. Therefore

2662	he must now begin to get ready. He would collect his resources together.

2663	He went to a rotten log near at hand and began to dig under one end of

2664	it with his Barlow knife. He soon struck wood that sounded hollow. He

2665	put his hand there and uttered this incantation impressively:

2666	

2667	“What hasn’t come here, come! What’s here, stay here!”

2668	

2669	Then he scraped away the dirt, and exposed a pine shingle. He took it

2670	up and disclosed a shapely little treasure-house whose bottom and sides

2671	were of shingles. In it lay a marble. Tom’s astonishment was bound-less!

2672	He scratched his head with a perplexed air, and said:

2673	

2674	“Well, that beats anything!”

2675	

2676	Then he tossed the marble away pettishly, and stood cogitating. The

2677	truth was, that a superstition of his had failed, here, which he and

2678	all his comrades had always looked upon as infallible. If you buried

2679	a marble with certain necessary incantations, and left it alone a

2680	fortnight, and then opened the place with the incantation he had just

2681	used, you would find that all the marbles you had ever lost had gathered

2682	themselves together there, meantime, no matter how widely they had been

2683	separated. But now, this thing had actually and unquestionably failed.

2684	Tom’s whole structure of faith was shaken to its foundations. He had

2685	many a time heard of this thing succeeding but never of its failing

2686	before. It did not occur to him that he had tried it several times

2687	before, himself, but could never find the hiding-places afterward. He

2688	puzzled over the matter some time, and finally decided that some witch

2689	had interfered and broken the charm. He thought he would satisfy himself

2690	on that point; so he searched around till he found a small sandy spot

2691	with a little funnel-shaped depression in it. He laid himself down and

2692	put his mouth close to this depression and called—

2693	

2694	“Doodle-bug, doodle-bug, tell me what I want to know! Doodle-bug,

2695	doodle-bug, tell me what I want to know!”

2696	

2697	The sand began to work, and presently a small black bug appeared for a

2698	second and then darted under again in a fright.

2699	

2700	“He dasn’t tell! So it _was_ a witch that done it. I just knowed it.”

2701	

2702	He well knew the futility of trying to contend against witches, so he

2703	gave up discouraged. But it occurred to him that he might as well have

2704	the marble he had just thrown away, and therefore he went and made a

2705	patient search for it. But he could not find it. Now he went back to his

2706	treasure-house and carefully placed himself just as he had been standing

2707	when he tossed the marble away; then he took another marble from his

2708	pocket and tossed it in the same way, saying:

2709	

2710	“Brother, go find your brother!”

2711	

2712	He watched where it stopped, and went there and looked. But it must

2713	have fallen short or gone too far; so he tried twice more. The last

2714	repetition was successful. The two marbles lay within a foot of each

2715	other.

2716	

2717	Just here the blast of a toy tin trumpet came faintly down the green

2718	aisles of the forest. Tom flung off his jacket and trousers, turned

2719	a suspender into a belt, raked away some brush behind the rotten log,

2720	disclosing a rude bow and arrow, a lath sword and a tin trumpet, and

2721	in a moment had seized these things and bounded away, barelegged,

2722	with fluttering shirt. He presently halted under a great elm, blew an

2723	answering blast, and then began to tiptoe and look warily out, this way

2724	and that. He said cautiously—to an imaginary company:

2725	

2726	“Hold, my merry men! Keep hid till I blow.”

2727	

2728	Now appeared Joe Harper, as airily clad and elaborately armed as Tom.

2729	Tom called:

2730	

2731	“Hold! Who comes here into Sherwood Forest without my pass?”

2732	

2733	“Guy of Guisborne wants no man’s pass. Who art thou that—that—”

2734	

2735	“Dares to hold such language,” said Tom, prompting—for they talked “by

2736	the book,” from memory.

2737	

2738	“Who art thou that dares to hold such language?”

2739	

2740	“I, indeed! I am Robin Hood, as thy caitiff carcase soon shall know.”

2741	

2742	“Then art thou indeed that famous outlaw? Right gladly will I dispute

2743	with thee the passes of the merry wood. Have at thee!”

2744	

2745	They took their lath swords, dumped their other traps on the ground,

2746	struck a fencing attitude, foot to foot, and began a grave, careful

2747	combat, “two up and two down.” Presently Tom said:

2748	

2749	“Now, if you’ve got the hang, go it lively!”

2750	

2751	So they “went it lively,” panting and perspiring with the work. By and

2752	by Tom shouted:

2753	

2754	“Fall! fall! Why don’t you fall?”

2755	

2756	“I sha’n’t! Why don’t you fall yourself? You’re getting the worst of

2757	it.”

2758	

2759	“Why, that ain’t anything. I can’t fall; that ain’t the way it is in the

2760	book. The book says, ‘Then with one back-handed stroke he slew poor Guy

2761	of Guisborne.’ You’re to turn around and let me hit you in the back.”

2762	

2763	There was no getting around the authorities, so Joe turned, received the

2764	whack and fell.

2765	

2766	“Now,” said Joe, getting up, “you got to let me kill _you_. That’s

2767	fair.”

2768	

2769	“Why, I can’t do that, it ain’t in the book.”

2770	

2771	“Well, it’s blamed mean—that’s all.”

2772	

2773	“Well, say, Joe, you can be Friar Tuck or Much the miller’s son, and lam

2774	me with a quarter-staff; or I’ll be the Sheriff of Nottingham and you be

2775	Robin Hood a little while and kill me.”

2776	

2777	This was satisfactory, and so these adventures were carried out. Then

2778	Tom became Robin Hood again, and was allowed by the treacherous nun to

2779	bleed his strength away through his neglected wound. And at last Joe,

2780	representing a whole tribe of weeping outlaws, dragged him sadly forth,

2781	gave his bow into his feeble hands, and Tom said, “Where this arrow

2782	falls, there bury poor Robin Hood under the greenwood tree.” Then he

2783	shot the arrow and fell back and would have died, but he lit on a nettle

2784	and sprang up too gaily for a corpse.

2785	

2786	The boys dressed themselves, hid their accoutrements, and went off

2787	grieving that there were no outlaws any more, and wondering what modern

2788	civilization could claim to have done to compensate for their loss.

2789	They said they would rather be outlaws a year in Sherwood Forest than

2790	President of the United States forever.

2791	

2792	

2793	

2794	

2795	CHAPTER IX

2796	

2797	

2798	AT half-past nine, that night, Tom and Sid were sent to bed, as usual.

2799	They said their prayers, and Sid was soon asleep. Tom lay awake and

2800	waited, in restless impatience. When it seemed to him that it must be

2801	nearly daylight, he heard the clock strike ten! This was despair. He

2802	would have tossed and fidgeted, as his nerves demanded, but he was

2803	afraid he might wake Sid. So he lay still, and stared up into the dark.

2804	Everything was dismally still. By and by, out of the stillness, little,

2805	scarcely perceptible noises began to emphasize themselves. The ticking

2806	of the clock began to bring itself into notice. Old beams began to crack

2807	mysteriously. The stairs creaked faintly. Evidently spirits were abroad.

2808	A measured, muffled snore issued from Aunt Polly’s chamber. And now the

2809	tiresome chirping of a cricket that no human ingenuity could locate,

2810	began. Next the ghastly ticking of a death-watch in the wall at the

2811	bed’s head made Tom shudder—it meant that somebody’s days were numbered.

2812	Then the howl of a far-off dog rose on the night air, and was answered

2813	by a fainter howl from a remoter distance. Tom was in an agony. At last

2814	he was satisfied that time had ceased and eternity begun; he began to

2815	doze, in spite of himself; the clock chimed eleven, but he did not hear

2816	it. And then there came, mingling with his half-formed dreams, a most

2817	melancholy caterwauling. The raising of a neighboring window disturbed

2818	him. A cry of “Scat! you devil!” and the crash of an empty bottle

2819	against the back of his aunt’s woodshed brought him wide awake, and a

2820	single minute later he was dressed and out of the window and creeping

2821	along the roof of the “ell” on all fours. He “meow’d” with caution once

2822	or twice, as he went; then jumped to the roof of the woodshed and thence

2823	to the ground. Huckleberry Finn was there, with his dead cat. The boys

2824	moved off and disappeared in the gloom. At the end of half an hour they

2825	were wading through the tall grass of the graveyard.

2826	

2827	It was a graveyard of the old-fashioned Western kind. It was on a hill,

2828	about a mile and a half from the village. It had a crazy board fence

2829	around it, which leaned inward in places, and outward the rest of the

2830	time, but stood upright nowhere. Grass and weeds grew rank over the

2831	whole cemetery. All the old graves were sunken in, there was not a

2832	tombstone on the place; round-topped, worm-eaten boards staggered over

2833	the graves, leaning for support and finding none. “Sacred to the memory

2834	of” So-and-So had been painted on them once, but it could no longer have

2835	been read, on the most of them, now, even if there had been light.

2836	

2837	A faint wind moaned through the trees, and Tom feared it might be the

2838	spirits of the dead, complaining at being disturbed. The boys talked

2839	little, and only under their breath, for the time and the place and the

2840	pervading solemnity and silence oppressed their spirits. They found the

2841	sharp new heap they were seeking, and ensconced themselves within the

2842	protection of three great elms that grew in a bunch within a few feet of

2843	the grave.

2844	

2845	Then they waited in silence for what seemed a long time. The hooting of

2846	a distant owl was all the sound that troubled the dead stillness. Tom’s

2847	reflections grew oppressive. He must force some talk. So he said in a

2848	whisper:

2849	

2850	“Hucky, do you believe the dead people like it for us to be here?”

2851	

2852	Huckleberry whispered:

2853	

2854	“I wisht I knowed. It’s awful solemn like, _ain’t_ it?”

2855	

2856	“I bet it is.”

2857	

2858	There was a considerable pause, while the boys canvassed this matter

2859	inwardly. Then Tom whispered:

2860	

2861	“Say, Hucky—do you reckon Hoss Williams hears us talking?”

2862	

2863	“O’ course he does. Least his sperrit does.”

2864	

2865	Tom, after a pause:

2866	

2867	“I wish I’d said Mister Williams. But I never meant any harm. Everybody

2868	calls him Hoss.”

2869	

2870	“A body can’t be too partic’lar how they talk ’bout these-yer dead

2871	people, Tom.”

2872	

2873	This was a damper, and conversation died again.

2874	

2875	Presently Tom seized his comrade’s arm and said:

2876	

2877	“Sh!”

2878	

2879	“What is it, Tom?” And the two clung together with beating hearts.

2880	

2881	“Sh! There ’tis again! Didn’t you hear it?”

2882	

2883	“I—”

2884	

2885	“There! Now you hear it.”

2886	

2887	“Lord, Tom, they’re coming! They’re coming, sure. What’ll we do?”

2888	

2889	“I dono. Think they’ll see us?”

2890	

2891	“Oh, Tom, they can see in the dark, same as cats. I wisht I hadn’t

2892	come.”

2893	

2894	“Oh, don’t be afeard. I don’t believe they’ll bother us. We ain’t doing

2895	any harm. If we keep perfectly still, maybe they won’t notice us at

2896	all.”

2897	

2898	“I’ll try to, Tom, but, Lord, I’m all of a shiver.”

2899	

2900	“Listen!”

2901	

2902	The boys bent their heads together and scarcely breathed. A muffled

2903	sound of voices floated up from the far end of the graveyard.

2904	

2905	“Look! See there!” whispered Tom. “What is it?”

2906	

2907	“It’s devil-fire. Oh, Tom, this is awful.”

2908	

2909	Some vague figures approached through the gloom, swinging an

2910	old-fashioned tin lantern that freckled the ground with innumerable

2911	little spangles of light. Presently Huckleberry whispered with a

2912	shudder:

2913	

2914	“It’s the devils sure enough. Three of ’em! Lordy, Tom, we’re goners!

2915	Can you pray?”

2916	

2917	“I’ll try, but don’t you be afeard. They ain’t going to hurt us. ‘Now I

2918	lay me down to sleep, I—’”

2919	

2920	“Sh!”

2921	

2922	“What is it, Huck?”

2923	

2924	“They’re _humans_! One of ’em is, anyway. One of ’em’s old Muff Potter’s

2925	voice.”

2926	

2927	“No—’tain’t so, is it?”

2928	

2929	“I bet I know it. Don’t you stir nor budge. He ain’t sharp enough to

2930	notice us. Drunk, the same as usual, likely—blamed old rip!”

2931	

2932	“All right, I’ll keep still. Now they’re stuck. Can’t find it. Here they

2933	come again. Now they’re hot. Cold again. Hot again. Red hot! They’re

2934	p’inted right, this time. Say, Huck, I know another o’ them voices; it’s

2935	Injun Joe.”

2936	

2937	“That’s so—that murderin’ half-breed! I’d druther they was devils a dern

2938	sight. What kin they be up to?”

2939	

2940	The whisper died wholly out, now, for the three men had reached the

2941	grave and stood within a few feet of the boys’ hiding-place.

2942	

2943	“Here it is,” said the third voice; and the owner of it held the lantern

2944	up and revealed the face of young Doctor Robinson.

2945	

2946	Potter and Injun Joe were carrying a handbarrow with a rope and a couple

2947	of shovels on it. They cast down their load and began to open the grave.

2948	The doctor put the lantern at the head of the grave and came and sat

2949	down with his back against one of the elm trees. He was so close the

2950	boys could have touched him.

2951	

2952	“Hurry, men!” he said, in a low voice; “the moon might come out at any

2953	moment.”

2954	

2955	They growled a response and went on digging. For some time there was no

2956	noise but the grating sound of the spades discharging their freight of

2957	mould and gravel. It was very monotonous. Finally a spade struck upon

2958	the coffin with a dull woody accent, and within another minute or two

2959	the men had hoisted it out on the ground. They pried off the lid with

2960	their shovels, got out the body and dumped it rudely on the ground. The

2961	moon drifted from behind the clouds and exposed the pallid face.

2962	The barrow was got ready and the corpse placed on it, covered with a

2963	blanket, and bound to its place with the rope. Potter took out a large

2964	spring-knife and cut off the dangling end of the rope and then said:

2965	

2966	“Now the cussed thing’s ready, Sawbones, and you’ll just out with

2967	another five, or here she stays.”

2968	

2969	“That’s the talk!” said Injun Joe.

2970	

2971	“Look here, what does this mean?” said the doctor. “You required your

2972	pay in advance, and I’ve paid you.”

2973	

2974	“Yes, and you done more than that,” said Injun Joe, approaching the

2975	doctor, who was now standing. “Five years ago you drove me away from

2976	your father’s kitchen one night, when I come to ask for something to

2977	eat, and you said I warn’t there for any good; and when I swore I’d get

2978	even with you if it took a hundred years, your father had me jailed for

2979	a vagrant. Did you think I’d forget? The Injun blood ain’t in me for

2980	nothing. And now I’ve _got_ you, and you got to _settle_, you know!”

2981	

2982	He was threatening the doctor, with his fist in his face, by this time.

2983	The doctor struck out suddenly and stretched the ruffian on the ground.

2984	Potter dropped his knife, and exclaimed:

2985	

2986	“Here, now, don’t you hit my pard!” and the next moment he had grappled

2987	with the doctor and the two were struggling with might and main,

2988	trampling the grass and tearing the ground with their heels. Injun Joe

2989	sprang to his feet, his eyes flaming with passion, snatched up Potter’s

2990	knife, and went creeping, catlike and stooping, round and round about

2991	the combatants, seeking an opportunity. All at once the doctor flung

2992	himself free, seized the heavy headboard of Williams’ grave and felled

2993	Potter to the earth with it—and in the same instant the half-breed saw

2994	his chance and drove the knife to the hilt in the young man’s breast. He

2995	reeled and fell partly upon Potter, flooding him with his blood, and in

2996	the same moment the clouds blotted out the dreadful spectacle and the

2997	two frightened boys went speeding away in the dark.

2998	

2999	Presently, when the moon emerged again, Injun Joe was standing over the

3000	two forms, contemplating them. The doctor murmured inarticulately, gave

3001	a long gasp or two and was still. The half-breed muttered:

3002	

3003	“_That_ score is settled—damn you.”

3004	

3005	Then he robbed the body. After which he put the fatal knife in Potter’s

3006	open right hand, and sat down on the dismantled coffin. Three—four—five

3007	minutes passed, and then Potter began to stir and moan. His hand closed

3008	upon the knife; he raised it, glanced at it, and let it fall, with a

3009	shudder. Then he sat up, pushing the body from him, and gazed at it, and

3010	then around him, confusedly. His eyes met Joe’s.

3011	

3012	“Lord, how is this, Joe?” he said.

3013	

3014	“It’s a dirty business,” said Joe, without moving.

3015	

3016	“What did you do it for?”

3017	

3018	“I! I never done it!”

3019	

3020	“Look here! That kind of talk won’t wash.”

3021	

3022	Potter trembled and grew white.

3023	

3024	“I thought I’d got sober. I’d no business to drink to-night. But it’s

3025	in my head yet—worse’n when we started here. I’m all in a muddle;

3026	can’t recollect anything of it, hardly. Tell me, Joe—_honest_, now,

3027	old feller—did I do it? Joe, I never meant to—’pon my soul and honor, I

3028	never meant to, Joe. Tell me how it was, Joe. Oh, it’s awful—and him so

3029	young and promising.”

3030	

3031	“Why, you two was scuffling, and he fetched you one with the headboard

3032	and you fell flat; and then up you come, all reeling and staggering

3033	like, and snatched the knife and jammed it into him, just as he fetched

3034	you another awful clip—and here you’ve laid, as dead as a wedge til

3035	now.”

3036	

3037	“Oh, I didn’t know what I was a-doing. I wish I may die this minute if I

3038	did. It was all on account of the whiskey and the excitement, I reckon.

3039	I never used a weepon in my life before, Joe. I’ve fought, but never

3040	with weepons. They’ll all say that. Joe, don’t tell! Say you won’t tell,

3041	Joe—that’s a good feller. I always liked you, Joe, and stood up for you,

3042	too. Don’t you remember? You _won’t_ tell, _will_ you, Joe?” And the

3043	poor creature dropped on his knees before the stolid murderer, and

3044	clasped his appealing hands.

3045	

3046	“No, you’ve always been fair and square with me, Muff Potter, and I

3047	won’t go back on you. There, now, that’s as fair as a man can say.”

3048	

3049	“Oh, Joe, you’re an angel. I’ll bless you for this the longest day I

3050	live.” And Potter began to cry.

3051	

3052	“Come, now, that’s enough of that. This ain’t any time for blubbering.

3053	You be off yonder way and I’ll go this. Move, now, and don’t leave any

3054	tracks behind you.”

3055	

3056	Potter started on a trot that quickly increased to a run. The half-breed

3057	stood looking after him. He muttered:

3058	

3059	“If he’s as much stunned with the lick and fuddled with the rum as he

3060	had the look of being, he won’t think of the knife till he’s gone so

3061	far he’ll be afraid to come back after it to such a place by

3062	himself—chicken-heart!”

3063	

3064	Two or three minutes later the murdered man, the blanketed corpse, the

3065	lidless coffin, and the open grave were under no inspection but the

3066	moon’s. The stillness was complete again, too.

3067	

3068	

3069	

3070	

3071	CHAPTER X

3072	

3073	

3074	THE two boys flew on and on, toward the village, speechless with

3075	horror. They glanced backward over their shoulders from time to time,

3076	apprehensively, as if they feared they might be followed. Every stump

3077	that started up in their path seemed a man and an enemy, and made them

3078	catch their breath; and as they sped by some outlying cottages that lay

3079	near the village, the barking of the aroused watch-dogs seemed to give

3080	wings to their feet.

3081	

3082	“If we can only get to the old tannery before we break down!” whispered

3083	Tom, in short catches between breaths. “I can’t stand it much longer.”

3084	

3085	Huckleberry’s hard pantings were his only reply, and the boys fixed

3086	their eyes on the goal of their hopes and bent to their work to win it.

3087	They gained steadily on it, and at last, breast to breast, they burst

3088	through the open door and fell grateful and exhausted in the sheltering

3089	shadows beyond. By and by their pulses slowed down, and Tom whispered:

3090	

3091	“Huckleberry, what do you reckon’ll come of this?”

3092	

3093	“If Doctor Robinson dies, I reckon hanging’ll come of it.”

3094	

3095	“Do you though?”

3096	

3097	“Why, I _know_ it, Tom.”

3098	

3099	Tom thought a while, then he said:

3100	

3101	“Who’ll tell? We?”

3102	

3103	“What are you talking about? S’pose something happened and Injun Joe

3104	_didn’t_ hang? Why, he’d kill us some time or other, just as dead sure

3105	as we’re a laying here.”

3106	

3107	“That’s just what I was thinking to myself, Huck.”

3108	

3109	“If anybody tells, let Muff Potter do it, if he’s fool enough. He’s

3110	generally drunk enough.”

3111	

3112	Tom said nothing—went on thinking. Presently he whispered:

3113	

3114	“Huck, Muff Potter don’t know it. How can he tell?”

3115	

3116	“What’s the reason he don’t know it?”

3117	

3118	“Because he’d just got that whack when Injun Joe done it. D’you reckon

3119	he could see anything? D’you reckon he knowed anything?”

3120	

3121	“By hokey, that’s so, Tom!”

3122	

3123	“And besides, look-a-here—maybe that whack done for _him_!”

3124	

3125	“No, ’taint likely, Tom. He had liquor in him; I could see that; and

3126	besides, he always has. Well, when pap’s full, you might take and belt

3127	him over the head with a church and you couldn’t phase him. He says so,

3128	his own self. So it’s the same with Muff Potter, of course. But if a man

3129	was dead sober, I reckon maybe that whack might fetch him; I dono.”

3130	

3131	After another reflective silence, Tom said:

3132	

3133	“Hucky, you sure you can keep mum?”

3134	

3135	“Tom, we _got_ to keep mum. You know that. That Injun devil wouldn’t

3136	make any more of drownding us than a couple of cats, if we was to squeak

3137	’bout this and they didn’t hang him. Now, look-a-here, Tom, less take

3138	and swear to one another—that’s what we got to do—swear to keep mum.”

3139	

3140	“I’m agreed. It’s the best thing. Would you just hold hands and swear

3141	that we—”

3142	

3143	“Oh no, that wouldn’t do for this. That’s good enough for little

3144	rubbishy common things—specially with gals, cuz _they_ go back on you

3145	anyway, and blab if they get in a huff—but there orter be writing ’bout

3146	a big thing like this. And blood.”

3147	

3148	Tom’s whole being applauded this idea. It was deep, and dark, and awful;

3149	the hour, the circumstances, the surroundings, were in keeping with it.

3150	He picked up a clean pine shingle that lay in the moon-light, took a

3151	little fragment of “red keel” out of his pocket, got the moon on

3152	his work, and painfully scrawled these lines, emphasizing each slow

3153	down-stroke by clamping his tongue between his teeth, and letting up the

3154	pressure on the up-strokes. [See next page.]

3155	

3156	“Huck Finn and Tom Sawyer swears they will keep mum about This and They

3157	wish They may Drop down dead in Their Tracks if They ever Tell and Rot.”

3158	

3159	Huckleberry was filled with admiration of Tom’s facility in writing, and

3160	the sublimity of his language. He at once took a pin from his lapel and

3161	was going to prick his flesh, but Tom said:

3162	

3163	“Hold on! Don’t do that. A pin’s brass. It might have verdigrease on

3164	it.”

3165	

3166	“What’s verdigrease?”

3167	

3168	“It’s p’ison. That’s what it is. You just swaller some of it once—you’ll

3169	see.”

3170	

3171	So Tom unwound the thread from one of his needles, and each boy pricked

3172	the ball of his thumb and squeezed out a drop of blood. In time, after

3173	many squeezes, Tom managed to sign his initials, using the ball of his

3174	little finger for a pen. Then he showed Huckleberry how to make an H and

3175	an F, and the oath was complete. They buried the shingle close to the

3176	wall, with some dismal ceremonies and incantations, and the fetters

3177	that bound their tongues were considered to be locked and the key thrown

3178	away.

3179	

3180	A figure crept stealthily through a break in the other end of the ruined

3181	building, now, but they did not notice it.

3182	

3183	“Tom,” whispered Huckleberry, “does this keep us from _ever_

3184	telling—_always_?”

3185	

3186	“Of course it does. It don’t make any difference _what_ happens, we got

3187	to keep mum. We’d drop down dead—don’t _you_ know that?”

3188	

3189	“Yes, I reckon that’s so.”

3190	

3191	They continued to whisper for some little time. Presently a dog set up

3192	a long, lugubrious howl just outside—within ten feet of them. The boys

3193	clasped each other suddenly, in an agony of fright.

3194	

3195	“Which of us does he mean?” gasped Huckleberry.

3196	

3197	“I dono—peep through the crack. Quick!”

3198	

3199	“No, _you_, Tom!”

3200	

3201	“I can’t—I can’t _do_ it, Huck!”

3202	

3203	“Please, Tom. There ’tis again!”

3204	

3205	“Oh, lordy, I’m thankful!” whispered Tom. “I know his voice. It’s Bull

3206	Harbison.” *

3207	

3208	[* If Mr. Harbison owned a slave named Bull, Tom would have spoken of

3209	him as “Harbison’s Bull,” but a son or a dog of that name was “Bull

3210	Harbison.”]

3211	

3212	“Oh, that’s good—I tell you, Tom, I was most scared to death; I’d a bet

3213	anything it was a _stray_ dog.”

3214	

3215	The dog howled again. The boys’ hearts sank once more.

3216	

3217	“Oh, my! that ain’t no Bull Harbison!” whispered Huckleberry. “_Do_,

3218	Tom!”

3219	

3220	Tom, quaking with fear, yielded, and put his eye to the crack. His

3221	whisper was hardly audible when he said:

3222	

3223	“Oh, Huck, _its a stray dog_!”

3224	

3225	“Quick, Tom, quick! Who does he mean?”

3226	

3227	“Huck, he must mean us both—we’re right together.”

3228	

3229	“Oh, Tom, I reckon we’re goners. I reckon there ain’t no mistake ’bout

3230	where _I’ll_ go to. I been so wicked.”

3231	

3232	“Dad fetch it! This comes of playing hookey and doing everything a

3233	feller’s told _not_ to do. I might a been good, like Sid, if I’d a

3234	tried—but no, I wouldn’t, of course. But if ever I get off this time,

3235	I lay I’ll just _waller_ in Sunday-schools!” And Tom began to snuffle a

3236	little.

3237	

3238	“_You_ bad!” and Huckleberry began to snuffle too. “Consound it, Tom

3239	Sawyer, you’re just old pie, ’long-side o’ what I am. Oh, _lordy_,

3240	lordy, lordy, I wisht I only had half your chance.”

3241	

3242	Tom choked off and whispered:

3243	

3244	“Look, Hucky, look! He’s got his _back_ to us!”

3245	

3246	Hucky looked, with joy in his heart.

3247	

3248	“Well, he has, by jingoes! Did he before?”

3249	

3250	“Yes, he did. But I, like a fool, never thought. Oh, this is bully, you

3251	know. _Now_ who can he mean?”

3252	

3253	The howling stopped. Tom pricked up his ears.

3254	

3255	“Sh! What’s that?” he whispered.

3256	

3257	“Sounds like—like hogs grunting. No—it’s somebody snoring, Tom.”

3258	

3259	“That _is_ it! Where ’bouts is it, Huck?”

3260	

3261	“I bleeve it’s down at ’tother end. Sounds so, anyway. Pap used to sleep

3262	there, sometimes, ’long with the hogs, but laws bless you, he just lifts

3263	things when _he_ snores. Besides, I reckon he ain’t ever coming back to

3264	this town any more.”

3265	

3266	The spirit of adventure rose in the boys’ souls once more.

3267	

3268	“Hucky, do you das’t to go if I lead?”

3269	

3270	“I don’t like to, much. Tom, s’pose it’s Injun Joe!”

3271	

3272	Tom quailed. But presently the temptation rose up strong again and the

3273	boys agreed to try, with the understanding that they would take to their

3274	heels if the snoring stopped. So they went tiptoeing stealthily down,

3275	the one behind the other. When they had got to within five steps of the

3276	snorer, Tom stepped on a stick, and it broke with a sharp snap. The man

3277	moaned, writhed a little, and his face came into the moonlight. It was

3278	Muff Potter. The boys’ hearts had stood still, and their hopes too,

3279	when the man moved, but their fears passed away now. They tip-toed out,

3280	through the broken weather-boarding, and stopped at a little distance

3281	to exchange a parting word. That long, lugubrious howl rose on the night

3282	air again! They turned and saw the strange dog standing within a few

3283	feet of where Potter was lying, and _facing_ Potter, with his nose

3284	pointing heavenward.

3285	

3286	“Oh, geeminy, it’s _him_!” exclaimed both boys, in a breath.

3287	

3288	“Say, Tom—they say a stray dog come howling around Johnny Miller’s

3289	house, ’bout midnight, as much as two weeks ago; and a whippoorwill come

3290	in and lit on the banisters and sung, the very same evening; and there

3291	ain’t anybody dead there yet.”

3292	

3293	“Well, I know that. And suppose there ain’t. Didn’t Gracie Miller fall

3294	in the kitchen fire and burn herself terrible the very next Saturday?”

3295	

3296	“Yes, but she ain’t _dead_. And what’s more, she’s getting better, too.”

3297	

3298	“All right, you wait and see. She’s a goner, just as dead sure as Muff

3299	Potter’s a goner. That’s what the niggers say, and they know all about

3300	these kind of things, Huck.”

3301	

3302	Then they separated, cogitating. When Tom crept in at his bedroom window

3303	the night was almost spent. He undressed with excessive caution, and

3304	fell asleep congratulating himself that nobody knew of his escapade. He

3305	was not aware that the gently-snoring Sid was awake, and had been so for

3306	an hour.

3307	

3308	When Tom awoke, Sid was dressed and gone. There was a late look in the

3309	light, a late sense in the atmosphere. He was startled. Why had he not

3310	been called—persecuted till he was up, as usual? The thought filled

3311	him with bodings. Within five minutes he was dressed and down-stairs,

3312	feeling sore and drowsy. The family were still at table, but they had

3313	finished breakfast. There was no voice of rebuke; but there were averted

3314	eyes; there was a silence and an air of solemnity that struck a chill

3315	to the culprit’s heart. He sat down and tried to seem gay, but it

3316	was up-hill work; it roused no smile, no response, and he lapsed into

3317	silence and let his heart sink down to the depths.

3318	

3319	After breakfast his aunt took him aside, and Tom almost brightened in

3320	the hope that he was going to be flogged; but it was not so. His aunt

3321	wept over him and asked him how he could go and break her old heart so;

3322	and finally told him to go on, and ruin himself and bring her gray hairs

3323	with sorrow to the grave, for it was no use for her to try any more.

3324	This was worse than a thousand whippings, and Tom’s heart was sorer now

3325	than his body. He cried, he pleaded for forgiveness, promised to reform

3326	over and over again, and then received his dismissal, feeling that

3327	he had won but an imperfect forgiveness and established but a feeble

3328	confidence.

3329	

3330	He left the presence too miserable to even feel revengeful toward

3331	Sid; and so the latter’s prompt retreat through the back gate was

3332	unnecessary. He moped to school gloomy and sad, and took his flogging,

3333	along with Joe Harper, for playing hookey the day before, with the

3334	air of one whose heart was busy with heavier woes and wholly dead to

3335	trifles. Then he betook himself to his seat, rested his elbows on his

3336	desk and his jaws in his hands, and stared at the wall with the stony

3337	stare of suffering that has reached the limit and can no further go.

3338	His elbow was pressing against some hard substance. After a long time

3339	he slowly and sadly changed his position, and took up this object with

3340	a sigh. It was in a paper. He unrolled it. A long, lingering, colossal

3341	sigh followed, and his heart broke. It was his brass andiron knob!

3342	

3343	This final feather broke the camel’s back.

3344	

3345	

3346	

3347	

3348	CHAPTER XI

3349	

3350	

3351	CLOSE upon the hour of noon the whole village was suddenly electrified

3352	with the ghastly news. No need of the as yet un-dreamed-of telegraph;

3353	the tale flew from man to man, from group to group, from house to house,

3354	with little less than telegraphic speed. Of course the schoolmaster gave

3355	holi-day for that afternoon; the town would have thought strangely of

3356	him if he had not.

3357	

3358	A gory knife had been found close to the murdered man, and it had been

3359	recognized by somebody as belonging to Muff Potter—so the story ran. And

3360	it was said that a belated citizen had come upon Potter washing himself

3361	in the “branch” about one or two o’clock in the morning, and that Potter

3362	had at once sneaked off—suspicious circumstances, especially the washing

3363	which was not a habit with Potter. It was also said that the town had

3364	been ransacked for this “murderer” (the public are not slow in the

3365	matter of sifting evidence and arriving at a verdict), but that he

3366	could not be found. Horsemen had departed down all the roads in every

3367	direction, and the Sheriff “was confident” that he would be captured

3368	before night.

3369	

3370	All the town was drifting toward the graveyard. Tom’s heartbreak

3371	vanished and he joined the procession, not because he would not

3372	a thousand times rather go anywhere else, but because an awful,

3373	unaccountable fascination drew him on. Arrived at the dreadful place, he

3374	wormed his small body through the crowd and saw the dismal spectacle.

3375	It seemed to him an age since he was there before. Somebody pinched

3376	his arm. He turned, and his eyes met Huckleberry’s. Then both looked

3377	elsewhere at once, and wondered if anybody had noticed anything in their

3378	mutual glance. But everybody was talking, and intent upon the grisly

3379	spectacle before them.

3380	

3381	“Poor fellow!” “Poor young fellow!” “This ought to be a lesson to grave

3382	robbers!” “Muff Potter’ll hang for this if they catch him!” This was the

3383	drift of remark; and the minister said, “It was a judgment; His hand is

3384	here.”

3385	

3386	Now Tom shivered from head to heel; for his eye fell upon the stolid

3387	face of Injun Joe. At this moment the crowd began to sway and struggle,

3388	and voices shouted, “It’s him! it’s him! he’s coming himself!”

3389	

3390	“Who? Who?” from twenty voices.

3391	

3392	“Muff Potter!”

3393	

3394	“Hallo, he’s stopped!—Look out, he’s turning! Don’t let him get away!”

3395	

3396	People in the branches of the trees over Tom’s head said he wasn’t

3397	trying to get away—he only looked doubtful and perplexed.

3398	

3399	“Infernal impudence!” said a bystander; “wanted to come and take a quiet

3400	look at his work, I reckon—didn’t expect any company.”

3401	

3402	The crowd fell apart, now, and the Sheriff came through, ostentatiously

3403	leading Potter by the arm. The poor fellow’s face was haggard, and

3404	his eyes showed the fear that was upon him. When he stood before the

3405	murdered man, he shook as with a palsy, and he put his face in his hands

3406	and burst into tears.

3407	

3408	“I didn’t do it, friends,” he sobbed; “’pon my word and honor I never

3409	done it.”

3410	

3411	“Who’s accused you?” shouted a voice.

3412	

3413	This shot seemed to carry home. Potter lifted his face and looked around

3414	him with a pathetic hopelessness in his eyes. He saw Injun Joe, and

3415	exclaimed:

3416	

3417	“Oh, Injun Joe, you promised me you’d never—”

3418	

3419	“Is that your knife?” and it was thrust before him by the Sheriff.

3420	

3421	Potter would have fallen if they had not caught him and eased him to the

3422	ground. Then he said:

3423	

3424	“Something told me ’t if I didn’t come back and get—” He shuddered; then

3425	waved his nerveless hand with a vanquished gesture and said, “Tell ’em,

3426	Joe, tell ’em—it ain’t any use any more.”

3427	

3428	Then Huckleberry and Tom stood dumb and staring, and heard the

3429	stony-hearted liar reel off his serene statement, they expecting every

3430	moment that the clear sky would deliver God’s lightnings upon his head,

3431	and wondering to see how long the stroke was delayed. And when he had

3432	finished and still stood alive and whole, their wavering impulse to

3433	break their oath and save the poor betrayed prisoner’s life faded and

3434	vanished away, for plainly this miscreant had sold himself to Satan and

3435	it would be fatal to meddle with the property of such a power as that.

3436	

3437	“Why didn’t you leave? What did you want to come here for?” somebody

3438	said.

3439	

3440	“I couldn’t help it—I couldn’t help it,” Potter moaned. “I wanted to

3441	run away, but I couldn’t seem to come anywhere but here.” And he fell to

3442	sobbing again.

3443	

3444	Injun Joe repeated his statement, just as calmly, a few minutes

3445	afterward on the inquest, under oath; and the boys, seeing that the

3446	lightnings were still withheld, were confirmed in their belief that

3447	Joe had sold himself to the devil. He was now become, to them, the most

3448	balefully interesting object they had ever looked upon, and they could

3449	not take their fascinated eyes from his face.

3450	

3451	They inwardly resolved to watch him nights, when opportunity should

3452	offer, in the hope of getting a glimpse of his dread master.

3453	

3454	Injun Joe helped to raise the body of the murdered man and put it in

3455	a wagon for removal; and it was whispered through the shuddering

3456	crowd that the wound bled a little! The boys thought that this happy

3457	circumstance would turn suspicion in the right direction; but they were

3458	disappointed, for more than one villager remarked:

3459	

3460	“It was within three feet of Muff Potter when it done it.”

3461	

3462	Tom’s fearful secret and gnawing conscience disturbed his sleep for as

3463	much as a week after this; and at breakfast one morning Sid said:

3464	

3465	“Tom, you pitch around and talk in your sleep so much that you keep me

3466	awake half the time.”

3467	

3468	Tom blanched and dropped his eyes.

3469	

3470	“It’s a bad sign,” said Aunt Polly, gravely. “What you got on your mind,

3471	Tom?”

3472	

3473	“Nothing. Nothing ’t I know of.” But the boy’s hand shook so that he

3474	spilled his coffee.

3475	

3476	“And you do talk such stuff,” Sid said. “Last night you said, ‘It’s

3477	blood, it’s blood, that’s what it is!’ You said that over and over.

3478	And you said, ‘Don’t torment me so—I’ll tell!’ Tell _what_? What is it

3479	you’ll tell?”

3480	

3481	Everything was swimming before Tom. There is no telling what might have

3482	happened, now, but luckily the concern passed out of Aunt Polly’s face

3483	and she came to Tom’s relief without knowing it. She said:

3484	

3485	“Sho! It’s that dreadful murder. I dream about it most every night

3486	myself. Sometimes I dream it’s me that done it.”

3487	

3488	Mary said she had been affected much the same way. Sid seemed satisfied.

3489	Tom got out of the presence as quick as he plausibly could, and after

3490	that he complained of toothache for a week, and tied up his jaws every

3491	night. He never knew that Sid lay nightly watching, and frequently

3492	slipped the bandage free and then leaned on his elbow listening a good

3493	while at a time, and afterward slipped the bandage back to its place

3494	again. Tom’s distress of mind wore off gradually and the toothache grew

3495	irksome and was discarded. If Sid really managed to make anything out of

3496	Tom’s disjointed mutterings, he kept it to himself.

3497	

3498	It seemed to Tom that his schoolmates never would get done holding

3499	inquests on dead cats, and thus keeping his trouble present to his mind.

3500	Sid noticed that Tom never was coroner at one of these inquiries,

3501	though it had been his habit to take the lead in all new enterprises;

3502	he noticed, too, that Tom never acted as a witness—and that was strange;

3503	and Sid did not overlook the fact that Tom even showed a marked aversion

3504	to these inquests, and always avoided them when he could. Sid marvelled,

3505	but said nothing. However, even inquests went out of vogue at last, and

3506	ceased to torture Tom’s conscience.

3507	

3508	Every day or two, during this time of sorrow, Tom watched his

3509	opportunity and went to the little grated jail-window and smuggled such

3510	small comforts through to the “murderer” as he could get hold of. The

3511	jail was a trifling little brick den that stood in a marsh at the edge

3512	of the village, and no guards were afforded for it; indeed, it

3513	was seldom occupied. These offerings greatly helped to ease Tom’s

3514	conscience.

3515	

3516	The villagers had a strong desire to tar-and-feather Injun Joe and ride

3517	him on a rail, for body-snatching, but so formidable was his character

3518	that nobody could be found who was willing to take the lead in the

3519	matter, so it was dropped. He had been careful to begin both of his

3520	inquest-statements with the fight, without confessing the grave-robbery

3521	that preceded it; therefore it was deemed wisest not to try the case in

3522	the courts at present.

3523	

3524	

3525	

3526	

3527	CHAPTER XII

3528	

3529	

3530	ONE of the reasons why Tom’s mind had drifted away from its secret

3531	troubles was, that it had found a new and weighty matter to interest

3532	itself about. Becky Thatcher had stopped coming to school. Tom had

3533	struggled with his pride a few days, and tried to “whistle her down the

3534	wind,” but failed. He began to find himself hanging around her father’s

3535	house, nights, and feeling very miserable. She was ill. What if she

3536	should die! There was distraction in the thought. He no longer took an

3537	interest in war, nor even in piracy. The charm of life was gone; there

3538	was nothing but dreariness left. He put his hoop away, and his bat;

3539	there was no joy in them any more. His aunt was concerned. She began to

3540	try all manner of remedies on him. She was one of those people who

3541	are infatuated with patent medicines and all new-fangled methods of

3542	producing health or mending it. She was an inveterate experimenter in

3543	these things. When something fresh in this line came out she was in a

3544	fever, right away, to try it; not on herself, for she was never ailing,

3545	but on anybody else that came handy. She was a subscriber for all the

3546	“Health” periodicals and phrenological frauds; and the solemn ignorance

3547	they were inflated with was breath to her nostrils. All the “rot” they

3548	contained about ventilation, and how to go to bed, and how to get up,

3549	and what to eat, and what to drink, and how much exercise to take, and

3550	what frame of mind to keep one’s self in, and what sort of clothing

3551	to wear, was all gospel to her, and she never observed that her

3552	health-journals of the current month customarily upset everything they

3553	had recommended the month before. She was as simple-hearted and honest

3554	as the day was long, and so she was an easy victim. She gathered

3555	together her quack periodicals and her quack medicines, and thus armed

3556	with death, went about on her pale horse, metaphorically speaking, with

3557	“hell following after.” But she never suspected that she was not an

3558	angel of healing and the balm of Gilead in disguise, to the suffering

3559	neighbors.

3560	

3561	The water treatment was new, now, and Tom’s low condition was a windfall

3562	to her. She had him out at daylight every morning, stood him up in the

3563	wood-shed and drowned him with a deluge of cold water; then she scrubbed

3564	him down with a towel like a file, and so brought him to; then she

3565	rolled him up in a wet sheet and put him away under blankets till she

3566	sweated his soul clean and “the yellow stains of it came through his

3567	pores”—as Tom said.

3568	

3569	Yet notwithstanding all this, the boy grew more and more melancholy and

3570	pale and dejected. She added hot baths, sitz baths, shower baths, and

3571	plunges. The boy remained as dismal as a hearse. She began to assist the

3572	water with a slim oatmeal diet and blister-plasters. She calculated his

3573	capacity as she would a jug’s, and filled him up every day with quack

3574	cure-alls.

3575	

3576	Tom had become indifferent to persecution by this time. This phase

3577	filled the old lady’s heart with consternation. This indifference must

3578	be broken up at any cost. Now she heard of Pain-killer for the first

3579	time. She ordered a lot at once. She tasted it and was filled with

3580	gratitude. It was simply fire in a liquid form. She dropped the water

3581	treatment and everything else, and pinned her faith to Pain-killer.

3582	She gave Tom a teaspoonful and watched with the deepest anxiety for the

3583	result. Her troubles were instantly at rest, her soul at peace again;

3584	for the “indifference” was broken up. The boy could not have shown a

3585	wilder, heartier interest, if she had built a fire under him.

3586	

3587	Tom felt that it was time to wake up; this sort of life might be

3588	romantic enough, in his blighted condition, but it was getting to have

3589	too little sentiment and too much distracting variety about it. So he

3590	thought over various plans for relief, and finally hit upon that of

3591	professing to be fond of Pain-killer. He asked for it so often that he

3592	became a nuisance, and his aunt ended by telling him to help himself and

3593	quit bothering her. If it had been Sid, she would have had no misgivings

3594	to alloy her delight; but since it was Tom, she watched the bottle

3595	clandestinely. She found that the medicine did really diminish, but it

3596	did not occur to her that the boy was mending the health of a crack in

3597	the sitting-room floor with it.

3598	

3599	One day Tom was in the act of dosing the crack when his aunt’s yellow

3600	cat came along, purring, eyeing the teaspoon avariciously, and begging

3601	for a taste. Tom said:

3602	

3603	“Don’t ask for it unless you want it, Peter.”

3604	

3605	But Peter signified that he did want it.

3606	

3607	“You better make sure.”

3608	

3609	Peter was sure.

3610	

3611	“Now you’ve asked for it, and I’ll give it to you, because there ain’t

3612	anything mean about me; but if you find you don’t like it, you mustn’t

3613	blame anybody but your own self.”

3614	

3615	Peter was agreeable. So Tom pried his mouth open and poured down

3616	the Pain-killer. Peter sprang a couple of yards in the air, and then

3617	delivered a war-whoop and set off round and round the room, banging
