3618	against furniture, upsetting flower-pots, and making general havoc. Next

3619	he rose on his hind feet and pranced around, in a frenzy of enjoyment,

3620	with his head over his shoulder and his voice proclaiming his

3621	unappeasable happiness. Then he went tearing around the house again

3622	spreading chaos and destruction in his path. Aunt Polly entered in time

3623	to see him throw a few double summersets, deliver a final mighty hurrah,

3624	and sail through the open window, carrying the rest of the flower-pots

3625	with him. The old lady stood petrified with astonishment, peering over

3626	her glasses; Tom lay on the floor expiring with laughter.

3627	

3628	“Tom, what on earth ails that cat?”

3629	

3630	“I don’t know, aunt,” gasped the boy.

3631	

3632	“Why, I never see anything like it. What did make him act so?”

3633	

3634	“Deed I don’t know, Aunt Polly; cats always act so when they’re having a

3635	good time.”

3636	

3637	“They do, do they?” There was something in the tone that made Tom

3638	apprehensive.

3639	

3640	“Yes’m. That is, I believe they do.”

3641	

3642	“You _do_?”

3643	

3644	“Yes’m.”

3645	

3646	The old lady was bending down, Tom watching, with interest emphasized

3647	by anxiety. Too late he divined her “drift.” The handle of the telltale

3648	tea-spoon was visible under the bed-valance. Aunt Polly took it, held it

3649	up. Tom winced, and dropped his eyes. Aunt Polly raised him by the usual

3650	handle—his ear—and cracked his head soundly with her thimble.

3651	

3652	“Now, sir, what did you want to treat that poor dumb beast so, for?”

3653	

3654	“I done it out of pity for him—because he hadn’t any aunt.”

3655	

3656	“Hadn’t any aunt!—you numskull. What has that got to do with it?”

3657	

3658	“Heaps. Because if he’d had one she’d a burnt him out herself! She’d a

3659	roasted his bowels out of him ’thout any more feeling than if he was a

3660	human!”

3661	

3662	Aunt Polly felt a sudden pang of remorse. This was putting the thing in

3663	a new light; what was cruelty to a cat _might_ be cruelty to a boy, too.

3664	She began to soften; she felt sorry. Her eyes watered a little, and she

3665	put her hand on Tom’s head and said gently:

3666	

3667	“I was meaning for the best, Tom. And, Tom, it _did_ do you good.”

3668	

3669	Tom looked up in her face with just a perceptible twinkle peeping

3670	through his gravity.

3671	

3672	“I know you was meaning for the best, aunty, and so was I with Peter. It

3673	done _him_ good, too. I never see him get around so since—”

3674	

3675	“Oh, go ’long with you, Tom, before you aggravate me again. And you try

3676	and see if you can’t be a good boy, for once, and you needn’t take any

3677	more medicine.”

3678	

3679	Tom reached school ahead of time. It was noticed that this strange thing

3680	had been occurring every day latterly. And now, as usual of late,

3681	he hung about the gate of the schoolyard instead of playing with his

3682	comrades. He was sick, he said, and he looked it. He tried to seem to

3683	be looking everywhere but whither he really was looking—down the road.

3684	Presently Jeff Thatcher hove in sight, and Tom’s face lighted; he gazed

3685	a moment, and then turned sorrowfully away. When Jeff arrived, Tom

3686	accosted him; and “led up” warily to opportunities for remark about

3687	Becky, but the giddy lad never could see the bait. Tom watched and

3688	watched, hoping whenever a frisking frock came in sight, and hating the

3689	owner of it as soon as he saw she was not the right one. At last frocks

3690	ceased to appear, and he dropped hopelessly into the dumps; he entered

3691	the empty schoolhouse and sat down to suffer. Then one more frock passed

3692	in at the gate, and Tom’s heart gave a great bound. The next instant he

3693	was out, and “going on” like an Indian; yelling, laughing, chasing boys,

3694	jumping over the fence at risk of life and limb, throwing handsprings,

3695	standing on his head—doing all the heroic things he could conceive of,

3696	and keeping a furtive eye out, all the while, to see if Becky Thatcher

3697	was noticing. But she seemed to be unconscious of it all; she never

3698	looked. Could it be possible that she was not aware that he was there?

3699	He carried his exploits to her immediate vicinity; came war-whooping

3700	around, snatched a boy’s cap, hurled it to the roof of the schoolhouse,

3701	broke through a group of boys, tumbling them in every direction, and

3702	fell sprawling, himself, under Becky’s nose, almost upsetting her—and

3703	she turned, with her nose in the air, and he heard her say: “Mf! some

3704	people think they’re mighty smart—always showing off!”

3705	

3706	Tom’s cheeks burned. He gathered himself up and sneaked off, crushed and

3707	crestfallen.

3708	

3709	

3710	

3711	

3712	CHAPTER XIII

3713	

3714	

3715	TOM’S mind was made up now. He was gloomy and desperate. He was a

3716	forsaken, friendless boy, he said; nobody loved him; when they found out

3717	what they had driven him to, perhaps they would be sorry; he had tried

3718	to do right and get along, but they would not let him; since nothing

3719	would do them but to be rid of him, let it be so; and let them blame

3720	_him_ for the consequences—why shouldn’t they? What right had the

3721	friendless to complain? Yes, they had forced him to it at last: he would

3722	lead a life of crime. There was no choice.

3723	

3724	By this time he was far down Meadow Lane, and the bell for school to

3725	“take up” tinkled faintly upon his ear. He sobbed, now, to think he

3726	should never, never hear that old familiar sound any more—it was very

3727	hard, but it was forced on him; since he was driven out into the cold

3728	world, he must submit—but he forgave them. Then the sobs came thick and

3729	fast.

3730	

3731	Just at this point he met his soul’s sworn comrade, Joe

3732	Harper—hard-eyed, and with evidently a great and dismal purpose in his

3733	heart. Plainly here were “two souls with but a single thought.” Tom,

3734	wiping his eyes with his sleeve, began to blubber out something about

3735	a resolution to escape from hard usage and lack of sympathy at home by

3736	roaming abroad into the great world never to return; and ended by hoping

3737	that Joe would not forget him.

3738	

3739	But it transpired that this was a request which Joe had just been going

3740	to make of Tom, and had come to hunt him up for that purpose. His mother

3741	had whipped him for drinking some cream which he had never tasted and

3742	knew nothing about; it was plain that she was tired of him and wished

3743	him to go; if she felt that way, there was nothing for him to do but

3744	succumb; he hoped she would be happy, and never regret having driven her

3745	poor boy out into the unfeeling world to suffer and die.

3746	

3747	As the two boys walked sorrowing along, they made a new compact to stand

3748	by each other and be brothers and never separate till death relieved

3749	them of their troubles. Then they began to lay their plans. Joe was for

3750	being a hermit, and living on crusts in a remote cave, and dying,

3751	some time, of cold and want and grief; but after listening to Tom, he

3752	conceded that there were some conspicuous advantages about a life of

3753	crime, and so he consented to be a pirate.

3754	

3755	Three miles below St. Petersburg, at a point where the Mississippi River

3756	was a trifle over a mile wide, there was a long, narrow, wooded island,

3757	with a shallow bar at the head of it, and this offered well as a

3758	rendezvous. It was not inhabited; it lay far over toward the further

3759	shore, abreast a dense and almost wholly unpeopled forest. So Jackson’s

3760	Island was chosen. Who were to be the subjects of their piracies was a

3761	matter that did not occur to them. Then they hunted up Huckleberry Finn,

3762	and he joined them promptly, for all careers were one to him; he was

3763	indifferent. They presently separated to meet at a lonely spot on the

3764	river-bank two miles above the village at the favorite hour—which was

3765	midnight. There was a small log raft there which they meant to capture.

3766	Each would bring hooks and lines, and such provision as he could steal

3767	in the most dark and mysterious way—as became outlaws. And before the

3768	afternoon was done, they had all managed to enjoy the sweet glory of

3769	spreading the fact that pretty soon the town would “hear something.” All

3770	who got this vague hint were cautioned to “be mum and wait.”

3771	

3772	About midnight Tom arrived with a boiled ham and a few trifles,

3773	and stopped in a dense undergrowth on a small bluff overlooking the

3774	meeting-place. It was starlight, and very still. The mighty river lay

3775	like an ocean at rest. Tom listened a moment, but no sound disturbed the

3776	quiet. Then he gave a low, distinct whistle. It was answered from under

3777	the bluff. Tom whistled twice more; these signals were answered in the

3778	same way. Then a guarded voice said:

3779	

3780	“Who goes there?”

3781	

3782	“Tom Sawyer, the Black Avenger of the Spanish Main. Name your names.”

3783	

3784	“Huck Finn the Red-Handed, and Joe Harper the Terror of the Seas.” Tom

3785	had furnished these titles, from his favorite literature.

3786	

3787	“’Tis well. Give the countersign.”

3788	

3789	Two hoarse whispers delivered the same awful word simultaneously to the

3790	brooding night:

3791	

3792	“_Blood_!”

3793	

3794	Then Tom tumbled his ham over the bluff and let himself down after it,

3795	tearing both skin and clothes to some extent in the effort. There was

3796	an easy, comfortable path along the shore under the bluff, but it lacked

3797	the advantages of difficulty and danger so valued by a pirate.

3798	

3799	The Terror of the Seas had brought a side of bacon, and had about worn

3800	himself out with getting it there. Finn the Red-Handed had stolen a

3801	skillet and a quantity of half-cured leaf tobacco, and had also brought

3802	a few corn-cobs to make pipes with. But none of the pirates smoked or

3803	“chewed” but himself. The Black Avenger of the Spanish Main said it

3804	would never do to start without some fire. That was a wise thought;

3805	matches were hardly known there in that day. They saw a fire smouldering

3806	upon a great raft a hundred yards above, and they went stealthily

3807	thither and helped themselves to a chunk. They made an imposing

3808	adventure of it, saying, “Hist!” every now and then, and suddenly

3809	halting with finger on lip; moving with hands on imaginary dagger-hilts;

3810	and giving orders in dismal whispers that if “the foe” stirred, to “let

3811	him have it to the hilt,” because “dead men tell no tales.” They knew

3812	well enough that the raftsmen were all down at the village laying

3813	in stores or having a spree, but still that was no excuse for their

3814	conducting this thing in an unpiratical way.

3815	

3816	They shoved off, presently, Tom in command, Huck at the after oar and

3817	Joe at the forward. Tom stood amidships, gloomy-browed, and with folded

3818	arms, and gave his orders in a low, stern whisper:

3819	

3820	“Luff, and bring her to the wind!”

3821	

3822	“Aye-aye, sir!”

3823	

3824	“Steady, steady-y-y-y!”

3825	

3826	“Steady it is, sir!”

3827	

3828	“Let her go off a point!”

3829	

3830	“Point it is, sir!”

3831	

3832	As the boys steadily and monotonously drove the raft toward mid-stream

3833	it was no doubt understood that these orders were given only for

3834	“style,” and were not intended to mean anything in particular.

3835	

3836	“What sail’s she carrying?”

3837	

3838	“Courses, tops’ls, and flying-jib, sir.”

3839	

3840	“Send the r’yals up! Lay out aloft, there, half a dozen of

3841	ye—foretopmaststuns’l! Lively, now!”

3842	

3843	“Aye-aye, sir!”

3844	

3845	“Shake out that maintogalans’l! Sheets and braces! _now_ my hearties!”

3846	

3847	“Aye-aye, sir!”

3848	

3849	“Hellum-a-lee—hard a port! Stand by to meet her when she comes! Port,

3850	port! _Now_, men! With a will! Stead-y-y-y!”

3851	

3852	“Steady it is, sir!”

3853	

3854	The raft drew beyond the middle of the river; the boys pointed her head

3855	right, and then lay on their oars. The river was not high, so there was

3856	not more than a two or three mile current. Hardly a word was said during

3857	the next three-quarters of an hour. Now the raft was passing before

3858	the distant town. Two or three glimmering lights showed where it lay,

3859	peacefully sleeping, beyond the vague vast sweep of star-gemmed water,

3860	unconscious of the tremendous event that was happening. The Black

3861	Avenger stood still with folded arms, “looking his last” upon the scene

3862	of his former joys and his later sufferings, and wishing “she” could see

3863	him now, abroad on the wild sea, facing peril and death with dauntless

3864	heart, going to his doom with a grim smile on his lips. It was but

3865	a small strain on his imagination to remove Jackson’s Island beyond

3866	eye-shot of the village, and so he “looked his last” with a broken and

3867	satisfied heart. The other pirates were looking their last, too; and

3868	they all looked so long that they came near letting the current drift

3869	them out of the range of the island. But they discovered the danger in

3870	time, and made shift to avert it. About two o’clock in the morning the

3871	raft grounded on the bar two hundred yards above the head of the island,

3872	and they waded back and forth until they had landed their freight. Part

3873	of the little raft’s belongings consisted of an old sail, and this they

3874	spread over a nook in the bushes for a tent to shelter their provisions;

3875	but they themselves would sleep in the open air in good weather, as

3876	became outlaws.

3877	

3878	They built a fire against the side of a great log twenty or thirty steps

3879	within the sombre depths of the forest, and then cooked some bacon in

3880	the frying-pan for supper, and used up half of the corn “pone” stock

3881	they had brought. It seemed glorious sport to be feasting in that wild,

3882	free way in the virgin forest of an unexplored and uninhabited island,

3883	far from the haunts of men, and they said they never would return to

3884	civilization. The climbing fire lit up their faces and threw its ruddy

3885	glare upon the pillared tree-trunks of their forest temple, and upon the

3886	varnished foliage and festooning vines.

3887	

3888	When the last crisp slice of bacon was gone, and the last allowance

3889	of corn pone devoured, the boys stretched themselves out on the grass,

3890	filled with contentment. They could have found a cooler place, but

3891	they would not deny themselves such a romantic feature as the roasting

3892	campfire.

3893	

3894	“_Ain’t_ it gay?” said Joe.

3895	

3896	“It’s _nuts_!” said Tom. “What would the boys say if they could see us?”

3897	

3898	“Say? Well, they’d just die to be here—hey, Hucky!”

3899	

3900	“I reckon so,” said Huckleberry; “anyways, I’m suited. I don’t want

3901	nothing better’n this. I don’t ever get enough to eat, gen’ally—and here

3902	they can’t come and pick at a feller and bullyrag him so.”

3903	

3904	“It’s just the life for me,” said Tom. “You don’t have to get up,

3905	mornings, and you don’t have to go to school, and wash, and all that

3906	blame foolishness. You see a pirate don’t have to do _anything_, Joe,

3907	when he’s ashore, but a hermit _he_ has to be praying considerable, and

3908	then he don’t have any fun, anyway, all by himself that way.”

3909	

3910	“Oh yes, that’s so,” said Joe, “but I hadn’t thought much about it, you

3911	know. I’d a good deal rather be a pirate, now that I’ve tried it.”

3912	

3913	“You see,” said Tom, “people don’t go much on hermits, nowadays, like

3914	they used to in old times, but a pirate’s always respected. And

3915	a hermit’s got to sleep on the hardest place he can find, and put

3916	sackcloth and ashes on his head, and stand out in the rain, and—”

3917	

3918	“What does he put sackcloth and ashes on his head for?” inquired Huck.

3919	

3920	“I dono. But they’ve _got_ to do it. Hermits always do. You’d have to do

3921	that if you was a hermit.”

3922	

3923	“Dern’d if I would,” said Huck.

3924	

3925	“Well, what would you do?”

3926	

3927	“I dono. But I wouldn’t do that.”

3928	

3929	“Why, Huck, you’d _have_ to. How’d you get around it?”

3930	

3931	“Why, I just wouldn’t stand it. I’d run away.”

3932	

3933	“Run away! Well, you _would_ be a nice old slouch of a hermit. You’d be

3934	a disgrace.”

3935	

3936	The Red-Handed made no response, being better employed. He had finished

3937	gouging out a cob, and now he fitted a weed stem to it, loaded it with

3938	tobacco, and was pressing a coal to the charge and blowing a cloud of

3939	fragrant smoke—he was in the full bloom of luxurious contentment. The

3940	other pirates envied him this majestic vice, and secretly resolved to

3941	acquire it shortly. Presently Huck said:

3942	

3943	“What does pirates have to do?”

3944	

3945	Tom said:

3946	

3947	“Oh, they have just a bully time—take ships and burn them, and get the

3948	money and bury it in awful places in their island where there’s ghosts

3949	and things to watch it, and kill everybody in the ships—make ’em walk a

3950	plank.”

3951	

3952	“And they carry the women to the island,” said Joe; “they don’t kill the

3953	women.”

3954	

3955	“No,” assented Tom, “they don’t kill the women—they’re too noble. And

3956	the women’s always beautiful, too.

3957	

3958	“And don’t they wear the bulliest clothes! Oh no! All gold and silver

3959	and di’monds,” said Joe, with enthusiasm.

3960	

3961	“Who?” said Huck.

3962	

3963	“Why, the pirates.”

3964	

3965	Huck scanned his own clothing forlornly.

3966	

3967	“I reckon I ain’t dressed fitten for a pirate,” said he, with a

3968	regretful pathos in his voice; “but I ain’t got none but these.”

3969	

3970	But the other boys told him the fine clothes would come fast enough,

3971	after they should have begun their adventures. They made him understand

3972	that his poor rags would do to begin with, though it was customary for

3973	wealthy pirates to start with a proper wardrobe.

3974	

3975	Gradually their talk died out and drowsiness began to steal upon the

3976	eyelids of the little waifs. The pipe dropped from the fingers of the

3977	Red-Handed, and he slept the sleep of the conscience-free and the weary.

3978	The Terror of the Seas and the Black Avenger of the Spanish Main had

3979	more difficulty in getting to sleep. They said their prayers inwardly,

3980	and lying down, since there was nobody there with authority to make them

3981	kneel and recite aloud; in truth, they had a mind not to say them at

3982	all, but they were afraid to proceed to such lengths as that, lest they

3983	might call down a sudden and special thunderbolt from heaven. Then at

3984	once they reached and hovered upon the imminent verge of sleep—but an

3985	intruder came, now, that would not “down.” It was conscience. They began

3986	to feel a vague fear that they had been doing wrong to run away; and

3987	next they thought of the stolen meat, and then the real torture came.

3988	They tried to argue it away by reminding conscience that they had

3989	purloined sweetmeats and apples scores of times; but conscience was not

3990	to be appeased by such thin plausibilities; it seemed to them, in the

3991	end, that there was no getting around the stubborn fact that taking

3992	sweetmeats was only “hooking,” while taking bacon and hams and such

3993	valuables was plain simple stealing—and there was a command against that

3994	in the Bible. So they inwardly resolved that so long as they remained in

3995	the business, their piracies should not again be sullied with the

3996	crime of stealing. Then conscience granted a truce, and these curiously

3997	inconsistent pirates fell peacefully to sleep.

3998	

3999	

4000	

4001	

4002	CHAPTER XIV

4003	

4004	

4005	WHEN Tom awoke in the morning, he wondered where he was. He sat up and

4006	rubbed his eyes and looked around. Then he comprehended. It was the cool

4007	gray dawn, and there was a delicious sense of repose and peace in the

4008	deep pervading calm and silence of the woods. Not a leaf stirred; not

4009	a sound obtruded upon great Nature’s meditation. Beaded dewdrops stood

4010	upon the leaves and grasses. A white layer of ashes covered the fire,

4011	and a thin blue breath of smoke rose straight into the air. Joe and Huck

4012	still slept.

4013	

4014	Now, far away in the woods a bird called; another answered; presently

4015	the hammering of a woodpecker was heard. Gradually the cool dim gray

4016	of the morning whitened, and as gradually sounds multiplied and life

4017	manifested itself. The marvel of Nature shaking off sleep and going

4018	to work unfolded itself to the musing boy. A little green worm came

4019	crawling over a dewy leaf, lifting two-thirds of his body into the air

4020	from time to time and “sniffing around,” then proceeding again—for he

4021	was measuring, Tom said; and when the worm approached him, of its own

4022	accord, he sat as still as a stone, with his hopes rising and falling,

4023	by turns, as the creature still came toward him or seemed inclined to

4024	go elsewhere; and when at last it considered a painful moment with its

4025	curved body in the air and then came decisively down upon Tom’s leg and

4026	began a journey over him, his whole heart was glad—for that meant that

4027	he was going to have a new suit of clothes—without the shadow of a

4028	doubt a gaudy piratical uniform. Now a procession of ants appeared,

4029	from nowhere in particular, and went about their labors; one struggled

4030	manfully by with a dead spider five times as big as itself in its arms,

4031	and lugged it straight up a tree-trunk. A brown spotted lady-bug climbed

4032	the dizzy height of a grass blade, and Tom bent down close to it and

4033	said, “Lady-bug, lady-bug, fly away home, your house is on fire, your

4034	children’s alone,” and she took wing and went off to see about it—which

4035	did not surprise the boy, for he knew of old that this insect was

4036	credulous about conflagrations, and he had practised upon its simplicity

4037	more than once. A tumblebug came next, heaving sturdily at its ball, and

4038	Tom touched the creature, to see it shut its legs against its body

4039	and pretend to be dead. The birds were fairly rioting by this time. A

4040	catbird, the Northern mocker, lit in a tree over Tom’s head, and trilled

4041	out her imitations of her neighbors in a rapture of enjoyment; then

4042	a shrill jay swept down, a flash of blue flame, and stopped on a twig

4043	almost within the boy’s reach, cocked his head to one side and eyed the

4044	strangers with a consuming curiosity; a gray squirrel and a big fellow

4045	of the “fox” kind came skurrying along, sitting up at intervals to

4046	inspect and chatter at the boys, for the wild things had probably never

4047	seen a human being before and scarcely knew whether to be afraid or not.

4048	All Nature was wide awake and stirring, now; long lances of sunlight

4049	pierced down through the dense foliage far and near, and a few

4050	butterflies came fluttering upon the scene.

4051	

4052	Tom stirred up the other pirates and they all clattered away with

4053	a shout, and in a minute or two were stripped and chasing after and

4054	tumbling over each other in the shallow limpid water of the white

4055	sandbar. They felt no longing for the little village sleeping in the

4056	distance beyond the majestic waste of water. A vagrant current or a

4057	slight rise in the river had carried off their raft, but this only

4058	gratified them, since its going was something like burning the bridge

4059	between them and civilization.

4060	

4061	They came back to camp wonderfully refreshed, glad-hearted, and

4062	ravenous; and they soon had the camp-fire blazing up again. Huck found a

4063	spring of clear cold water close by, and the boys made cups of broad oak

4064	or hickory leaves, and felt that water, sweetened with such a wildwood

4065	charm as that, would be a good enough substitute for coffee. While Joe

4066	was slicing bacon for breakfast, Tom and Huck asked him to hold on a

4067	minute; they stepped to a promising nook in the river-bank and threw in

4068	their lines; almost immediately they had reward. Joe had not had time

4069	to get impatient before they were back again with some handsome bass,

4070	a couple of sun-perch and a small catfish—provisions enough for quite a

4071	family. They fried the fish with the bacon, and were astonished; for

4072	no fish had ever seemed so delicious before. They did not know that the

4073	quicker a fresh-water fish is on the fire after he is caught the better

4074	he is; and they reflected little upon what a sauce open-air sleeping,

4075	open-air exercise, bathing, and a large ingredient of hunger make, too.

4076	

4077	They lay around in the shade, after breakfast, while Huck had a smoke,

4078	and then went off through the woods on an exploring expedition. They

4079	tramped gayly along, over decaying logs, through tangled underbrush,

4080	among solemn monarchs of the forest, hung from their crowns to the

4081	ground with a drooping regalia of grape-vines. Now and then they came

4082	upon snug nooks carpeted with grass and jeweled with flowers.

4083	

4084	They found plenty of things to be delighted with, but nothing to be

4085	astonished at. They discovered that the island was about three miles

4086	long and a quarter of a mile wide, and that the shore it lay closest to

4087	was only separated from it by a narrow channel hardly two hundred yards

4088	wide. They took a swim about every hour, so it was close upon the middle

4089	of the afternoon when they got back to camp. They were too hungry to

4090	stop to fish, but they fared sumptuously upon cold ham, and then threw

4091	themselves down in the shade to talk. But the talk soon began to drag,

4092	and then died. The stillness, the solemnity that brooded in the woods,

4093	and the sense of loneliness, began to tell upon the spirits of the boys.

4094	They fell to thinking. A sort of undefined longing crept upon them. This

4095	took dim shape, presently—it was budding homesickness. Even Finn the

4096	Red-Handed was dreaming of his doorsteps and empty hogsheads. But they

4097	were all ashamed of their weakness, and none was brave enough to speak

4098	his thought.

4099	

4100	For some time, now, the boys had been dully conscious of a peculiar

4101	sound in the distance, just as one sometimes is of the ticking of a

4102	clock which he takes no distinct note of. But now this mysterious sound

4103	became more pronounced, and forced a recognition. The boys started,

4104	glanced at each other, and then each assumed a listening attitude. There

4105	was a long silence, profound and unbroken; then a deep, sullen boom came

4106	floating down out of the distance.

4107	

4108	“What is it!” exclaimed Joe, under his breath.

4109	

4110	“I wonder,” said Tom in a whisper.

4111	

4112	“’Tain’t thunder,” said Huckleberry, in an awed tone, “becuz thunder—”

4113	

4114	“Hark!” said Tom. “Listen—don’t talk.”

4115	

4116	They waited a time that seemed an age, and then the same muffled boom

4117	troubled the solemn hush.

4118	

4119	“Let’s go and see.”

4120	

4121	They sprang to their feet and hurried to the shore toward the town. They

4122	parted the bushes on the bank and peered out over the water. The little

4123	steam ferry-boat was about a mile below the village, drifting with the

4124	current. Her broad deck seemed crowded with people. There were a great

4125	many skiffs rowing about or floating with the stream in the neighborhood

4126	of the ferryboat, but the boys could not determine what the men in

4127	them were doing. Presently a great jet of white smoke burst from the

4128	ferryboat’s side, and as it expanded and rose in a lazy cloud, that same

4129	dull throb of sound was borne to the listeners again.

4130	

4131	“I know now!” exclaimed Tom; “somebody’s drownded!”

4132	

4133	“That’s it!” said Huck; “they done that last summer, when Bill Turner

4134	got drownded; they shoot a cannon over the water, and that makes

4135	him come up to the top. Yes, and they take loaves of bread and put

4136	quicksilver in ’em and set ’em afloat, and wherever there’s anybody

4137	that’s drownded, they’ll float right there and stop.”

4138	

4139	“Yes, I’ve heard about that,” said Joe. “I wonder what makes the bread

4140	do that.”

4141	

4142	“Oh, it ain’t the bread, so much,” said Tom; “I reckon it’s mostly what

4143	they _say_ over it before they start it out.”

4144	

4145	“But they don’t say anything over it,” said Huck. “I’ve seen ’em and

4146	they don’t.”

4147	

4148	“Well, that’s funny,” said Tom. “But maybe they say it to themselves. Of

4149	_course_ they do. Anybody might know that.”

4150	

4151	The other boys agreed that there was reason in what Tom said, because

4152	an ignorant lump of bread, uninstructed by an incantation, could not

4153	be expected to act very intelligently when set upon an errand of such

4154	gravity.

4155	

4156	“By jings, I wish I was over there, now,” said Joe.

4157	

4158	“I do too” said Huck “I’d give heaps to know who it is.”

4159	

4160	The boys still listened and watched. Presently a revealing thought

4161	flashed through Tom’s mind, and he exclaimed:

4162	

4163	“Boys, I know who’s drownded—it’s us!”

4164	

4165	They felt like heroes in an instant. Here was a gorgeous triumph; they

4166	were missed; they were mourned; hearts were breaking on their account;

4167	tears were being shed; accusing memories of unkindness to these poor

4168	lost lads were rising up, and unavailing regrets and remorse were being

4169	indulged; and best of all, the departed were the talk of the whole town,

4170	and the envy of all the boys, as far as this dazzling notoriety was

4171	concerned. This was fine. It was worth while to be a pirate, after all.

4172	

4173	As twilight drew on, the ferryboat went back to her accustomed business

4174	and the skiffs disappeared. The pirates returned to camp. They were

4175	jubilant with vanity over their new grandeur and the illustrious trouble

4176	they were making. They caught fish, cooked supper and ate it, and then

4177	fell to guessing at what the village was thinking and saying about them;

4178	and the pictures they drew of the public distress on their account were

4179	gratifying to look upon—from their point of view. But when the shadows

4180	of night closed them in, they gradually ceased to talk, and sat gazing

4181	into the fire, with their minds evidently wandering elsewhere. The

4182	excitement was gone, now, and Tom and Joe could not keep back thoughts

4183	of certain persons at home who were not enjoying this fine frolic as

4184	much as they were. Misgivings came; they grew troubled and unhappy; a

4185	sigh or two escaped, unawares. By and by Joe timidly ventured upon a

4186	roundabout “feeler” as to how the others might look upon a return to

4187	civilization—not right now, but—

4188	

4189	Tom withered him with derision! Huck, being uncommitted as yet, joined

4190	in with Tom, and the waverer quickly “explained,” and was glad to get

4191	out of the scrape with as little taint of chicken-hearted home-sickness

4192	clinging to his garments as he could. Mutiny was effectually laid to

4193	rest for the moment.

4194	

4195	As the night deepened, Huck began to nod, and presently to snore.

4196	Joe followed next. Tom lay upon his elbow motionless, for some time,

4197	watching the two intently. At last he got up cautiously, on his knees,

4198	and went searching among the grass and the flickering reflections flung

4199	by the campfire. He picked up and inspected several large semi-cylinders

4200	of the thin white bark of a sycamore, and finally chose two which seemed

4201	to suit him. Then he knelt by the fire and painfully wrote something

4202	upon each of these with his “red keel”; one he rolled up and put in his

4203	jacket pocket, and the other he put in Joe’s hat and removed it to a

4204	little distance from the owner. And he also put into the hat certain

4205	schoolboy treasures of almost inestimable value—among them a lump of

4206	chalk, an India-rubber ball, three fishhooks, and one of that kind

4207	of marbles known as a “sure ’nough crystal.” Then he tiptoed his way

4208	cautiously among the trees till he felt that he was out of hearing, and

4209	straightway broke into a keen run in the direction of the sandbar.

4210	

4211	

4212	

4213	

4214	CHAPTER XV

4215	

4216	

4217	A few minutes later Tom was in the shoal water of the bar, wading toward

4218	the Illinois shore. Before the depth reached his middle he was halfway

4219	over; the current would permit no more wading, now, so he struck out

4220	confidently to swim the remaining hundred yards. He swam quartering

4221	upstream, but still was swept downward rather faster than he had

4222	expected. However, he reached the shore finally, and drifted along till

4223	he found a low place and drew himself out. He put his hand on his jacket

4224	pocket, found his piece of bark safe, and then struck through the woods,

4225	following the shore, with streaming garments. Shortly before ten

4226	o’clock he came out into an open place opposite the village, and saw the

4227	ferryboat lying in the shadow of the trees and the high bank. Everything

4228	was quiet under the blinking stars. He crept down the bank, watching

4229	with all his eyes, slipped into the water, swam three or four strokes

4230	and climbed into the skiff that did “yawl” duty at the boat’s stern. He

4231	laid himself down under the thwarts and waited, panting.

4232	

4233	Presently the cracked bell tapped and a voice gave the order to “cast

4234	off.” A minute or two later the skiff’s head was standing high up,

4235	against the boat’s swell, and the voyage was begun. Tom felt happy in

4236	his success, for he knew it was the boat’s last trip for the night. At

4237	the end of a long twelve or fifteen minutes the wheels stopped, and

4238	Tom slipped overboard and swam ashore in the dusk, landing fifty yards

4239	downstream, out of danger of possible stragglers.

4240	

4241	He flew along unfrequented alleys, and shortly found himself at his

4242	aunt’s back fence. He climbed over, approached the “ell,” and looked

4243	in at the sitting-room window, for a light was burning there. There

4244	sat Aunt Polly, Sid, Mary, and Joe Harper’s mother, grouped together,

4245	talking. They were by the bed, and the bed was between them and the

4246	door. Tom went to the door and began to softly lift the latch; then

4247	he pressed gently and the door yielded a crack; he continued pushing

4248	cautiously, and quaking every time it creaked, till he judged he might

4249	squeeze through on his knees; so he put his head through and began,

4250	warily.

4251	

4252	“What makes the candle blow so?” said Aunt Polly. Tom hurried up. “Why,

4253	that door’s open, I believe. Why, of course it is. No end of strange

4254	things now. Go ’long and shut it, Sid.”

4255	

4256	Tom disappeared under the bed just in time. He lay and “breathed”

4257	 himself for a time, and then crept to where he could almost touch his

4258	aunt’s foot.

4259	

4260	“But as I was saying,” said Aunt Polly, “he warn’t _bad_, so to say—only

4261	misch_ee_vous. Only just giddy, and harum-scarum, you know. He warn’t

4262	any more responsible than a colt. _He_ never meant any harm, and he was

4263	the best-hearted boy that ever was”—and she began to cry.

4264	

4265	“It was just so with my Joe—always full of his devilment, and up to

4266	every kind of mischief, but he was just as unselfish and kind as he

4267	could be—and laws bless me, to think I went and whipped him for taking

4268	that cream, never once recollecting that I throwed it out myself because

4269	it was sour, and I never to see him again in this world, never, never,

4270	never, poor abused boy!” And Mrs. Harper sobbed as if her heart would

4271	break.

4272	

4273	“I hope Tom’s better off where he is,” said Sid, “but if he’d been

4274	better in some ways—”

4275	

4276	“_Sid!_” Tom felt the glare of the old lady’s eye, though he could not

4277	see it. “Not a word against my Tom, now that he’s gone! God’ll take care

4278	of _him_—never you trouble _your_self, sir! Oh, Mrs. Harper, I don’t

4279	know how to give him up! I don’t know how to give him up! He was such a

4280	comfort to me, although he tormented my old heart out of me, ’most.”

4281	

4282	“The Lord giveth and the Lord hath taken away—Blessed be the name of

4283	the Lord! But it’s so hard—Oh, it’s so hard! Only last Saturday my Joe

4284	busted a firecracker right under my nose and I knocked him sprawling.

4285	Little did I know then, how soon—Oh, if it was to do over again I’d hug

4286	him and bless him for it.”

4287	

4288	“Yes, yes, yes, I know just how you feel, Mrs. Harper, I know just

4289	exactly how you feel. No longer ago than yesterday noon, my Tom took

4290	and filled the cat full of Pain-killer, and I did think the cretur would

4291	tear the house down. And God forgive me, I cracked Tom’s head with my

4292	thimble, poor boy, poor dead boy. But he’s out of all his troubles now.

4293	And the last words I ever heard him say was to reproach—”

4294	

4295	But this memory was too much for the old lady, and she broke entirely

4296	down. Tom was snuffling, now, himself—and more in pity of himself than

4297	anybody else. He could hear Mary crying, and putting in a kindly word

4298	for him from time to time. He began to have a nobler opinion of himself

4299	than ever before. Still, he was sufficiently touched by his aunt’s grief

4300	to long to rush out from under the bed and overwhelm her with joy—and

4301	the theatrical gorgeousness of the thing appealed strongly to his

4302	nature, too, but he resisted and lay still.

4303	

4304	He went on listening, and gathered by odds and ends that it was

4305	conjectured at first that the boys had got drowned while taking a swim;

4306	then the small raft had been missed; next, certain boys said the missing

4307	lads had promised that the village should “hear something” soon; the

4308	wise-heads had “put this and that together” and decided that the lads

4309	had gone off on that raft and would turn up at the next town below,

4310	presently; but toward noon the raft had been found, lodged against the

4311	Missouri shore some five or six miles below the village—and then hope

4312	perished; they must be drowned, else hunger would have driven them home

4313	by nightfall if not sooner. It was believed that the search for the

4314	bodies had been a fruitless effort merely because the drowning must

4315	have occurred in mid-channel, since the boys, being good swimmers, would

4316	otherwise have escaped to shore. This was Wednesday night. If the bodies

4317	continued missing until Sunday, all hope would be given over, and the

4318	funerals would be preached on that morning. Tom shuddered.

4319	

4320	Mrs. Harper gave a sobbing goodnight and turned to go. Then with a

4321	mutual impulse the two bereaved women flung themselves into each other’s

4322	arms and had a good, consoling cry, and then parted. Aunt Polly was

4323	tender far beyond her wont, in her goodnight to Sid and Mary. Sid

4324	snuffled a bit and Mary went off crying with all her heart.

4325	

4326	Aunt Polly knelt down and prayed for Tom so touchingly, so appealingly,

4327	and with such measureless love in her words and her old trembling voice,

4328	that he was weltering in tears again, long before she was through.

4329	

4330	He had to keep still long after she went to bed, for she kept making

4331	broken-hearted ejaculations from time to time, tossing unrestfully, and

4332	turning over. But at last she was still, only moaning a little in her

4333	sleep. Now the boy stole out, rose gradually by the bedside, shaded the

4334	candle-light with his hand, and stood regarding her. His heart was full

4335	of pity for her. He took out his sycamore scroll and placed it by the

4336	candle. But something occurred to him, and he lingered considering.

4337	His face lighted with a happy solution of his thought; he put the bark

4338	hastily in his pocket. Then he bent over and kissed the faded lips, and

4339	straightway made his stealthy exit, latching the door behind him.

4340	

4341	He threaded his way back to the ferry landing, found nobody at large

4342	there, and walked boldly on board the boat, for he knew she was

4343	tenantless except that there was a watchman, who always turned in and

4344	slept like a graven image. He untied the skiff at the stern, slipped

4345	into it, and was soon rowing cautiously upstream. When he had pulled a

4346	mile above the village, he started quartering across and bent himself

4347	stoutly to his work. He hit the landing on the other side neatly, for

4348	this was a familiar bit of work to him. He was moved to capture

4349	the skiff, arguing that it might be considered a ship and therefore

4350	legitimate prey for a pirate, but he knew a thorough search would be

4351	made for it and that might end in revelations. So he stepped ashore and

4352	entered the woods.

4353	

4354	He sat down and took a long rest, torturing himself meanwhile to keep

4355	awake, and then started warily down the home-stretch. The night was far

4356	spent. It was broad daylight before he found himself fairly abreast the

4357	island bar. He rested again until the sun was well up and gilding the

4358	great river with its splendor, and then he plunged into the stream. A

4359	little later he paused, dripping, upon the threshold of the camp, and

4360	heard Joe say:

4361	

4362	“No, Tom’s true-blue, Huck, and he’ll come back. He won’t desert. He

4363	knows that would be a disgrace to a pirate, and Tom’s too proud for that

4364	sort of thing. He’s up to something or other. Now I wonder what?”

4365	

4366	“Well, the things is ours, anyway, ain’t they?”

4367	

4368	“Pretty near, but not yet, Huck. The writing says they are if he ain’t

4369	back here to breakfast.”

4370	

4371	“Which he is!” exclaimed Tom, with fine dramatic effect, stepping

4372	grandly into camp.

4373	

4374	A sumptuous breakfast of bacon and fish was shortly provided, and as the

4375	boys set to work upon it, Tom recounted (and adorned) his adventures.

4376	They were a vain and boastful company of heroes when the tale was done.

4377	Then Tom hid himself away in a shady nook to sleep till noon, and the

4378	other pirates got ready to fish and explore.

4379	

4380	

4381	

4382	

4383	CHAPTER XVI

4384	

4385	

4386	AFTER dinner all the gang turned out to hunt for turtle eggs on the bar.

4387	They went about poking sticks into the sand, and when they found a soft

4388	place they went down on their knees and dug with their hands. Sometimes

4389	they would take fifty or sixty eggs out of one hole. They were perfectly

4390	round white things a trifle smaller than an English walnut. They had a

4391	famous fried-egg feast that night, and another on Friday morning.

4392	

4393	After breakfast they went whooping and prancing out on the bar, and

4394	chased each other round and round, shedding clothes as they went, until

4395	they were naked, and then continued the frolic far away up the shoal

4396	water of the bar, against the stiff current, which latter tripped their

4397	legs from under them from time to time and greatly increased the fun.

4398	And now and then they stooped in a group and splashed water in each

4399	other’s faces with their palms, gradually approaching each other, with

4400	averted faces to avoid the strangling sprays, and finally gripping and

4401	struggling till the best man ducked his neighbor, and then they all

4402	went under in a tangle of white legs and arms and came up blowing,

4403	sputtering, laughing, and gasping for breath at one and the same time.

4404	

4405	When they were well exhausted, they would run out and sprawl on the dry,

4406	hot sand, and lie there and cover themselves up with it, and by and by

4407	break for the water again and go through the original performance once

4408	more. Finally it occurred to them that their naked skin represented

4409	flesh-colored “tights” very fairly; so they drew a ring in the sand and

4410	had a circus—with three clowns in it, for none would yield this proudest

4411	post to his neighbor.

4412	

4413	Next they got their marbles and played “knucks” and “ringtaw” and

4414	“keeps” till that amusement grew stale. Then Joe and Huck had another

4415	swim, but Tom would not venture, because he found that in kicking off

4416	his trousers he had kicked his string of rattlesnake rattles off his

4417	ankle, and he wondered how he had escaped cramp so long without the

4418	protection of this mysterious charm. He did not venture again until he

4419	had found it, and by that time the other boys were tired and ready to

4420	rest. They gradually wandered apart, dropped into the “dumps,” and

4421	fell to gazing longingly across the wide river to where the village lay

4422	drowsing in the sun. Tom found himself writing “BECKY” in the sand with

4423	his big toe; he scratched it out, and was angry with himself for his

4424	weakness. But he wrote it again, nevertheless; he could not help it. He

4425	erased it once more and then took himself out of temptation by driving

4426	the other boys together and joining them.

4427	

4428	But Joe’s spirits had gone down almost beyond resurrection. He was so

4429	homesick that he could hardly endure the misery of it. The tears lay

4430	very near the surface. Huck was melancholy, too. Tom was downhearted,

4431	but tried hard not to show it. He had a secret which he was not ready

4432	to tell, yet, but if this mutinous depression was not broken up soon, he

4433	would have to bring it out. He said, with a great show of cheerfulness:

4434	

4435	“I bet there’s been pirates on this island before, boys. We’ll explore

4436	it again. They’ve hid treasures here somewhere. How’d you feel to light

4437	on a rotten chest full of gold and silver—hey?”

4438	

4439	But it roused only faint enthusiasm, which faded out, with no reply.

4440	Tom tried one or two other seductions; but they failed, too. It was

4441	discouraging work. Joe sat poking up the sand with a stick and looking

4442	very gloomy. Finally he said:

4443	

4444	“Oh, boys, let’s give it up. I want to go home. It’s so lonesome.”

4445	

4446	“Oh no, Joe, you’ll feel better by and by,” said Tom. “Just think of the

4447	fishing that’s here.”

4448	

4449	“I don’t care for fishing. I want to go home.”

4450	

4451	“But, Joe, there ain’t such another swimming-place anywhere.”

4452	

4453	“Swimming’s no good. I don’t seem to care for it, somehow, when there

4454	ain’t anybody to say I sha’n’t go in. I mean to go home.”

4455	

4456	“Oh, shucks! Baby! You want to see your mother, I reckon.”

4457	

4458	“Yes, I _do_ want to see my mother—and you would, too, if you had one. I

4459	ain’t any more baby than you are.” And Joe snuffled a little.

4460	

4461	“Well, we’ll let the crybaby go home to his mother, won’t we, Huck? Poor

4462	thing—does it want to see its mother? And so it shall. You like it here,

4463	don’t you, Huck? We’ll stay, won’t we?”

4464	

4465	Huck said, “Y-e-s”—without any heart in it.

4466	

4467	“I’ll never speak to you again as long as I live,” said Joe, rising.

4468	“There now!” And he moved moodily away and began to dress himself.

4469	

4470	“Who cares!” said Tom. “Nobody wants you to. Go ’long home and get

4471	laughed at. Oh, you’re a nice pirate. Huck and me ain’t crybabies. We’ll

4472	stay, won’t we, Huck? Let him go if he wants to. I reckon we can get

4473	along without him, per’aps.”

4474	

4475	But Tom was uneasy, nevertheless, and was alarmed to see Joe go sullenly

4476	on with his dressing. And then it was discomforting to see Huck eying

4477	Joe’s preparations so wistfully, and keeping up such an ominous silence.

4478	Presently, without a parting word, Joe began to wade off toward the

4479	Illinois shore. Tom’s heart began to sink. He glanced at Huck. Huck

4480	could not bear the look, and dropped his eyes. Then he said:

4481	

4482	“I want to go, too, Tom. It was getting so lonesome anyway, and now

4483	it’ll be worse. Let’s us go, too, Tom.”

4484	

4485	“I won’t! You can all go, if you want to. I mean to stay.”

4486	

4487	“Tom, I better go.”

4488	

4489	“Well, go ’long—who’s hendering you.”

4490	

4491	Huck began to pick up his scattered clothes. He said:

4492	

4493	“Tom, I wisht you’d come, too. Now you think it over. We’ll wait for you

4494	when we get to shore.”

4495	

4496	“Well, you’ll wait a blame long time, that’s all.”

4497	

4498	Huck started sorrowfully away, and Tom stood looking after him, with a

4499	strong desire tugging at his heart to yield his pride and go along

4500	too. He hoped the boys would stop, but they still waded slowly on. It

4501	suddenly dawned on Tom that it was become very lonely and still. He made

4502	one final struggle with his pride, and then darted after his comrades,

4503	yelling:

4504	

4505	“Wait! Wait! I want to tell you something!”

4506	

4507	They presently stopped and turned around. When he got to where they

4508	were, he began unfolding his secret, and they listened moodily till

4509	at last they saw the “point” he was driving at, and then they set up a

4510	warwhoop of applause and said it was “splendid!” and said if he had

4511	told them at first, they wouldn’t have started away. He made a plausible

4512	excuse; but his real reason had been the fear that not even the secret

4513	would keep them with him any very great length of time, and so he had

4514	meant to hold it in reserve as a last seduction.

4515	

4516	The lads came gayly back and went at their sports again with a will,

4517	chattering all the time about Tom’s stupendous plan and admiring the

4518	genius of it. After a dainty egg and fish dinner, Tom said he wanted to

4519	learn to smoke, now. Joe caught at the idea and said he would like to

4520	try, too. So Huck made pipes and filled them. These novices had never

4521	smoked anything before but cigars made of grapevine, and they “bit” the

4522	tongue, and were not considered manly anyway.

4523	

4524	Now they stretched themselves out on their elbows and began to puff,

4525	charily, and with slender confidence. The smoke had an unpleasant taste,

4526	and they gagged a little, but Tom said:

4527	

4528	“Why, it’s just as easy! If I’d a knowed this was all, I’d a learnt long

4529	ago.”

4530	

4531	“So would I,” said Joe. “It’s just nothing.”

4532	

4533	“Why, many a time I’ve looked at people smoking, and thought well I wish

4534	I could do that; but I never thought I could,” said Tom.

4535	

4536	“That’s just the way with me, hain’t it, Huck? You’ve heard me talk just

4537	that way—haven’t you, Huck? I’ll leave it to Huck if I haven’t.”

4538	

4539	“Yes—heaps of times,” said Huck.

4540	

4541	“Well, I have too,” said Tom; “oh, hundreds of times. Once down by the

4542	slaughter-house. Don’t you remember, Huck? Bob Tanner was there, and

4543	Johnny Miller, and Jeff Thatcher, when I said it. Don’t you remember,

4544	Huck, ’bout me saying that?”

4545	

4546	“Yes, that’s so,” said Huck. “That was the day after I lost a white

4547	alley. No, ’twas the day before.”

4548	

4549	“There—I told you so,” said Tom. “Huck recollects it.”

4550	

4551	“I bleeve I could smoke this pipe all day,” said Joe. “I don’t feel

4552	sick.”

4553	

4554	“Neither do I,” said Tom. “I could smoke it all day. But I bet you Jeff

4555	Thatcher couldn’t.”

4556	

4557	“Jeff Thatcher! Why, he’d keel over just with two draws. Just let him

4558	try it once. _He’d_ see!”

4559	

4560	“I bet he would. And Johnny Miller—I wish could see Johnny Miller tackle

4561	it once.”

4562	

4563	“Oh, don’t I!” said Joe. “Why, I bet you Johnny Miller couldn’t any more

4564	do this than nothing. Just one little snifter would fetch _him_.”

4565	

4566	“’Deed it would, Joe. Say—I wish the boys could see us now.”

4567	

4568	“So do I.”

4569	

4570	“Say—boys, don’t say anything about it, and some time when they’re

4571	around, I’ll come up to you and say, ‘Joe, got a pipe? I want a smoke.’

4572	And you’ll say, kind of careless like, as if it warn’t anything, you’ll

4573	say, ‘Yes, I got my _old_ pipe, and another one, but my tobacker ain’t

4574	very good.’ And I’ll say, ‘Oh, that’s all right, if it’s _strong_

4575	enough.’ And then you’ll out with the pipes, and we’ll light up just as

4576	ca’m, and then just see ’em look!”

4577	

4578	“By jings, that’ll be gay, Tom! I wish it was _now_!”

4579	

4580	“So do I! And when we tell ’em we learned when we was off pirating,

4581	won’t they wish they’d been along?”

4582	

4583	“Oh, I reckon not! I’ll just _bet_ they will!”

4584	

4585	So the talk ran on. But presently it began to flag a trifle, and

4586	grow disjointed. The silences widened; the expectoration marvellously

4587	increased. Every pore inside the boys’ cheeks became a spouting

4588	fountain; they could scarcely bail out the cellars under their tongues

4589	fast enough to prevent an inundation; little overflowings down their

4590	throats occurred in spite of all they could do, and sudden retchings

4591	followed every time. Both boys were looking very pale and miserable,

4592	now. Joe’s pipe dropped from his nerveless fingers. Tom’s followed. Both

4593	fountains were going furiously and both pumps bailing with might and

4594	main. Joe said feebly:

4595	

4596	“I’ve lost my knife. I reckon I better go and find it.”

4597	

4598	Tom said, with quivering lips and halting utterance:

4599	

4600	“I’ll help you. You go over that way and I’ll hunt around by the spring.

4601	No, you needn’t come, Huck—we can find it.”

4602	

4603	So Huck sat down again, and waited an hour. Then he found it lonesome,

4604	and went to find his comrades. They were wide apart in the woods, both

4605	very pale, both fast asleep. But something informed him that if they had

4606	had any trouble they had got rid of it.

4607	

4608	They were not talkative at supper that night. They had a humble look,

4609	and when Huck prepared his pipe after the meal and was going to prepare

4610	theirs, they said no, they were not feeling very well—something they ate

4611	at dinner had disagreed with them.

4612	

4613	About midnight Joe awoke, and called the boys. There was a brooding

4614	oppressiveness in the air that seemed to bode something. The boys

4615	huddled themselves together and sought the friendly companionship of

4616	the fire, though the dull dead heat of the breathless atmosphere was

4617	stifling. They sat still, intent and waiting. The solemn hush continued.

4618	Beyond the light of the fire everything was swallowed up in the

4619	blackness of darkness. Presently there came a quivering glow that

4620	vaguely revealed the foliage for a moment and then vanished. By and by

4621	another came, a little stronger. Then another. Then a faint moan came

4622	sighing through the branches of the forest and the boys felt a fleeting

4623	breath upon their cheeks, and shuddered with the fancy that the Spirit

4624	of the Night had gone by. There was a pause. Now a weird flash turned

4625	night into day and showed every little grassblade, separate and

4626	distinct, that grew about their feet. And it showed three white,

4627	startled faces, too. A deep peal of thunder went rolling and tumbling

4628	down the heavens and lost itself in sullen rumblings in the distance. A

4629	sweep of chilly air passed by, rustling all the leaves and snowing the

4630	flaky ashes broadcast about the fire. Another fierce glare lit up the

4631	forest and an instant crash followed that seemed to rend the treetops

4632	right over the boys’ heads. They clung together in terror, in the thick

4633	gloom that followed. A few big raindrops fell pattering upon the leaves.

4634	

4635	“Quick! boys, go for the tent!” exclaimed Tom.

4636	

4637	They sprang away, stumbling over roots and among vines in the dark, no

4638	two plunging in the same direction. A furious blast roared through

4639	the trees, making everything sing as it went. One blinding flash after

4640	another came, and peal on peal of deafening thunder. And now a drenching

4641	rain poured down and the rising hurricane drove it in sheets along the

4642	ground. The boys cried out to each other, but the roaring wind and the

4643	booming thunderblasts drowned their voices utterly. However, one by one

4644	they straggled in at last and took shelter under the tent, cold, scared,

4645	and streaming with water; but to have company in misery seemed something

4646	to be grateful for. They could not talk, the old sail flapped so

4647	furiously, even if the other noises would have allowed them. The tempest

4648	rose higher and higher, and presently the sail tore loose from its

4649	fastenings and went winging away on the blast. The boys seized each

4650	others’ hands and fled, with many tumblings and bruises, to the shelter

4651	of a great oak that stood upon the riverbank. Now the battle was at its

4652	highest. Under the ceaseless conflagration of lightning that flamed

4653	in the skies, everything below stood out in cleancut and shadowless

4654	distinctness: the bending trees, the billowy river, white with foam, the

4655	driving spray of spumeflakes, the dim outlines of the high bluffs on

4656	the other side, glimpsed through the drifting cloudrack and the slanting

4657	veil of rain. Every little while some giant tree yielded the fight

4658	and fell crashing through the younger growth; and the unflagging

4659	thunderpeals came now in ear-splitting explosive bursts, keen and sharp,

4660	and unspeakably appalling. The storm culminated in one matchless effort

4661	that seemed likely to tear the island to pieces, burn it up, drown it to

4662	the treetops, blow it away, and deafen every creature in it, all at one

4663	and the same moment. It was a wild night for homeless young heads to be

4664	out in.

4665	

4666	But at last the battle was done, and the forces retired with weaker and

4667	weaker threatenings and grumblings, and peace resumed her sway. The

4668	boys went back to camp, a good deal awed; but they found there was still

4669	something to be thankful for, because the great sycamore, the shelter

4670	of their beds, was a ruin, now, blasted by the lightnings, and they were

4671	not under it when the catastrophe happened.

4672	

4673	Everything in camp was drenched, the campfire as well; for they were but

4674	heedless lads, like their generation, and had made no provision against

4675	rain. Here was matter for dismay, for they were soaked through and

4676	chilled. They were eloquent in their distress; but they presently

4677	discovered that the fire had eaten so far up under the great log it had

4678	been built against (where it curved upward and separated itself from

4679	the ground), that a handbreadth or so of it had escaped wetting; so they

4680	patiently wrought until, with shreds and bark gathered from the under

4681	sides of sheltered logs, they coaxed the fire to burn again. Then they

4682	piled on great dead boughs till they had a roaring furnace, and were

4683	gladhearted once more. They dried their boiled ham and had a feast,

4684	and after that they sat by the fire and expanded and glorified their

4685	midnight adventure until morning, for there was not a dry spot to sleep

4686	on, anywhere around.

4687	

4688	As the sun began to steal in upon the boys, drowsiness came over

4689	them, and they went out on the sandbar and lay down to sleep. They got

4690	scorched out by and by, and drearily set about getting breakfast. After

4691	the meal they felt rusty, and stiff-jointed, and a little homesick once

4692	more. Tom saw the signs, and fell to cheering up the pirates as well as

4693	he could. But they cared nothing for marbles, or circus, or swimming, or

4694	anything. He reminded them of the imposing secret, and raised a ray of

4695	cheer. While it lasted, he got them interested in a new device. This was

4696	to knock off being pirates, for a while, and be Indians for a change.

4697	They were attracted by this idea; so it was not long before they were

4698	stripped, and striped from head to heel with black mud, like so many

4699	zebras—all of them chiefs, of course—and then they went tearing through

4700	the woods to attack an English settlement.

4701	

4702	By and by they separated into three hostile tribes, and darted upon each

4703	other from ambush with dreadful warwhoops, and killed and scalped each

4704	other by thousands. It was a gory day. Consequently it was an extremely

4705	satisfactory one.

4706	

4707	They assembled in camp toward suppertime, hungry and happy; but now

4708	a difficulty arose—hostile Indians could not break the bread of

4709	hospitality together without first making peace, and this was a simple

4710	impossibility without smoking a pipe of peace. There was no other

4711	process that ever they had heard of. Two of the savages almost wished

4712	they had remained pirates. However, there was no other way; so with such

4713	show of cheerfulness as they could muster they called for the pipe and

4714	took their whiff as it passed, in due form.

4715	

4716	And behold, they were glad they had gone into savagery, for they had

4717	gained something; they found that they could now smoke a little without

4718	having to go and hunt for a lost knife; they did not get sick enough to

4719	be seriously uncomfortable. They were not likely to fool away this high

4720	promise for lack of effort. No, they practised cautiously, after supper,

4721	with right fair success, and so they spent a jubilant evening. They were

4722	prouder and happier in their new acquirement than they would have been

4723	in the scalping and skinning of the Six Nations. We will leave them to

4724	smoke and chatter and brag, since we have no further use for them at

4725	present.

4726	

4727	

4728	

4729	

4730	CHAPTER XVII

4731	

4732	

4733	BUT there was no hilarity in the little town that same tranquil Saturday

4734	afternoon. The Harpers, and Aunt Polly’s family, were being put into

4735	mourning, with great grief and many tears. An unusual quiet possessed

4736	the village, although it was ordinarily quiet enough, in all conscience.

4737	The villagers conducted their concerns with an absent air, and talked

4738	little; but they sighed often. The Saturday holiday seemed a burden to

4739	the children. They had no heart in their sports, and gradually gave them

4740	up.

4741	

4742	In the afternoon Becky Thatcher found herself moping about the deserted

4743	schoolhouse yard, and feeling very melancholy. But she found nothing

4744	there to comfort her. She soliloquized:

4745	

4746	“Oh, if I only had a brass andiron-knob again! But I haven’t got

4747	anything now to remember him by.” And she choked back a little sob.

4748	

4749	Presently she stopped, and said to herself:

4750	

4751	“It was right here. Oh, if it was to do over again, I wouldn’t say

4752	that—I wouldn’t say it for the whole world. But he’s gone now; I’ll

4753	never, never, never see him any more.”

4754	

4755	This thought broke her down, and she wandered away, with tears rolling

4756	down her cheeks. Then quite a group of boys and girls—playmates of Tom’s

4757	and Joe’s—came by, and stood looking over the paling fence and talking

4758	in reverent tones of how Tom did so-and-so the last time they saw

4759	him, and how Joe said this and that small trifle (pregnant with awful

4760	prophecy, as they could easily see now!)—and each speaker pointed out

4761	the exact spot where the lost lads stood at the time, and then added

4762	something like “and I was a-standing just so—just as I am now, and as if

4763	you was him—I was as close as that—and he smiled, just this way—and then

4764	something seemed to go all over me, like—awful, you know—and I never

4765	thought what it meant, of course, but I can see now!”

4766	

4767	Then there was a dispute about who saw the dead boys last in life, and

4768	many claimed that dismal distinction, and offered evidences, more or

4769	less tampered with by the witness; and when it was ultimately decided

4770	who _did_ see the departed last, and exchanged the last words with them,

4771	the lucky parties took upon themselves a sort of sacred importance,

4772	and were gaped at and envied by all the rest. One poor chap, who had

4773	no other grandeur to offer, said with tolerably manifest pride in the

4774	remembrance:

4775	

4776	“Well, Tom Sawyer he licked me once.”

4777	

4778	But that bid for glory was a failure. Most of the boys could say that,

4779	and so that cheapened the distinction too much. The group loitered away,

4780	still recalling memories of the lost heroes, in awed voices.

4781	

4782	When the Sunday-school hour was finished, the next morning, the bell

4783	began to toll, instead of ringing in the usual way. It was a very still

4784	Sabbath, and the mournful sound seemed in keeping with the musing hush

4785	that lay upon nature. The villagers began to gather, loitering a moment

4786	in the vestibule to converse in whispers about the sad event. But there

4787	was no whispering in the house; only the funereal rustling of dresses

4788	as the women gathered to their seats disturbed the silence there. None

4789	could remember when the little church had been so full before. There

4790	was finally a waiting pause, an expectant dumbness, and then Aunt Polly

4791	entered, followed by Sid and Mary, and they by the Harper family, all in

4792	deep black, and the whole congregation, the old minister as well, rose

4793	reverently and stood until the mourners were seated in the front pew.

4794	There was another communing silence, broken at intervals by muffled

4795	sobs, and then the minister spread his hands abroad and prayed. A moving

4796	hymn was sung, and the text followed: “I am the Resurrection and the

4797	Life.”

4798	

4799	As the service proceeded, the clergyman drew such pictures of the

4800	graces, the winning ways, and the rare promise of the lost lads that

4801	every soul there, thinking he recognized these pictures, felt a pang

4802	in remembering that he had persistently blinded himself to them always

4803	before, and had as persistently seen only faults and flaws in the poor

4804	boys. The minister related many a touching incident in the lives of the

4805	departed, too, which illustrated their sweet, generous natures, and the

4806	people could easily see, now, how noble and beautiful those episodes

4807	were, and remembered with grief that at the time they occurred they had

4808	seemed rank rascalities, well deserving of the cowhide. The congregation

4809	became more and more moved, as the pathetic tale went on, till at last

4810	the whole company broke down and joined the weeping mourners in a chorus

4811	of anguished sobs, the preacher himself giving way to his feelings, and

4812	crying in the pulpit.

4813	

4814	There was a rustle in the gallery, which nobody noticed; a moment later

4815	the church door creaked; the minister raised his streaming eyes above

4816	his handkerchief, and stood transfixed! First one and then another pair

4817	of eyes followed the minister’s, and then almost with one impulse the

4818	congregation rose and stared while the three dead boys came marching up

4819	the aisle, Tom in the lead, Joe next, and Huck, a ruin of drooping rags,

4820	sneaking sheepishly in the rear! They had been hid in the unused gallery

4821	listening to their own funeral sermon!

4822	

4823	Aunt Polly, Mary, and the Harpers threw themselves upon their restored

4824	ones, smothered them with kisses and poured out thanksgivings, while

4825	poor Huck stood abashed and uncomfortable, not knowing exactly what

4826	to do or where to hide from so many unwelcoming eyes. He wavered, and

4827	started to slink away, but Tom seized him and said:

4828	

4829	“Aunt Polly, it ain’t fair. Somebody’s got to be glad to see Huck.”

4830	

4831	“And so they shall. I’m glad to see him, poor motherless thing!” And

4832	the loving attentions Aunt Polly lavished upon him were the one thing

4833	capable of making him more uncomfortable than he was before.

4834	

4835	Suddenly the minister shouted at the top of his voice: “Praise God from

4836	whom all blessings flow—_sing_!—and put your hearts in it!”

4837	

4838	And they did. Old Hundred swelled up with a triumphant burst, and

4839	while it shook the rafters Tom Sawyer the Pirate looked around upon the

4840	envying juveniles about him and confessed in his heart that this was the

4841	proudest moment of his life.

4842	

4843	As the “sold” congregation trooped out they said they would almost be

4844	willing to be made ridiculous again to hear Old Hundred sung like that

4845	once more.

4846	

4847	Tom got more cuffs and kisses that day—according to Aunt Polly’s varying

4848	moods—than he had earned before in a year; and he hardly knew which

4849	expressed the most gratefulness to God and affection for himself.

4850	

4851	

4852	

4853	

4854	CHAPTER XVIII

4855	

4856	

4857	THAT was Tom’s great secret—the scheme to return home with his brother

4858	pirates and attend their own funerals. They had paddled over to the

4859	Missouri shore on a log, at dusk on Saturday, landing five or six miles

4860	below the village; they had slept in the woods at the edge of the town

4861	till nearly daylight, and had then crept through back lanes and alleys

4862	and finished their sleep in the gallery of the church among a chaos of

4863	invalided benches.

4864	

4865	At breakfast, Monday morning, Aunt Polly and Mary were very loving to

4866	Tom, and very attentive to his wants. There was an unusual amount of

4867	talk. In the course of it Aunt Polly said:

4868	

4869	“Well, I don’t say it wasn’t a fine joke, Tom, to keep everybody

4870	suffering ’most a week so you boys had a good time, but it is a pity you

4871	could be so hard-hearted as to let me suffer so. If you could come over

4872	on a log to go to your funeral, you could have come over and give me a

4873	hint some way that you warn’t dead, but only run off.”

4874	

4875	“Yes, you could have done that, Tom,” said Mary; “and I believe you

4876	would if you had thought of it.”

4877	

4878	“Would you, Tom?” said Aunt Polly, her face lighting wistfully. “Say,

4879	now, would you, if you’d thought of it?”

4880	

4881	“I—well, I don’t know. ’Twould ’a’ spoiled everything.”

4882	

4883	“Tom, I hoped you loved me that much,” said Aunt Polly, with a grieved

4884	tone that discomforted the boy. “It would have been something if you’d

4885	cared enough to _think_ of it, even if you didn’t _do_ it.”

4886	

4887	“Now, auntie, that ain’t any harm,” pleaded Mary; “it’s only Tom’s giddy

4888	way—he is always in such a rush that he never thinks of anything.”

4889	

4890	“More’s the pity. Sid would have thought. And Sid would have come and

4891	_done_ it, too. Tom, you’ll look back, some day, when it’s too late,

4892	and wish you’d cared a little more for me when it would have cost you so

4893	little.”

4894	

4895	“Now, auntie, you know I do care for you,” said Tom.

4896	

4897	“I’d know it better if you acted more like it.”

4898	

4899	“I wish now I’d thought,” said Tom, with a repentant tone; “but I dreamt

4900	about you, anyway. That’s something, ain’t it?”

4901	

4902	“It ain’t much—a cat does that much—but it’s better than nothing. What

4903	did you dream?”

4904	

4905	“Why, Wednesday night I dreamt that you was sitting over there by the

4906	bed, and Sid was sitting by the woodbox, and Mary next to him.”

4907	

4908	“Well, so we did. So we always do. I’m glad your dreams could take even

4909	that much trouble about us.”

4910	

4911	“And I dreamt that Joe Harper’s mother was here.”

4912	

4913	“Why, she was here! Did you dream any more?”

4914	

4915	“Oh, lots. But it’s so dim, now.”

4916	

4917	“Well, try to recollect—can’t you?”

4918	

4919	“Somehow it seems to me that the wind—the wind blowed the—the—”

4920	

4921	“Try harder, Tom! The wind did blow something. Come!”

4922	

4923	Tom pressed his fingers on his forehead an anxious minute, and then

4924	said:

4925	

4926	“I’ve got it now! I’ve got it now! It blowed the candle!”

4927	

4928	“Mercy on us! Go on, Tom—go on!”

4929	

4930	“And it seems to me that you said, ‘Why, I believe that that door—’”

4931	

4932	“Go _on_, Tom!”

4933	

4934	“Just let me study a moment—just a moment. Oh, yes—you said you believed

4935	the door was open.”

4936	

4937	“As I’m sitting here, I did! Didn’t I, Mary! Go on!”

4938	

4939	“And then—and then—well I won’t be certain, but it seems like as if you

4940	made Sid go and—and—”

4941	

4942	“Well? Well? What did I make him do, Tom? What did I make him do?”

4943	

4944	“You made him—you—Oh, you made him shut it.”

4945	

4946	“Well, for the land’s sake! I never heard the beat of that in all my

4947	days! Don’t tell _me_ there ain’t anything in dreams, any more. Sereny

4948	Harper shall know of this before I’m an hour older. I’d like to see her

4949	get around _this_ with her rubbage ’bout superstition. Go on, Tom!”

4950	

4951	“Oh, it’s all getting just as bright as day, now. Next you said I warn’t

4952	_bad_, only mischeevous and harum-scarum, and not any more responsible

4953	than—than—I think it was a colt, or something.”

4954	

4955	“And so it was! Well, goodness gracious! Go on, Tom!”

4956	

4957	“And then you began to cry.”

4958	

4959	“So I did. So I did. Not the first time, neither. And then—”

4960	

4961	“Then Mrs. Harper she began to cry, and said Joe was just the same, and

4962	she wished she hadn’t whipped him for taking cream when she’d throwed it

4963	out her own self—”

4964	

4965	“Tom! The sperrit was upon you! You was a prophesying—that’s what you

4966	was doing! Land alive, go on, Tom!”

4967	

4968	“Then Sid he said—he said—”

4969	

4970	“I don’t think I said anything,” said Sid.

4971	

4972	“Yes you did, Sid,” said Mary.

4973	

4974	“Shut your heads and let Tom go on! What did he say, Tom?”

4975	

4976	“He said—I _think_ he said he hoped I was better off where I was gone

4977	to, but if I’d been better sometimes—”

4978	

4979	“_There_, d’you hear that! It was his very words!”

4980	

4981	“And you shut him up sharp.”

4982	

4983	“I lay I did! There must ’a’ been an angel there. There _was_ an angel

4984	there, somewheres!”

4985	

4986	“And Mrs. Harper told about Joe scaring her with a firecracker, and you

4987	told about Peter and the Pain-killer—”

4988	

4989	“Just as true as I live!”

4990	

4991	“And then there was a whole lot of talk ’bout dragging the river for us,

4992	and ’bout having the funeral Sunday, and then you and old Miss Harper

4993	hugged and cried, and she went.”

4994	

4995	“It happened just so! It happened just so, as sure as I’m a-sitting in

4996	these very tracks. Tom, you couldn’t told it more like if you’d ’a’ seen

4997	it! And then what? Go on, Tom!”

4998	

4999	“Then I thought you prayed for me—and I could see you and hear every

5000	word you said. And you went to bed, and I was so sorry that I took and

5001	wrote on a piece of sycamore bark, ‘We ain’t dead—we are only off being

5002	pirates,’ and put it on the table by the candle; and then you looked

5003	so good, laying there asleep, that I thought I went and leaned over and

5004	kissed you on the lips.”

5005	

5006	“Did you, Tom, _did_ you! I just forgive you everything for that!” And

5007	she seized the boy in a crushing embrace that made him feel like the

5008	guiltiest of villains.

5009	

5010	“It was very kind, even though it was only a—dream,” Sid soliloquized

5011	just audibly.

5012	

5013	“Shut up, Sid! A body does just the same in a dream as he’d do if he was

5014	awake. Here’s a big Milum apple I’ve been saving for you, Tom, if you

5015	was ever found again—now go ’long to school. I’m thankful to the good

5016	God and Father of us all I’ve got you back, that’s long-suffering and

5017	merciful to them that believe on Him and keep His word, though goodness

5018	knows I’m unworthy of it, but if only the worthy ones got His blessings

5019	and had His hand to help them over the rough places, there’s few enough

5020	would smile here or ever enter into His rest when the long night comes.

5021	Go ’long Sid, Mary, Tom—take yourselves off—you’ve hendered me long

5022	enough.”

5023	

5024	The children left for school, and the old lady to call on Mrs. Harper

5025	and vanquish her realism with Tom’s marvellous dream. Sid had better

5026	judgment than to utter the thought that was in his mind as he left the

5027	house. It was this: “Pretty thin—as long a dream as that, without any

5028	mistakes in it!”

5029	

5030	What a hero Tom was become, now! He did not go skipping and prancing,

5031	but moved with a dignified swagger as became a pirate who felt that the

5032	public eye was on him. And indeed it was; he tried not to seem to see

5033	the looks or hear the remarks as he passed along, but they were food and

5034	drink to him. Smaller boys than himself flocked at his heels, as proud

5035	to be seen with him, and tolerated by him, as if he had been the drummer

5036	at the head of a procession or the elephant leading a menagerie into

5037	town. Boys of his own size pretended not to know he had been away at

5038	all; but they were consuming with envy, nevertheless. They would have

5039	given anything to have that swarthy sun-tanned skin of his, and his

5040	glittering notoriety; and Tom would not have parted with either for a

5041	circus.

5042	

5043	At school the children made so much of him and of Joe, and delivered

5044	such eloquent admiration from their eyes, that the two heroes were

5045	not long in becoming insufferably “stuck-up.” They began to tell their

5046	adventures to hungry listeners—but they only began; it was not a

5047	thing likely to have an end, with imaginations like theirs to furnish

5048	material. And finally, when they got out their pipes and went serenely

5049	puffing around, the very summit of glory was reached.

5050	

5051	Tom decided that he could be independent of Becky Thatcher now. Glory

5052	was sufficient. He would live for glory. Now that he was distinguished,

5053	maybe she would be wanting to “make up.” Well, let her—she should see

5054	that he could be as indifferent as some other people. Presently she

5055	arrived. Tom pretended not to see her. He moved away and joined a group

5056	of boys and girls and began to talk. Soon he observed that she was

5057	tripping gayly back and forth with flushed face and dancing eyes,

5058	pretending to be busy chasing schoolmates, and screaming with laughter

5059	when she made a capture; but he noticed that she always made her
