5060	captures in his vicinity, and that she seemed to cast a conscious eye

5061	in his direction at such times, too. It gratified all the vicious vanity

5062	that was in him; and so, instead of winning him, it only “set him up”

5063	 the more and made him the more diligent to avoid betraying that he

5064	knew she was about. Presently she gave over skylarking, and moved

5065	irresolutely about, sighing once or twice and glancing furtively and

5066	wistfully toward Tom. Then she observed that now Tom was talking more

5067	particularly to Amy Lawrence than to any one else. She felt a sharp pang

5068	and grew disturbed and uneasy at once. She tried to go away, but her

5069	feet were treacherous, and carried her to the group instead. She said to

5070	a girl almost at Tom’s elbow—with sham vivacity:

5071	

5072	“Why, Mary Austin! you bad girl, why didn’t you come to Sunday-school?”

5073	

5074	“I did come—didn’t you see me?”

5075	

5076	“Why, no! Did you? Where did you sit?”

5077	

5078	“I was in Miss Peters’ class, where I always go. I saw _you_.”

5079	

5080	“Did you? Why, it’s funny I didn’t see you. I wanted to tell you about

5081	the picnic.”

5082	

5083	“Oh, that’s jolly. Who’s going to give it?”

5084	

5085	“My ma’s going to let me have one.”

5086	

5087	“Oh, goody; I hope she’ll let _me_ come.”

5088	

5089	“Well, she will. The picnic’s for me. She’ll let anybody come that I

5090	want, and I want you.”

5091	

5092	“That’s ever so nice. When is it going to be?”

5093	

5094	“By and by. Maybe about vacation.”

5095	

5096	“Oh, won’t it be fun! You going to have all the girls and boys?”

5097	

5098	“Yes, every one that’s friends to me—or wants to be”; and she glanced

5099	ever so furtively at Tom, but he talked right along to Amy Lawrence

5100	about the terrible storm on the island, and how the lightning tore the

5101	great sycamore tree “all to flinders” while he was “standing within

5102	three feet of it.”

5103	

5104	“Oh, may I come?” said Grace Miller.

5105	

5106	“Yes.”

5107	

5108	“And me?” said Sally Rogers.

5109	

5110	“Yes.”

5111	

5112	“And me, too?” said Susy Harper. “And Joe?”

5113	

5114	“Yes.”

5115	

5116	And so on, with clapping of joyful hands till all the group had begged

5117	for invitations but Tom and Amy. Then Tom turned coolly away, still

5118	talking, and took Amy with him. Becky’s lips trembled and the tears

5119	came to her eyes; she hid these signs with a forced gayety and went on

5120	chattering, but the life had gone out of the picnic, now, and out of

5121	everything else; she got away as soon as she could and hid herself and

5122	had what her sex call “a good cry.” Then she sat moody, with wounded

5123	pride, till the bell rang. She roused up, now, with a vindictive cast

5124	in her eye, and gave her plaited tails a shake and said she knew what

5125	_she’d_ do.

5126	

5127	At recess Tom continued his flirtation with Amy with jubilant

5128	self-satisfaction. And he kept drifting about to find Becky and lacerate

5129	her with the performance. At last he spied her, but there was a sudden

5130	falling of his mercury. She was sitting cosily on a little bench behind

5131	the schoolhouse looking at a picture-book with Alfred Temple—and so

5132	absorbed were they, and their heads so close together over the book,

5133	that they did not seem to be conscious of anything in the world besides.

5134	Jealousy ran red-hot through Tom’s veins. He began to hate himself for

5135	throwing away the chance Becky had offered for a reconciliation. He

5136	called himself a fool, and all the hard names he could think of. He

5137	wanted to cry with vexation. Amy chatted happily along, as they walked,

5138	for her heart was singing, but Tom’s tongue had lost its function. He

5139	did not hear what Amy was saying, and whenever she paused expectantly

5140	he could only stammer an awkward assent, which was as often misplaced

5141	as otherwise. He kept drifting to the rear of the schoolhouse, again and

5142	again, to sear his eyeballs with the hateful spectacle there. He could

5143	not help it. And it maddened him to see, as he thought he saw, that

5144	Becky Thatcher never once suspected that he was even in the land of the

5145	living. But she did see, nevertheless; and she knew she was winning her

5146	fight, too, and was glad to see him suffer as she had suffered.

5147	

5148	Amy’s happy prattle became intolerable. Tom hinted at things he had

5149	to attend to; things that must be done; and time was fleeting. But in

5150	vain—the girl chirped on. Tom thought, “Oh, hang her, ain’t I ever going

5151	to get rid of her?” At last he must be attending to those things—and she

5152	said artlessly that she would be “around” when school let out. And he

5153	hastened away, hating her for it.

5154	

5155	“Any other boy!” Tom thought, grating his teeth. “Any boy in the whole

5156	town but that Saint Louis smarty that thinks he dresses so fine and is

5157	aristocracy! Oh, all right, I licked you the first day you ever saw this

5158	town, mister, and I’ll lick you again! You just wait till I catch you

5159	out! I’ll just take and—”

5160	

5161	And he went through the motions of thrashing an imaginary boy—pummelling

5162	the air, and kicking and gouging. “Oh, you do, do you? You holler

5163	’nough, do you? Now, then, let that learn you!” And so the imaginary

5164	flogging was finished to his satisfaction.

5165	

5166	Tom fled home at noon. His conscience could not endure any more of Amy’s

5167	grateful happiness, and his jealousy could bear no more of the other

5168	distress. Becky resumed her picture inspections with Alfred, but as the

5169	minutes dragged along and no Tom came to suffer, her triumph began to

5170	cloud and she lost interest; gravity and absentmindedness followed,

5171	and then melancholy; two or three times she pricked up her ear at

5172	a footstep, but it was a false hope; no Tom came. At last she grew

5173	entirely miserable and wished she hadn’t carried it so far. When

5174	poor Alfred, seeing that he was losing her, he did not know how, kept

5175	exclaiming: “Oh, here’s a jolly one! look at this!” she lost patience at

5176	last, and said, “Oh, don’t bother me! I don’t care for them!” and burst

5177	into tears, and got up and walked away.

5178	

5179	Alfred dropped alongside and was going to try to comfort her, but she

5180	said:

5181	

5182	“Go away and leave me alone, can’t you! I hate you!”

5183	

5184	So the boy halted, wondering what he could have done—for she had said

5185	she would look at pictures all through the nooning—and she walked on,

5186	crying. Then Alfred went musing into the deserted schoolhouse. He was

5187	humiliated and angry. He easily guessed his way to the truth—the girl

5188	had simply made a convenience of him to vent her spite upon Tom Sawyer.

5189	He was far from hating Tom the less when this thought occurred to him.

5190	He wished there was some way to get that boy into trouble without much

5191	risk to himself. Tom’s spelling-book fell under his eye. Here was his

5192	opportunity. He gratefully opened to the lesson for the afternoon and

5193	poured ink upon the page.

5194	

5195	Becky, glancing in at a window behind him at the moment, saw the act,

5196	and moved on, without discovering herself. She started homeward, now,

5197	intending to find Tom and tell him; Tom would be thankful and their

5198	troubles would be healed. Before she was half way home, however, she

5199	had changed her mind. The thought of Tom’s treatment of her when she was

5200	talking about her picnic came scorching back and filled her with shame.

5201	She resolved to let him get whipped on the damaged spelling-book’s

5202	account, and to hate him forever, into the bargain.

5203	

5204	

5205	

5206	

5207	CHAPTER XIX

5208	

5209	

5210	TOM arrived at home in a dreary mood, and the first thing his aunt said

5211	to him showed him that he had brought his sorrows to an unpromising

5212	market:

5213	

5214	“Tom, I’ve a notion to skin you alive!”

5215	

5216	“Auntie, what have I done?”

5217	

5218	“Well, you’ve done enough. Here I go over to Sereny Harper, like an old

5219	softy, expecting I’m going to make her believe all that rubbage about

5220	that dream, when lo and behold you she’d found out from Joe that you was

5221	over here and heard all the talk we had that night. Tom, I don’t know

5222	what is to become of a boy that will act like that. It makes me feel so

5223	bad to think you could let me go to Sereny Harper and make such a fool

5224	of myself and never say a word.”

5225	

5226	This was a new aspect of the thing. His smartness of the morning had

5227	seemed to Tom a good joke before, and very ingenious. It merely looked

5228	mean and shabby now. He hung his head and could not think of anything to

5229	say for a moment. Then he said:

5230	

5231	“Auntie, I wish I hadn’t done it—but I didn’t think.”

5232	

5233	“Oh, child, you never think. You never think of anything but your

5234	own selfishness. You could think to come all the way over here from

5235	Jackson’s Island in the night to laugh at our troubles, and you could

5236	think to fool me with a lie about a dream; but you couldn’t ever think

5237	to pity us and save us from sorrow.”

5238	

5239	“Auntie, I know now it was mean, but I didn’t mean to be mean. I didn’t,

5240	honest. And besides, I didn’t come over here to laugh at you that

5241	night.”

5242	

5243	“What did you come for, then?”

5244	

5245	“It was to tell you not to be uneasy about us, because we hadn’t got

5246	drownded.”

5247	

5248	“Tom, Tom, I would be the thankfullest soul in this world if I could

5249	believe you ever had as good a thought as that, but you know you never

5250	did—and I know it, Tom.”

5251	

5252	“Indeed and ’deed I did, auntie—I wish I may never stir if I didn’t.”

5253	

5254	“Oh, Tom, don’t lie—don’t do it. It only makes things a hundred times

5255	worse.”

5256	

5257	“It ain’t a lie, auntie; it’s the truth. I wanted to keep you from

5258	grieving—that was all that made me come.”

5259	

5260	“I’d give the whole world to believe that—it would cover up a power

5261	of sins, Tom. I’d ’most be glad you’d run off and acted so bad. But it

5262	ain’t reasonable; because, why didn’t you tell me, child?”

5263	

5264	“Why, you see, when you got to talking about the funeral, I just got all

5265	full of the idea of our coming and hiding in the church, and I couldn’t

5266	somehow bear to spoil it. So I just put the bark back in my pocket and

5267	kept mum.”

5268	

5269	“What bark?”

5270	

5271	“The bark I had wrote on to tell you we’d gone pirating. I wish, now,

5272	you’d waked up when I kissed you—I do, honest.”

5273	

5274	The hard lines in his aunt’s face relaxed and a sudden tenderness dawned

5275	in her eyes.

5276	

5277	“_Did_ you kiss me, Tom?”

5278	

5279	“Why, yes, I did.”

5280	

5281	“Are you sure you did, Tom?”

5282	

5283	“Why, yes, I did, auntie—certain sure.”

5284	

5285	“What did you kiss me for, Tom?”

5286	

5287	“Because I loved you so, and you laid there moaning and I was so sorry.”

5288	

5289	The words sounded like truth. The old lady could not hide a tremor in

5290	her voice when she said:

5291	

5292	“Kiss me again, Tom!—and be off with you to school, now, and don’t

5293	bother me any more.”

5294	

5295	The moment he was gone, she ran to a closet and got out the ruin of a

5296	jacket which Tom had gone pirating in. Then she stopped, with it in her

5297	hand, and said to herself:

5298	

5299	“No, I don’t dare. Poor boy, I reckon he’s lied about it—but it’s a

5300	blessed, blessed lie, there’s such a comfort come from it. I hope

5301	the Lord—I _know_ the Lord will forgive him, because it was such

5302	good-heartedness in him to tell it. But I don’t want to find out it’s a

5303	lie. I won’t look.”

5304	

5305	She put the jacket away, and stood by musing a minute. Twice she put out

5306	her hand to take the garment again, and twice she refrained. Once more

5307	she ventured, and this time she fortified herself with the thought:

5308	“It’s a good lie—it’s a good lie—I won’t let it grieve me.” So she

5309	sought the jacket pocket. A moment later she was reading Tom’s piece of

5310	bark through flowing tears and saying: “I could forgive the boy, now, if

5311	he’d committed a million sins!”

5312	

5313	

5314	

5315	

5316	CHAPTER XX

5317	

5318	

5319	THERE was something about Aunt Polly’s manner, when she kissed Tom, that

5320	swept away his low spirits and made him lighthearted and happy again. He

5321	started to school and had the luck of coming upon Becky Thatcher at the

5322	head of Meadow Lane. His mood always determined his manner. Without a

5323	moment’s hesitation he ran to her and said:

5324	

5325	“I acted mighty mean today, Becky, and I’m so sorry. I won’t ever, ever

5326	do that way again, as long as ever I live—please make up, won’t you?”

5327	

5328	The girl stopped and looked him scornfully in the face:

5329	

5330	“I’ll thank you to keep yourself _to_ yourself, Mr. Thomas Sawyer. I’ll

5331	never speak to you again.”

5332	

5333	She tossed her head and passed on. Tom was so stunned that he had not

5334	even presence of mind enough to say “Who cares, Miss Smarty?” until the

5335	right time to say it had gone by. So he said nothing. But he was in a

5336	fine rage, nevertheless. He moped into the schoolyard wishing she were

5337	a boy, and imagining how he would trounce her if she were. He presently

5338	encountered her and delivered a stinging remark as he passed. She hurled

5339	one in return, and the angry breach was complete. It seemed to Becky, in

5340	her hot resentment, that she could hardly wait for school to “take in,”

5341	 she was so impatient to see Tom flogged for the injured spelling-book.

5342	If she had had any lingering notion of exposing Alfred Temple, Tom’s

5343	offensive fling had driven it entirely away.

5344	

5345	Poor girl, she did not know how fast she was nearing trouble herself.

5346	The master, Mr. Dobbins, had reached middle age with an unsatisfied

5347	ambition. The darling of his desires was, to be a doctor, but

5348	poverty had decreed that he should be nothing higher than a village

5349	schoolmaster. Every day he took a mysterious book out of his desk and

5350	absorbed himself in it at times when no classes were reciting. He kept

5351	that book under lock and key. There was not an urchin in school but was

5352	perishing to have a glimpse of it, but the chance never came. Every boy

5353	and girl had a theory about the nature of that book; but no two theories

5354	were alike, and there was no way of getting at the facts in the case.

5355	Now, as Becky was passing by the desk, which stood near the door, she

5356	noticed that the key was in the lock! It was a precious moment. She

5357	glanced around; found herself alone, and the next instant she had the

5358	book in her hands. The titlepage—Professor Somebody’s _Anatomy_—carried

5359	no information to her mind; so she began to turn the leaves. She came at

5360	once upon a handsomely engraved and colored frontispiece—a human figure,

5361	stark naked. At that moment a shadow fell on the page and Tom Sawyer

5362	stepped in at the door and caught a glimpse of the picture. Becky

5363	snatched at the book to close it, and had the hard luck to tear the

5364	pictured page half down the middle. She thrust the volume into the desk,

5365	turned the key, and burst out crying with shame and vexation.

5366	

5367	“Tom Sawyer, you are just as mean as you can be, to sneak up on a person

5368	and look at what they’re looking at.”

5369	

5370	“How could I know you was looking at anything?”

5371	

5372	“You ought to be ashamed of yourself, Tom Sawyer; you know you’re

5373	going to tell on me, and oh, what shall I do, what shall I do! I’ll be

5374	whipped, and I never was whipped in school.”

5375	

5376	Then she stamped her little foot and said:

5377	

5378	“_Be_ so mean if you want to! I know something that’s going to happen.

5379	You just wait and you’ll see! Hateful, hateful, hateful!”—and she flung

5380	out of the house with a new explosion of crying.

5381	

5382	Tom stood still, rather flustered by this onslaught. Presently he said

5383	to himself:

5384	

5385	“What a curious kind of a fool a girl is! Never been licked in

5386	school! Shucks! What’s a licking! That’s just like a girl—they’re so

5387	thin-skinned and chicken-hearted. Well, of course I ain’t going to tell

5388	old Dobbins on this little fool, because there’s other ways of getting

5389	even on her, that ain’t so mean; but what of it? Old Dobbins will ask

5390	who it was tore his book. Nobody’ll answer. Then he’ll do just the way

5391	he always does—ask first one and then t’other, and when he comes to the

5392	right girl he’ll know it, without any telling. Girls’ faces always tell

5393	on them. They ain’t got any backbone. She’ll get licked. Well, it’s a

5394	kind of a tight place for Becky Thatcher, because there ain’t any way

5395	out of it.” Tom conned the thing a moment longer, and then added: “All

5396	right, though; she’d like to see me in just such a fix—let her sweat it

5397	out!”

5398	

5399	Tom joined the mob of skylarking scholars outside. In a few moments the

5400	master arrived and school “took in.” Tom did not feel a strong interest

5401	in his studies. Every time he stole a glance at the girls’ side of the

5402	room Becky’s face troubled him. Considering all things, he did not want

5403	to pity her, and yet it was all he could do to help it. He could get

5404	up no exultation that was really worthy the name. Presently the

5405	spelling-book discovery was made, and Tom’s mind was entirely full

5406	of his own matters for a while after that. Becky roused up from her

5407	lethargy of distress and showed good interest in the proceedings. She

5408	did not expect that Tom could get out of his trouble by denying that he

5409	spilt the ink on the book himself; and she was right. The denial only

5410	seemed to make the thing worse for Tom. Becky supposed she would be glad

5411	of that, and she tried to believe she was glad of it, but she found she

5412	was not certain. When the worst came to the worst, she had an impulse

5413	to get up and tell on Alfred Temple, but she made an effort and forced

5414	herself to keep still—because, said she to herself, “he’ll tell about me

5415	tearing the picture sure. I wouldn’t say a word, not to save his life!”

5416	

5417	Tom took his whipping and went back to his seat not at all

5418	broken-hearted, for he thought it was possible that he had unknowingly

5419	upset the ink on the spelling-book himself, in some skylarking bout—he

5420	had denied it for form’s sake and because it was custom, and had stuck

5421	to the denial from principle.

5422	

5423	A whole hour drifted by, the master sat nodding in his throne, the air

5424	was drowsy with the hum of study. By and by, Mr. Dobbins straightened

5425	himself up, yawned, then unlocked his desk, and reached for his book,

5426	but seemed undecided whether to take it out or leave it. Most of the

5427	pupils glanced up languidly, but there were two among them that watched

5428	his movements with intent eyes. Mr. Dobbins fingered his book absently

5429	for a while, then took it out and settled himself in his chair to read!

5430	Tom shot a glance at Becky. He had seen a hunted and helpless rabbit

5431	look as she did, with a gun levelled at its head. Instantly he forgot

5432	his quarrel with her. Quick—something must be done! done in a flash,

5433	too! But the very imminence of the emergency paralyzed his invention.

5434	Good!—he had an inspiration! He would run and snatch the book, spring

5435	through the door and fly. But his resolution shook for one little

5436	instant, and the chance was lost—the master opened the volume. If Tom

5437	only had the wasted opportunity back again! Too late. There was no help

5438	for Becky now, he said. The next moment the master faced the school.

5439	Every eye sank under his gaze. There was that in it which smote even

5440	the innocent with fear. There was silence while one might count ten—the

5441	master was gathering his wrath. Then he spoke: “Who tore this book?”

5442	

5443	There was not a sound. One could have heard a pin drop. The stillness

5444	continued; the master searched face after face for signs of guilt.

5445	

5446	“Benjamin Rogers, did you tear this book?”

5447	

5448	A denial. Another pause.

5449	

5450	“Joseph Harper, did you?”

5451	

5452	Another denial. Tom’s uneasiness grew more and more intense under the

5453	slow torture of these proceedings. The master scanned the ranks of

5454	boys—considered a while, then turned to the girls:

5455	

5456	“Amy Lawrence?”

5457	

5458	A shake of the head.

5459	

5460	“Gracie Miller?”

5461	

5462	The same sign.

5463	

5464	“Susan Harper, did you do this?”

5465	

5466	Another negative. The next girl was Becky Thatcher. Tom was trembling

5467	from head to foot with excitement and a sense of the hopelessness of the

5468	situation.

5469	

5470	“Rebecca Thatcher” [Tom glanced at her face—it was white with

5471	terror]—“did you tear—no, look me in the face” [her hands rose in

5472	appeal]—“did you tear this book?”

5473	

5474	A thought shot like lightning through Tom’s brain. He sprang to his feet

5475	and shouted—“I done it!”

5476	

5477	The school stared in perplexity at this incredible folly. Tom stood a

5478	moment, to gather his dismembered faculties; and when he stepped forward

5479	to go to his punishment the surprise, the gratitude, the adoration that

5480	shone upon him out of poor Becky’s eyes seemed pay enough for a hundred

5481	floggings. Inspired by the splendor of his own act, he took without

5482	an outcry the most merciless flaying that even Mr. Dobbins had ever

5483	administered; and also received with indifference the added cruelty of a

5484	command to remain two hours after school should be dismissed—for he

5485	knew who would wait for him outside till his captivity was done, and not

5486	count the tedious time as loss, either.

5487	

5488	Tom went to bed that night planning vengeance against Alfred Temple; for

5489	with shame and repentance Becky had told him all, not forgetting her own

5490	treachery; but even the longing for vengeance had to give way, soon, to

5491	pleasanter musings, and he fell asleep at last with Becky’s latest words

5492	lingering dreamily in his ear—

5493	

5494	“Tom, how _could_ you be so noble!”

5495	

5496	

5497	

5498	

5499	CHAPTER XXI

5500	

5501	

5502	VACATION was approaching. The schoolmaster, always severe, grew severer

5503	and more exacting than ever, for he wanted the school to make a good

5504	showing on “Examination” day. His rod and his ferule were seldom idle

5505	now—at least among the smaller pupils. Only the biggest boys, and young

5506	ladies of eighteen and twenty, escaped lashing. Mr. Dobbins’ lashings

5507	were very vigorous ones, too; for although he carried, under his wig, a

5508	perfectly bald and shiny head, he had only reached middle age, and there

5509	was no sign of feebleness in his muscle. As the great day approached,

5510	all the tyranny that was in him came to the surface; he seemed to take a

5511	vindictive pleasure in punishing the least shortcomings. The consequence

5512	was, that the smaller boys spent their days in terror and suffering and

5513	their nights in plotting revenge. They threw away no opportunity to do

5514	the master a mischief. But he kept ahead all the time. The retribution

5515	that followed every vengeful success was so sweeping and majestic that

5516	the boys always retired from the field badly worsted. At last they

5517	conspired together and hit upon a plan that promised a dazzling victory.

5518	They swore in the signpainter’s boy, told him the scheme, and asked his

5519	help. He had his own reasons for being delighted, for the master boarded

5520	in his father’s family and had given the boy ample cause to hate him.

5521	The master’s wife would go on a visit to the country in a few days, and

5522	there would be nothing to interfere with the plan; the master always

5523	prepared himself for great occasions by getting pretty well fuddled, and

5524	the signpainter’s boy said that when the dominie had reached the proper

5525	condition on Examination Evening he would “manage the thing” while he

5526	napped in his chair; then he would have him awakened at the right time

5527	and hurried away to school.

5528	

5529	In the fulness of time the interesting occasion arrived. At eight in

5530	the evening the schoolhouse was brilliantly lighted, and adorned with

5531	wreaths and festoons of foliage and flowers. The master sat throned in

5532	his great chair upon a raised platform, with his blackboard behind him.

5533	He was looking tolerably mellow. Three rows of benches on each side and

5534	six rows in front of him were occupied by the dignitaries of the town

5535	and by the parents of the pupils. To his left, back of the rows of

5536	citizens, was a spacious temporary platform upon which were seated the

5537	scholars who were to take part in the exercises of the evening; rows of

5538	small boys, washed and dressed to an intolerable state of discomfort;

5539	rows of gawky big boys; snowbanks of girls and young ladies clad in

5540	lawn and muslin and conspicuously conscious of their bare arms, their

5541	grandmothers’ ancient trinkets, their bits of pink and blue ribbon and

5542	the flowers in their hair. All the rest of the house was filled with

5543	non-participating scholars.

5544	

5545	The exercises began. A very little boy stood up and sheepishly recited,

5546	“You’d scarce expect one of my age to speak in public on the stage,”

5547	 etc.—accompanying himself with the painfully exact and spasmodic

5548	gestures which a machine might have used—supposing the machine to be a

5549	trifle out of order. But he got through safely, though cruelly scared,

5550	and got a fine round of applause when he made his manufactured bow and

5551	retired.

5552	

5553	A little shamefaced girl lisped, “Mary had a little lamb,” etc.,

5554	performed a compassion-inspiring curtsy, got her meed of applause, and

5555	sat down flushed and happy.

5556	

5557	Tom Sawyer stepped forward with conceited confidence and soared into

5558	the unquenchable and indestructible “Give me liberty or give me death”

5559	 speech, with fine fury and frantic gesticulation, and broke down in the

5560	middle of it. A ghastly stage-fright seized him, his legs quaked under

5561	him and he was like to choke. True, he had the manifest sympathy of the

5562	house but he had the house’s silence, too, which was even worse than

5563	its sympathy. The master frowned, and this completed the disaster. Tom

5564	struggled awhile and then retired, utterly defeated. There was a weak

5565	attempt at applause, but it died early.

5566	

5567	“The Boy Stood on the Burning Deck” followed; also “The Assyrian Came

5568	Down,” and other declamatory gems. Then there were reading exercises,

5569	and a spelling fight. The meagre Latin class recited with honor. The

5570	prime feature of the evening was in order, now—original “compositions”

5571	 by the young ladies. Each in her turn stepped forward to the edge of the

5572	platform, cleared her throat, held up her manuscript (tied with dainty

5573	ribbon), and proceeded to read, with labored attention to “expression”

5574	 and punctuation. The themes were the same that had been illuminated upon

5575	similar occasions by their mothers before them, their grandmothers,

5576	and doubtless all their ancestors in the female line clear back to the

5577	Crusades. “Friendship” was one; “Memories of Other Days”; “Religion in

5578	History”; “Dream Land”; “The Advantages of Culture”; “Forms of Political

5579	Government Compared and Contrasted”; “Melancholy”; “Filial Love”; “Heart

5580	Longings,” etc., etc.

5581	

5582	A prevalent feature in these compositions was a nursed and petted

5583	melancholy; another was a wasteful and opulent gush of “fine language”;

5584	another was a tendency to lug in by the ears particularly prized words

5585	and phrases until they were worn entirely out; and a peculiarity that

5586	conspicuously marked and marred them was the inveterate and intolerable

5587	sermon that wagged its crippled tail at the end of each and every one

5588	of them. No matter what the subject might be, a brainracking effort was

5589	made to squirm it into some aspect or other that the moral and religious

5590	mind could contemplate with edification. The glaring insincerity of

5591	these sermons was not sufficient to compass the banishment of the

5592	fashion from the schools, and it is not sufficient today; it never will

5593	be sufficient while the world stands, perhaps. There is no school in

5594	all our land where the young ladies do not feel obliged to close their

5595	compositions with a sermon; and you will find that the sermon of the

5596	most frivolous and the least religious girl in the school is always

5597	the longest and the most relentlessly pious. But enough of this. Homely

5598	truth is unpalatable.

5599	

5600	Let us return to the “Examination.” The first composition that was read

5601	was one entitled “Is this, then, Life?” Perhaps the reader can endure an

5602	extract from it:

5603	

5604	“In the common walks of life, with what delightful emotions does the

5605	youthful mind look forward to some anticipated scene of festivity!

5606	Imagination is busy sketching rose-tinted pictures of joy. In fancy, the

5607	voluptuous votary of fashion sees herself amid the festive throng, ‘the

5608	observed of all observers.’ Her graceful form, arrayed in snowy robes,

5609	is whirling through the mazes of the joyous dance; her eye is brightest,

5610	her step is lightest in the gay assembly.

5611	

5612	“In such delicious fancies time quickly glides by, and the welcome hour

5613	arrives for her entrance into the Elysian world, of which she has

5614	had such bright dreams. How fairy-like does everything appear to her

5615	enchanted vision! Each new scene is more charming than the last. But

5616	after a while she finds that beneath this goodly exterior, all is

5617	vanity, the flattery which once charmed her soul, now grates harshly

5618	upon her ear; the ballroom has lost its charms; and with wasted health

5619	and imbittered heart, she turns away with the conviction that earthly

5620	pleasures cannot satisfy the longings of the soul!”

5621	

5622	And so forth and so on. There was a buzz of gratification from time to

5623	time during the reading, accompanied by whispered ejaculations of “How

5624	sweet!” “How eloquent!” “So true!” etc., and after the thing had closed

5625	with a peculiarly afflicting sermon the applause was enthusiastic.

5626	

5627	Then arose a slim, melancholy girl, whose face had the “interesting”

5628	 paleness that comes of pills and indigestion, and read a “poem.” Two

5629	stanzas of it will do:

5630	

5631	“A MISSOURI MAIDEN’S FAREWELL TO ALABAMA

5632	

5633	“Alabama, goodbye! I love thee well! But yet for a while do I leave thee

5634	now! Sad, yes, sad thoughts of thee my heart doth swell, And burning

5635	recollections throng my brow! For I have wandered through thy flowery

5636	woods; Have roamed and read near Tallapoosa’s stream; Have listened to

5637	Tallassee’s warring floods, And wooed on Coosa’s side Aurora’s beam.

5638	

5639	“Yet shame I not to bear an o’erfull heart, Nor blush to turn behind

5640	my tearful eyes; ’Tis from no stranger land I now must part, ’Tis to no

5641	strangers left I yield these sighs. Welcome and home were mine within

5642	this State, Whose vales I leave—whose spires fade fast from me And cold

5643	must be mine eyes, and heart, and tete, When, dear Alabama! they turn

5644	cold on thee!” There were very few there who knew what “tete” meant, but

5645	the poem was very satisfactory, nevertheless.

5646	

5647	Next appeared a dark-complexioned, black-eyed, black-haired young lady,

5648	who paused an impressive moment, assumed a tragic expression, and began

5649	to read in a measured, solemn tone:

5650	

5651	“A VISION

5652	

5653	“Dark and tempestuous was night. Around the throne on high not a single

5654	star quivered; but the deep intonations of the heavy thunder constantly

5655	vibrated upon the ear; whilst the terrific lightning revelled in angry

5656	mood through the cloudy chambers of heaven, seeming to scorn the power

5657	exerted over its terror by the illustrious Franklin! Even the boisterous

5658	winds unanimously came forth from their mystic homes, and blustered

5659	about as if to enhance by their aid the wildness of the scene.

5660	

5661	“At such a time, so dark, so dreary, for human sympathy my very spirit

5662	sighed; but instead thereof,

5663	

5664	“‘My dearest friend, my counsellor, my comforter and guide—My joy in

5665	grief, my second bliss in joy,’ came to my side. She moved like one of

5666	those bright beings pictured in the sunny walks of fancy’s Eden by

5667	the romantic and young, a queen of beauty unadorned save by her own

5668	transcendent loveliness. So soft was her step, it failed to make even a

5669	sound, and but for the magical thrill imparted by her genial touch,

5670	as other unobtrusive beauties, she would have glided away

5671	unperceived—unsought. A strange sadness rested upon her features, like

5672	icy tears upon the robe of December, as she pointed to the contending

5673	elements without, and bade me contemplate the two beings presented.”

5674	

5675	This nightmare occupied some ten pages of manuscript and wound up with a

5676	sermon so destructive of all hope to non-Presbyterians that it took

5677	the first prize. This composition was considered to be the very finest

5678	effort of the evening. The mayor of the village, in delivering the prize

5679	to the author of it, made a warm speech in which he said that it was by

5680	far the most “eloquent” thing he had ever listened to, and that Daniel

5681	Webster himself might well be proud of it.

5682	

5683	It may be remarked, in passing, that the number of compositions in which

5684	the word “beauteous” was over-fondled, and human experience referred to

5685	as “life’s page,” was up to the usual average.

5686	

5687	Now the master, mellow almost to the verge of geniality, put his chair

5688	aside, turned his back to the audience, and began to draw a map of

5689	America on the blackboard, to exercise the geography class upon. But he

5690	made a sad business of it with his unsteady hand, and a smothered titter

5691	rippled over the house. He knew what the matter was, and set himself to

5692	right it. He sponged out lines and remade them; but he only distorted

5693	them more than ever, and the tittering was more pronounced. He threw his

5694	entire attention upon his work, now, as if determined not to be put down

5695	by the mirth. He felt that all eyes were fastened upon him; he imagined

5696	he was succeeding, and yet the tittering continued; it even manifestly

5697	increased. And well it might. There was a garret above, pierced with

5698	a scuttle over his head; and down through this scuttle came a cat,

5699	suspended around the haunches by a string; she had a rag tied about

5700	her head and jaws to keep her from mewing; as she slowly descended she

5701	curved upward and clawed at the string, she swung downward and clawed

5702	at the intangible air. The tittering rose higher and higher—the cat was

5703	within six inches of the absorbed teacher’s head—down, down, a little

5704	lower, and she grabbed his wig with her desperate claws, clung to it,

5705	and was snatched up into the garret in an instant with her trophy still

5706	in her possession! And how the light did blaze abroad from the master’s

5707	bald pate—for the signpainter’s boy had _gilded_ it!

5708	

5709	That broke up the meeting. The boys were avenged. Vacation had come.

5710	

5711	NOTE:—The pretended “compositions” quoted in this chapter are taken

5712	without alteration from a volume entitled “Prose and Poetry, by a

5713	Western Lady”—but they are exactly and precisely after the schoolgirl

5714	pattern, and hence are much happier than any mere imitations could be.

5715	

5716	

5717	

5718	

5719	CHAPTER XXII

5720	

5721	

5722	TOM joined the new order of Cadets of Temperance, being attracted by the

5723	showy character of their “regalia.” He promised to abstain from smoking,

5724	chewing, and profanity as long as he remained a member. Now he found out

5725	a new thing—namely, that to promise not to do a thing is the surest way

5726	in the world to make a body want to go and do that very thing. Tom soon

5727	found himself tormented with a desire to drink and swear; the desire

5728	grew to be so intense that nothing but the hope of a chance to display

5729	himself in his red sash kept him from withdrawing from the order. Fourth

5730	of July was coming; but he soon gave that up—gave it up before he had

5731	worn his shackles over forty-eight hours—and fixed his hopes upon old

5732	Judge Frazer, justice of the peace, who was apparently on his deathbed

5733	and would have a big public funeral, since he was so high an official.

5734	During three days Tom was deeply concerned about the Judge’s condition

5735	and hungry for news of it. Sometimes his hopes ran high—so high that

5736	he would venture to get out his regalia and practise before the

5737	looking-glass. But the Judge had a most discouraging way of fluctuating.

5738	At last he was pronounced upon the mend—and then convalescent. Tom was

5739	disgusted; and felt a sense of injury, too. He handed in his resignation

5740	at once—and that night the Judge suffered a relapse and died. Tom

5741	resolved that he would never trust a man like that again.

5742	

5743	The funeral was a fine thing. The Cadets paraded in a style calculated

5744	to kill the late member with envy. Tom was a free boy again,

5745	however—there was something in that. He could drink and swear, now—but

5746	found to his surprise that he did not want to. The simple fact that he

5747	could, took the desire away, and the charm of it.

5748	

5749	Tom presently wondered to find that his coveted vacation was beginning

5750	to hang a little heavily on his hands.

5751	

5752	He attempted a diary—but nothing happened during three days, and so he

5753	abandoned it.

5754	

5755	The first of all the negro minstrel shows came to town, and made a

5756	sensation. Tom and Joe Harper got up a band of performers and were happy

5757	for two days.

5758	

5759	Even the Glorious Fourth was in some sense a failure, for it rained

5760	hard, there was no procession in consequence, and the greatest man

5761	in the world (as Tom supposed), Mr. Benton, an actual United States

5762	Senator, proved an overwhelming disappointment—for he was not

5763	twenty-five feet high, nor even anywhere in the neighborhood of it.

5764	

5765	A circus came. The boys played circus for three days afterward in tents

5766	made of rag carpeting—admission, three pins for boys, two for girls—and

5767	then circusing was abandoned.

5768	

5769	A phrenologist and a mesmerizer came—and went again and left the village

5770	duller and drearier than ever.

5771	

5772	There were some boys-and-girls’ parties, but they were so few and so

5773	delightful that they only made the aching voids between ache the harder.

5774	

5775	Becky Thatcher was gone to her Constantinople home to stay with her

5776	parents during vacation—so there was no bright side to life anywhere.

5777	

5778	The dreadful secret of the murder was a chronic misery. It was a very

5779	cancer for permanency and pain.

5780	

5781	Then came the measles.

5782	

5783	During two long weeks Tom lay a prisoner, dead to the world and its

5784	happenings. He was very ill, he was interested in nothing. When he got

5785	upon his feet at last and moved feebly downtown, a melancholy change had

5786	come over everything and every creature. There had been a “revival,” and

5787	everybody had “got religion,” not only the adults, but even the boys and

5788	girls. Tom went about, hoping against hope for the sight of one blessed

5789	sinful face, but disappointment crossed him everywhere. He found Joe

5790	Harper studying a Testament, and turned sadly away from the depressing

5791	spectacle. He sought Ben Rogers, and found him visiting the poor with a

5792	basket of tracts. He hunted up Jim Hollis, who called his attention to

5793	the precious blessing of his late measles as a warning. Every boy

5794	he encountered added another ton to his depression; and when, in

5795	desperation, he flew for refuge at last to the bosom of Huckleberry Finn

5796	and was received with a Scriptural quotation, his heart broke and he

5797	crept home and to bed realizing that he alone of all the town was lost,

5798	forever and forever.

5799	

5800	And that night there came on a terrific storm, with driving rain, awful

5801	claps of thunder and blinding sheets of lightning. He covered his head

5802	with the bedclothes and waited in a horror of suspense for his doom; for

5803	he had not the shadow of a doubt that all this hubbub was about him.

5804	He believed he had taxed the forbearance of the powers above to the

5805	extremity of endurance and that this was the result. It might have

5806	seemed to him a waste of pomp and ammunition to kill a bug with a

5807	battery of artillery, but there seemed nothing incongruous about the

5808	getting up such an expensive thunderstorm as this to knock the turf from

5809	under an insect like himself.

5810	

5811	By and by the tempest spent itself and died without accomplishing its

5812	object. The boy’s first impulse was to be grateful, and reform. His

5813	second was to wait—for there might not be any more storms.

5814	

5815	The next day the doctors were back; Tom had relapsed. The three weeks he

5816	spent on his back this time seemed an entire age. When he got abroad

5817	at last he was hardly grateful that he had been spared, remembering how

5818	lonely was his estate, how companionless and forlorn he was. He drifted

5819	listlessly down the street and found Jim Hollis acting as judge in a

5820	juvenile court that was trying a cat for murder, in the presence of her

5821	victim, a bird. He found Joe Harper and Huck Finn up an alley eating a

5822	stolen melon. Poor lads! they—like Tom—had suffered a relapse.

5823	

5824	

5825	

5826	

5827	CHAPTER XXIII

5828	

5829	

5830	AT last the sleepy atmosphere was stirred—and vigorously: the murder

5831	trial came on in the court. It became the absorbing topic of village

5832	talk immediately. Tom could not get away from it. Every reference to

5833	the murder sent a shudder to his heart, for his troubled conscience

5834	and fears almost persuaded him that these remarks were put forth in

5835	his hearing as “feelers”; he did not see how he could be suspected of

5836	knowing anything about the murder, but still he could not be comfortable

5837	in the midst of this gossip. It kept him in a cold shiver all the time.

5838	He took Huck to a lonely place to have a talk with him. It would be some

5839	relief to unseal his tongue for a little while; to divide his burden of

5840	distress with another sufferer. Moreover, he wanted to assure himself

5841	that Huck had remained discreet.

5842	

5843	“Huck, have you ever told anybody about—that?”

5844	

5845	“’Bout what?”

5846	

5847	“You know what.”

5848	

5849	“Oh—’course I haven’t.”

5850	

5851	“Never a word?”

5852	

5853	“Never a solitary word, so help me. What makes you ask?”

5854	

5855	“Well, I was afeard.”

5856	

5857	“Why, Tom Sawyer, we wouldn’t be alive two days if that got found out.

5858	_You_ know that.”

5859	

5860	Tom felt more comfortable. After a pause:

5861	

5862	“Huck, they couldn’t anybody get you to tell, could they?”

5863	

5864	“Get me to tell? Why, if I wanted that halfbreed devil to drownd me they

5865	could get me to tell. They ain’t no different way.”

5866	

5867	“Well, that’s all right, then. I reckon we’re safe as long as we keep

5868	mum. But let’s swear again, anyway. It’s more surer.”

5869	

5870	“I’m agreed.”

5871	

5872	So they swore again with dread solemnities.

5873	

5874	“What is the talk around, Huck? I’ve heard a power of it.”

5875	

5876	“Talk? Well, it’s just Muff Potter, Muff Potter, Muff Potter all the

5877	time. It keeps me in a sweat, constant, so’s I want to hide som’ers.”

5878	

5879	“That’s just the same way they go on round me. I reckon he’s a goner.

5880	Don’t you feel sorry for him, sometimes?”

5881	

5882	“Most always—most always. He ain’t no account; but then he hain’t ever

5883	done anything to hurt anybody. Just fishes a little, to get money to

5884	get drunk on—and loafs around considerable; but lord, we all do

5885	that—leastways most of us—preachers and such like. But he’s kind of

5886	good—he give me half a fish, once, when there warn’t enough for two; and

5887	lots of times he’s kind of stood by me when I was out of luck.”

5888	

5889	“Well, he’s mended kites for me, Huck, and knitted hooks on to my line.

5890	I wish we could get him out of there.”

5891	

5892	“My! we couldn’t get him out, Tom. And besides, ’twouldn’t do any good;

5893	they’d ketch him again.”

5894	

5895	“Yes—so they would. But I hate to hear ’em abuse him so like the dickens

5896	when he never done—that.”

5897	

5898	“I do too, Tom. Lord, I hear ’em say he’s the bloodiest looking villain

5899	in this country, and they wonder he wasn’t ever hung before.”

5900	

5901	“Yes, they talk like that, all the time. I’ve heard ’em say that if he

5902	was to get free they’d lynch him.”

5903	

5904	“And they’d do it, too.”

5905	

5906	The boys had a long talk, but it brought them little comfort. As the

5907	twilight drew on, they found themselves hanging about the neighborhood

5908	of the little isolated jail, perhaps with an undefined hope that

5909	something would happen that might clear away their difficulties. But

5910	nothing happened; there seemed to be no angels or fairies interested in

5911	this luckless captive.

5912	

5913	The boys did as they had often done before—went to the cell grating and

5914	gave Potter some tobacco and matches. He was on the ground floor and

5915	there were no guards.

5916	

5917	His gratitude for their gifts had always smote their consciences

5918	before—it cut deeper than ever, this time. They felt cowardly and

5919	treacherous to the last degree when Potter said:

5920	

5921	“You’ve been mighty good to me, boys—better’n anybody else in this town.

5922	And I don’t forget it, I don’t. Often I says to myself, says I, ‘I used

5923	to mend all the boys’ kites and things, and show ’em where the good

5924	fishin’ places was, and befriend ’em what I could, and now they’ve

5925	all forgot old Muff when he’s in trouble; but Tom don’t, and Huck

5926	don’t—_they_ don’t forget him, says I, ‘and I don’t forget them.’ Well,

5927	boys, I done an awful thing—drunk and crazy at the time—that’s the only

5928	way I account for it—and now I got to swing for it, and it’s right.

5929	Right, and _best_, too, I reckon—hope so, anyway. Well, we won’t talk

5930	about that. I don’t want to make _you_ feel bad; you’ve befriended me.

5931	But what I want to say, is, don’t _you_ ever get drunk—then you won’t

5932	ever get here. Stand a litter furder west—so—that’s it; it’s a prime

5933	comfort to see faces that’s friendly when a body’s in such a muck

5934	of trouble, and there don’t none come here but yourn. Good friendly

5935	faces—good friendly faces. Git up on one another’s backs and let me

5936	touch ’em. That’s it. Shake hands—yourn’ll come through the bars, but

5937	mine’s too big. Little hands, and weak—but they’ve helped Muff Potter a

5938	power, and they’d help him more if they could.”

5939	

5940	Tom went home miserable, and his dreams that night were full of horrors.

5941	The next day and the day after, he hung about the courtroom, drawn by an

5942	almost irresistible impulse to go in, but forcing himself to stay out.

5943	Huck was having the same experience. They studiously avoided each other.

5944	Each wandered away, from time to time, but the same dismal fascination

5945	always brought them back presently. Tom kept his ears open when idlers

5946	sauntered out of the courtroom, but invariably heard distressing

5947	news—the toils were closing more and more relentlessly around poor

5948	Potter. At the end of the second day the village talk was to the effect

5949	that Injun Joe’s evidence stood firm and unshaken, and that there was

5950	not the slightest question as to what the jury’s verdict would be.

5951	

5952	Tom was out late, that night, and came to bed through the window. He

5953	was in a tremendous state of excitement. It was hours before he got to

5954	sleep. All the village flocked to the courthouse the next morning, for

5955	this was to be the great day. Both sexes were about equally represented

5956	in the packed audience. After a long wait the jury filed in and took

5957	their places; shortly afterward, Potter, pale and haggard, timid and

5958	hopeless, was brought in, with chains upon him, and seated where all

5959	the curious eyes could stare at him; no less conspicuous was Injun Joe,

5960	stolid as ever. There was another pause, and then the judge arrived and

5961	the sheriff proclaimed the opening of the court. The usual whisperings

5962	among the lawyers and gathering together of papers followed. These

5963	details and accompanying delays worked up an atmosphere of preparation

5964	that was as impressive as it was fascinating.

5965	

5966	Now a witness was called who testified that he found Muff Potter washing

5967	in the brook, at an early hour of the morning that the murder was

5968	discovered, and that he immediately sneaked away. After some further

5969	questioning, counsel for the prosecution said:

5970	

5971	“Take the witness.”

5972	

5973	The prisoner raised his eyes for a moment, but dropped them again when

5974	his own counsel said:

5975	

5976	“I have no questions to ask him.”

5977	

5978	The next witness proved the finding of the knife near the corpse.

5979	Counsel for the prosecution said:

5980	

5981	“Take the witness.”

5982	

5983	“I have no questions to ask him,” Potter’s lawyer replied.

5984	

5985	A third witness swore he had often seen the knife in Potter’s

5986	possession.

5987	

5988	“Take the witness.”

5989	

5990	Counsel for Potter declined to question him. The faces of the audience

5991	began to betray annoyance. Did this attorney mean to throw away his

5992	client’s life without an effort?

5993	

5994	Several witnesses deposed concerning Potter’s guilty behavior when

5995	brought to the scene of the murder. They were allowed to leave the stand

5996	without being cross-questioned.

5997	

5998	Every detail of the damaging circumstances that occurred in the

5999	graveyard upon that morning which all present remembered so well was

6000	brought out by credible witnesses, but none of them were cross-examined

6001	by Potter’s lawyer. The perplexity and dissatisfaction of the house

6002	expressed itself in murmurs and provoked a reproof from the bench.

6003	Counsel for the prosecution now said:

6004	

6005	“By the oaths of citizens whose simple word is above suspicion, we have

6006	fastened this awful crime, beyond all possibility of question, upon the

6007	unhappy prisoner at the bar. We rest our case here.”

6008	

6009	A groan escaped from poor Potter, and he put his face in his hands and

6010	rocked his body softly to and fro, while a painful silence reigned

6011	in the courtroom. Many men were moved, and many women’s compassion

6012	testified itself in tears. Counsel for the defence rose and said:

6013	

6014	“Your honor, in our remarks at the opening of this trial, we

6015	foreshadowed our purpose to prove that our client did this fearful deed

6016	while under the influence of a blind and irresponsible delirium produced

6017	by drink. We have changed our mind. We shall not offer that plea.” [Then

6018	to the clerk:] “Call Thomas Sawyer!”

6019	

6020	A puzzled amazement awoke in every face in the house, not even excepting

6021	Potter’s. Every eye fastened itself with wondering interest upon Tom as

6022	he rose and took his place upon the stand. The boy looked wild enough,

6023	for he was badly scared. The oath was administered.

6024	

6025	“Thomas Sawyer, where were you on the seventeenth of June, about the

6026	hour of midnight?”

6027	

6028	Tom glanced at Injun Joe’s iron face and his tongue failed him. The

6029	audience listened breathless, but the words refused to come. After a few

6030	moments, however, the boy got a little of his strength back, and managed

6031	to put enough of it into his voice to make part of the house hear:

6032	

6033	“In the graveyard!”

6034	

6035	“A little bit louder, please. Don’t be afraid. You were—”

6036	

6037	“In the graveyard.”

6038	

6039	A contemptuous smile flitted across Injun Joe’s face.

6040	

6041	“Were you anywhere near Horse Williams’ grave?”

6042	

6043	“Yes, sir.”

6044	

6045	“Speak up—just a trifle louder. How near were you?”

6046	

6047	“Near as I am to you.”

6048	

6049	“Were you hidden, or not?”

6050	

6051	“I was hid.”

6052	

6053	“Where?”

6054	

6055	“Behind the elms that’s on the edge of the grave.”

6056	

6057	Injun Joe gave a barely perceptible start.

6058	

6059	“Any one with you?”

6060	

6061	“Yes, sir. I went there with—”

6062	

6063	“Wait—wait a moment. Never mind mentioning your companion’s name. We

6064	will produce him at the proper time. Did you carry anything there with

6065	you.”

6066	

6067	Tom hesitated and looked confused.

6068	

6069	“Speak out, my boy—don’t be diffident. The truth is always respectable.

6070	What did you take there?”

6071	

6072	“Only a—a—dead cat.”

6073	

6074	There was a ripple of mirth, which the court checked.

6075	

6076	“We will produce the skeleton of that cat. Now, my boy, tell us

6077	everything that occurred—tell it in your own way—don’t skip anything,

6078	and don’t be afraid.”

6079	

6080	Tom began—hesitatingly at first, but as he warmed to his subject his

6081	words flowed more and more easily; in a little while every sound ceased

6082	but his own voice; every eye fixed itself upon him; with parted lips and

6083	bated breath the audience hung upon his words, taking no note of time,

6084	rapt in the ghastly fascinations of the tale. The strain upon pent

6085	emotion reached its climax when the boy said:

6086	

6087	“—and as the doctor fetched the board around and Muff Potter fell, Injun

6088	Joe jumped with the knife and—”

6089	

6090	Crash! Quick as lightning the halfbreed sprang for a window, tore his

6091	way through all opposers, and was gone!

6092	

6093	

6094	

6095	

6096	CHAPTER XXIV

6097	

6098	

6099	TOM was a glittering hero once more—the pet of the old, the envy of the

6100	young. His name even went into immortal print, for the village paper

6101	magnified him. There were some that believed he would be President, yet,

6102	if he escaped hanging.

6103	

6104	As usual, the fickle, unreasoning world took Muff Potter to its bosom

6105	and fondled him as lavishly as it had abused him before. But that sort

6106	of conduct is to the world’s credit; therefore it is not well to find

6107	fault with it.

6108	

6109	Tom’s days were days of splendor and exultation to him, but his nights

6110	were seasons of horror. Injun Joe infested all his dreams, and always

6111	with doom in his eye. Hardly any temptation could persuade the boy

6112	to stir abroad after nightfall. Poor Huck was in the same state of

6113	wretchedness and terror, for Tom had told the whole story to the lawyer

6114	the night before the great day of the trial, and Huck was sore afraid

6115	that his share in the business might leak out, yet, notwithstanding

6116	Injun Joe’s flight had saved him the suffering of testifying in court.

6117	The poor fellow had got the attorney to promise secrecy, but what of

6118	that? Since Tom’s harassed conscience had managed to drive him to the

6119	lawyer’s house by night and wring a dread tale from lips that had

6120	been sealed with the dismalest and most formidable of oaths, Huck’s

6121	confidence in the human race was wellnigh obliterated.

6122	

6123	Daily Muff Potter’s gratitude made Tom glad he had spoken; but nightly

6124	he wished he had sealed up his tongue.

6125	

6126	Half the time Tom was afraid Injun Joe would never be captured; the

6127	other half he was afraid he would be. He felt sure he never could draw a

6128	safe breath again until that man was dead and he had seen the corpse.

6129	

6130	Rewards had been offered, the country had been scoured, but no Injun

6131	Joe was found. One of those omniscient and aweinspiring marvels, a

6132	detective, came up from St. Louis, moused around, shook his head, looked

6133	wise, and made that sort of astounding success which members of that

6134	craft usually achieve. That is to say, he “found a clew.” But you can’t

6135	hang a “clew” for murder, and so after that detective had got through

6136	and gone home, Tom felt just as insecure as he was before.

6137	

6138	The slow days drifted on, and each left behind it a slightly lightened

6139	weight of apprehension.

6140	

6141	

6142	

6143	

6144	CHAPTER XXV

6145	

6146	

6147	THERE comes a time in every rightly-constructed boy’s life when he has

6148	a raging desire to go somewhere and dig for hidden treasure. This desire

6149	suddenly came upon Tom one day. He sallied out to find Joe Harper,

6150	but failed of success. Next he sought Ben Rogers; he had gone fishing.

6151	Presently he stumbled upon Huck Finn the Red-Handed. Huck would

6152	answer. Tom took him to a private place and opened the matter to him

6153	confidentially. Huck was willing. Huck was always willing to take a hand

6154	in any enterprise that offered entertainment and required no capital,

6155	for he had a troublesome superabundance of that sort of time which is

6156	not money. “Where’ll we dig?” said Huck.

6157	

6158	“Oh, most anywhere.”

6159	

6160	“Why, is it hid all around?”

6161	

6162	“No, indeed it ain’t. It’s hid in mighty particular places,

6163	Huck—sometimes on islands, sometimes in rotten chests under the end of

6164	a limb of an old dead tree, just where the shadow falls at midnight; but

6165	mostly under the floor in ha’nted houses.”

6166	

6167	“Who hides it?”

6168	

6169	“Why, robbers, of course—who’d you reckon? Sunday-school

6170	sup’rintendents?”

6171	

6172	“I don’t know. If ’twas mine I wouldn’t hide it; I’d spend it and have a

6173	good time.”

6174	

6175	“So would I. But robbers don’t do that way. They always hide it and

6176	leave it there.”

6177	

6178	“Don’t they come after it any more?”

6179	

6180	“No, they think they will, but they generally forget the marks, or else

6181	they die. Anyway, it lays there a long time and gets rusty; and by and

6182	by somebody finds an old yellow paper that tells how to find the marks—a

6183	paper that’s got to be ciphered over about a week because it’s mostly

6184	signs and hy’roglyphics.”

6185	

6186	“Hyro—which?”

6187	

6188	“Hy’roglyphics—pictures and things, you know, that don’t seem to mean

6189	anything.”

6190	

6191	“Have you got one of them papers, Tom?”

6192	

6193	“No.”

6194	

6195	“Well then, how you going to find the marks?”

6196	

6197	“I don’t want any marks. They always bury it under a ha’nted house or on

6198	an island, or under a dead tree that’s got one limb sticking out. Well,

6199	we’ve tried Jackson’s Island a little, and we can try it again some

6200	time; and there’s the old ha’nted house up the Still-House branch, and

6201	there’s lots of dead-limb trees—dead loads of ’em.”

6202	

6203	“Is it under all of them?”

6204	

6205	“How you talk! No!”

6206	

6207	“Then how you going to know which one to go for?”

6208	

6209	“Go for all of ’em!”

6210	

6211	“Why, Tom, it’ll take all summer.”

6212	

6213	“Well, what of that? Suppose you find a brass pot with a hundred dollars

6214	in it, all rusty and gray, or rotten chest full of di’monds. How’s

6215	that?”

6216	

6217	Huck’s eyes glowed.

6218	

6219	“That’s bully. Plenty bully enough for me. Just you gimme the hundred

6220	dollars and I don’t want no di’monds.”

6221	

6222	“All right. But I bet you I ain’t going to throw off on di’monds. Some

6223	of ’em’s worth twenty dollars apiece—there ain’t any, hardly, but’s

6224	worth six bits or a dollar.”

6225	

6226	“No! Is that so?”

6227	

6228	“Cert’nly—anybody’ll tell you so. Hain’t you ever seen one, Huck?”

6229	

6230	“Not as I remember.”

6231	

6232	“Oh, kings have slathers of them.”

6233	

6234	“Well, I don’ know no kings, Tom.”

6235	

6236	“I reckon you don’t. But if you was to go to Europe you’d see a raft of

6237	’em hopping around.”

6238	

6239	“Do they hop?”

6240	

6241	“Hop?—your granny! No!”

6242	

6243	“Well, what did you say they did, for?”

6244	

6245	“Shucks, I only meant you’d _see_ ’em—not hopping, of course—what do

6246	they want to hop for?—but I mean you’d just see ’em—scattered around,

6247	you know, in a kind of a general way. Like that old humpbacked Richard.”

6248	

6249	“Richard? What’s his other name?”

6250	

6251	“He didn’t have any other name. Kings don’t have any but a given name.”

6252	

6253	“No?”

6254	

6255	“But they don’t.”

6256	

6257	“Well, if they like it, Tom, all right; but I don’t want to be a king

6258	and have only just a given name, like a nigger. But say—where you going

6259	to dig first?”

6260	

6261	“Well, I don’t know. S’pose we tackle that old dead-limb tree on the

6262	hill t’other side of Still-House branch?”

6263	

6264	“I’m agreed.”

6265	

6266	So they got a crippled pick and a shovel, and set out on their

6267	three-mile tramp. They arrived hot and panting, and threw themselves

6268	down in the shade of a neighboring elm to rest and have a smoke.

6269	

6270	“I like this,” said Tom.

6271	

6272	“So do I.”

6273	

6274	“Say, Huck, if we find a treasure here, what you going to do with your

6275	share?”

6276	

6277	“Well, I’ll have pie and a glass of soda every day, and I’ll go to every

6278	circus that comes along. I bet I’ll have a gay time.”

6279	

6280	“Well, ain’t you going to save any of it?”

6281	

6282	“Save it? What for?”

6283	

6284	“Why, so as to have something to live on, by and by.”

6285	

6286	“Oh, that ain’t any use. Pap would come back to thish-yer town some day

6287	and get his claws on it if I didn’t hurry up, and I tell you he’d clean

6288	it out pretty quick. What you going to do with yourn, Tom?”

6289	

6290	“I’m going to buy a new drum, and a sure’nough sword, and a red necktie

6291	and a bull pup, and get married.”

6292	

6293	“Married!”

6294	

6295	“That’s it.”

6296	

6297	“Tom, you—why, you ain’t in your right mind.”

6298	

6299	“Wait—you’ll see.”

6300	

6301	“Well, that’s the foolishest thing you could do. Look at pap and my

6302	mother. Fight! Why, they used to fight all the time. I remember, mighty

6303	well.”

6304	

6305	“That ain’t anything. The girl I’m going to marry won’t fight.”

6306	

6307	“Tom, I reckon they’re all alike. They’ll all comb a body. Now you

6308	better think ’bout this awhile. I tell you you better. What’s the name

6309	of the gal?”

6310	

6311	“It ain’t a gal at all—it’s a girl.”

6312	

6313	“It’s all the same, I reckon; some says gal, some says girl—both’s

6314	right, like enough. Anyway, what’s her name, Tom?”

6315	

6316	“I’ll tell you some time—not now.”

6317	

6318	“All right—that’ll do. Only if you get married I’ll be more lonesomer

6319	than ever.”

6320	

6321	“No you won’t. You’ll come and live with me. Now stir out of this and

6322	we’ll go to digging.”

6323	

6324	They worked and sweated for half an hour. No result. They toiled another

6325	halfhour. Still no result. Huck said:

6326	

6327	“Do they always bury it as deep as this?”

6328	

6329	“Sometimes—not always. Not generally. I reckon we haven’t got the right

6330	place.”

6331	

6332	So they chose a new spot and began again. The labor dragged a little,

6333	but still they made progress. They pegged away in silence for some time.

6334	Finally Huck leaned on his shovel, swabbed the beaded drops from his

6335	brow with his sleeve, and said:

6336	

6337	“Where you going to dig next, after we get this one?”

6338	

6339	“I reckon maybe we’ll tackle the old tree that’s over yonder on Cardiff

6340	Hill back of the widow’s.”

6341	

6342	“I reckon that’ll be a good one. But won’t the widow take it away from

6343	us, Tom? It’s on her land.”

6344	

6345	“_She_ take it away! Maybe she’d like to try it once. Whoever finds one

6346	of these hid treasures, it belongs to him. It don’t make any difference

6347	whose land it’s on.”

6348	

6349	That was satisfactory. The work went on. By and by Huck said:

6350	

6351	“Blame it, we must be in the wrong place again. What do you think?”

6352	

6353	“It is mighty curious, Huck. I don’t understand it. Sometimes witches

6354	interfere. I reckon maybe that’s what’s the trouble now.”

6355	

6356	“Shucks! Witches ain’t got no power in the daytime.”

6357	

6358	“Well, that’s so. I didn’t think of that. Oh, I know what the matter is!

6359	What a blamed lot of fools we are! You got to find out where the shadow

6360	of the limb falls at midnight, and that’s where you dig!”

6361	

6362	“Then consound it, we’ve fooled away all this work for nothing. Now hang

6363	it all, we got to come back in the night. It’s an awful long way. Can

6364	you get out?”

6365	

6366	“I bet I will. We’ve got to do it tonight, too, because if somebody sees

6367	these holes they’ll know in a minute what’s here and they’ll go for it.”

6368	

6369	“Well, I’ll come around and maow tonight.”

6370	

6371	“All right. Let’s hide the tools in the bushes.”

6372	

6373	The boys were there that night, about the appointed time. They sat in

6374	the shadow waiting. It was a lonely place, and an hour made solemn by

6375	old traditions. Spirits whispered in the rustling leaves, ghosts lurked

6376	in the murky nooks, the deep baying of a hound floated up out of the

6377	distance, an owl answered with his sepulchral note. The boys were

6378	subdued by these solemnities, and talked little. By and by they judged

6379	that twelve had come; they marked where the shadow fell, and began to

6380	dig. Their hopes commenced to rise. Their interest grew stronger, and

6381	their industry kept pace with it. The hole deepened and still deepened,

6382	but every time their hearts jumped to hear the pick strike upon

6383	something, they only suffered a new disappointment. It was only a stone

6384	or a chunk. At last Tom said:

6385	

6386	“It ain’t any use, Huck, we’re wrong again.”

6387	

6388	“Well, but we _can’t_ be wrong. We spotted the shadder to a dot.”

6389	

6390	“I know it, but then there’s another thing.”

6391	

6392	“What’s that?”.

6393	

6394	“Why, we only guessed at the time. Like enough it was too late or too

6395	early.”

6396	

6397	Huck dropped his shovel.

6398	

6399	“That’s it,” said he. “That’s the very trouble. We got to give this one

6400	up. We can’t ever tell the right time, and besides this kind of thing’s

6401	too awful, here this time of night with witches and ghosts a-fluttering

6402	around so. I feel as if something’s behind me all the time; and I’m

6403	afeard to turn around, becuz maybe there’s others in front a-waiting for

6404	a chance. I been creeping all over, ever since I got here.”

6405	

6406	“Well, I’ve been pretty much so, too, Huck. They most always put in a

6407	dead man when they bury a treasure under a tree, to look out for it.”

6408	

6409	“Lordy!”

6410	

6411	“Yes, they do. I’ve always heard that.”

6412	

6413	“Tom, I don’t like to fool around much where there’s dead people. A

6414	body’s bound to get into trouble with ’em, sure.”

6415	

6416	“I don’t like to stir ’em up, either. S’pose this one here was to stick

6417	his skull out and say something!”

6418	

6419	“Don’t Tom! It’s awful.”

6420	

6421	“Well, it just is. Huck, I don’t feel comfortable a bit.”

6422	

6423	“Say, Tom, let’s give this place up, and try somewheres else.”

6424	

6425	“All right, I reckon we better.”

6426	

6427	“What’ll it be?”

6428	

6429	Tom considered awhile; and then said:

6430	

6431	“The ha’nted house. That’s it!”

6432	

6433	“Blame it, I don’t like ha’nted houses, Tom. Why, they’re a dern sight

6434	worse’n dead people. Dead people might talk, maybe, but they don’t come

6435	sliding around in a shroud, when you ain’t noticing, and peep over your

6436	shoulder all of a sudden and grit their teeth, the way a ghost does. I

6437	couldn’t stand such a thing as that, Tom—nobody could.”

6438	

6439	“Yes, but, Huck, ghosts don’t travel around only at night. They won’t

6440	hender us from digging there in the daytime.”

6441	

6442	“Well, that’s so. But you know mighty well people don’t go about that

6443	ha’nted house in the day nor the night.”

6444	

6445	“Well, that’s mostly because they don’t like to go where a man’s been

6446	murdered, anyway—but nothing’s ever been seen around that house except

6447	in the night—just some blue lights slipping by the windows—no regular

6448	ghosts.”

6449	

6450	“Well, where you see one of them blue lights flickering around, Tom,

6451	you can bet there’s a ghost mighty close behind it. It stands to reason.

6452	Becuz you know that they don’t anybody but ghosts use ’em.”

6453	

6454	“Yes, that’s so. But anyway they don’t come around in the daytime, so

6455	what’s the use of our being afeard?”

6456	

6457	“Well, all right. We’ll tackle the ha’nted house if you say so—but I

6458	reckon it’s taking chances.”

6459	

6460	They had started down the hill by this time. There in the middle of the

6461	moonlit valley below them stood the “ha’nted” house, utterly isolated,

6462	its fences gone long ago, rank weeds smothering the very doorsteps, the

6463	chimney crumbled to ruin, the window-sashes vacant, a corner of the roof

6464	caved in. The boys gazed awhile, half expecting to see a blue light flit

6465	past a window; then talking in a low tone, as befitted the time and the

6466	circumstances, they struck far off to the right, to give the haunted

6467	house a wide berth, and took their way homeward through the woods that

6468	adorned the rearward side of Cardiff Hill.

6469	

6470	

6471	

6472	

6473	CHAPTER XXVI

6474	

6475	

6476	ABOUT noon the next day the boys arrived at the dead tree; they had come

6477	for their tools. Tom was impatient to go to the haunted house; Huck was

6478	measurably so, also—but suddenly said:

6479	

6480	“Lookyhere, Tom, do you know what day it is?”

6481	

6482	Tom mentally ran over the days of the week, and then quickly lifted his

6483	eyes with a startled look in them—

6484	

6485	“My! I never once thought of it, Huck!”

6486	

6487	“Well, I didn’t neither, but all at once it popped onto me that it was

6488	Friday.”

6489	

6490	“Blame it, a body can’t be too careful, Huck. We might ’a’ got into an

6491	awful scrape, tackling such a thing on a Friday.”

6492	

6493	“_Might_! Better say we _would_! There’s some lucky days, maybe, but

6494	Friday ain’t.”

6495	

6496	“Any fool knows that. I don’t reckon _you_ was the first that found it

6497	out, Huck.”

6498	

6499	“Well, I never said I was, did I? And Friday ain’t all, neither. I had a

6500	rotten bad dream last night—dreampt about rats.”

6501	

6502	“No! Sure sign of trouble. Did they fight?”

6503	

6504	“No.”

6505	

6506	“Well, that’s good, Huck. When they don’t fight it’s only a sign that

6507	there’s trouble around, you know. All we got to do is to look mighty

6508	sharp and keep out of it. We’ll drop this thing for today, and play. Do

6509	you know Robin Hood, Huck?”

6510	

6511	“No. Who’s Robin Hood?”

6512	

6513	“Why, he was one of the greatest men that was ever in England—and the

6514	best. He was a robber.”

6515	

6516	“Cracky, I wisht I was. Who did he rob?”

6517	

6518	“Only sheriffs and bishops and rich people and kings, and such like. But

6519	he never bothered the poor. He loved ’em. He always divided up with ’em

6520	perfectly square.”

6521	

6522	“Well, he must ’a’ been a brick.”

6523	

6524	“I bet you he was, Huck. Oh, he was the noblest man that ever was.

6525	They ain’t any such men now, I can tell you. He could lick any man in

6526	England, with one hand tied behind him; and he could take his yew bow

6527	and plug a ten-cent piece every time, a mile and a half.”

6528	

6529	“What’s a _yew_ bow?”

6530	

6531	“I don’t know. It’s some kind of a bow, of course. And if he hit that

6532	dime only on the edge he would set down and cry—and curse. But we’ll

6533	play Robin Hood—it’s nobby fun. I’ll learn you.”

6534	

6535	“I’m agreed.”

6536	

6537	So they played Robin Hood all the afternoon, now and then casting a

6538	yearning eye down upon the haunted house and passing a remark about the

6539	morrow’s prospects and possibilities there. As the sun began to sink

6540	into the west they took their way homeward athwart the long shadows

6541	of the trees and soon were buried from sight in the forests of Cardiff

6542	Hill.

6543	

6544	On Saturday, shortly after noon, the boys were at the dead tree again.

6545	They had a smoke and a chat in the shade, and then dug a little in their

6546	last hole, not with great hope, but merely because Tom said there were

6547	so many cases where people had given up a treasure after getting down

6548	within six inches of it, and then somebody else had come along and

6549	turned it up with a single thrust of a shovel. The thing failed this

6550	time, however, so the boys shouldered their tools and went away feeling

6551	that they had not trifled with fortune, but had fulfilled all the

6552	requirements that belong to the business of treasure-hunting.

6553	

6554	When they reached the haunted house there was something so weird and

6555	grisly about the dead silence that reigned there under the baking sun,

6556	and something so depressing about the loneliness and desolation of the

6557	place, that they were afraid, for a moment, to venture in. Then they

6558	crept to the door and took a trembling peep. They saw a weedgrown,

6559	floorless room, unplastered, an ancient fireplace, vacant windows,

6560	a ruinous staircase; and here, there, and everywhere hung ragged and

6561	abandoned cobwebs. They presently entered, softly, with quickened

6562	pulses, talking in whispers, ears alert to catch the slightest sound,

6563	and muscles tense and ready for instant retreat.

6564	

6565	In a little while familiarity modified their fears and they gave the

6566	place a critical and interested examination, rather admiring their own

6567	boldness, and wondering at it, too. Next they wanted to look upstairs.

6568	This was something like cutting off retreat, but they got to daring

6569	each other, and of course there could be but one result—they threw their

6570	tools into a corner and made the ascent. Up there were the same signs of

6571	decay. In one corner they found a closet that promised mystery, but the

6572	promise was a fraud—there was nothing in it. Their courage was up now

6573	and well in hand. They were about to go down and begin work when—

6574	

6575	“Sh!” said Tom.

6576	

6577	“What is it?” whispered Huck, blanching with fright.

6578	

6579	“Sh!... There!... Hear it?”

6580	

6581	“Yes!... Oh, my! Let’s run!”

6582	

6583	“Keep still! Don’t you budge! They’re coming right toward the door.”

6584	

6585	The boys stretched themselves upon the floor with their eyes to

6586	knotholes in the planking, and lay waiting, in a misery of fear.

6587	

6588	“They’ve stopped.... No—coming.... Here they are. Don’t whisper another

6589	word, Huck. My goodness, I wish I was out of this!”

6590	

6591	Two men entered. Each boy said to himself: “There’s the old deaf and

6592	dumb Spaniard that’s been about town once or twice lately—never saw

6593	t’other man before.”

6594	

6595	“T’other” was a ragged, unkempt creature, with nothing very pleasant

6596	in his face. The Spaniard was wrapped in a serape; he had bushy white

6597	whiskers; long white hair flowed from under his sombrero, and he wore

6598	green goggles. When they came in, “t’other” was talking in a low voice;

6599	they sat down on the ground, facing the door, with their backs to the

6600	wall, and the speaker continued his remarks. His manner became less

6601	guarded and his words more distinct as he proceeded:

6602	

6603	“No,” said he, “I’ve thought it all over, and I don’t like it. It’s

6604	dangerous.”

6605	

6606	“Dangerous!” grunted the “deaf and dumb” Spaniard—to the vast surprise

6607	of the boys. “Milksop!”

6608	

6609	This voice made the boys gasp and quake. It was Injun Joe’s! There was

6610	silence for some time. Then Joe said:

6611	

6612	“What’s any more dangerous than that job up yonder—but nothing’s come of

6613	it.”

6614	

6615	“That’s different. Away up the river so, and not another house about.

6616	’Twon’t ever be known that we tried, anyway, long as we didn’t succeed.”

6617	

6618	“Well, what’s more dangerous than coming here in the daytime!—anybody

6619	would suspicion us that saw us.”

6620	

6621	“I know that. But there warn’t any other place as handy after that fool

6622	of a job. I want to quit this shanty. I wanted to yesterday, only it

6623	warn’t any use trying to stir out of here, with those infernal boys

6624	playing over there on the hill right in full view.”

6625	

6626	“Those infernal boys” quaked again under the inspiration of this remark,

6627	and thought how lucky it was that they had remembered it was Friday and

6628	concluded to wait a day. They wished in their hearts they had waited a

6629	year.

6630	

6631	The two men got out some food and made a luncheon. After a long and

6632	thoughtful silence, Injun Joe said:

6633	

6634	“Look here, lad—you go back up the river where you belong. Wait there

6635	till you hear from me. I’ll take the chances on dropping into this town

6636	just once more, for a look. We’ll do that ‘dangerous’ job after I’ve

6637	spied around a little and think things look well for it. Then for Texas!

6638	We’ll leg it together!”

6639	

6640	This was satisfactory. Both men presently fell to yawning, and Injun Joe

6641	said:

6642	

6643	“I’m dead for sleep! It’s your turn to watch.”

6644	

6645	He curled down in the weeds and soon began to snore. His comrade stirred

6646	him once or twice and he became quiet. Presently the watcher began to

6647	nod; his head drooped lower and lower, both men began to snore now.

6648	

6649	The boys drew a long, grateful breath. Tom whispered:

6650	

6651	“Now’s our chance—come!”

6652	

6653	Huck said:

6654	

6655	“I can’t—I’d die if they was to wake.”

6656	

6657	Tom urged—Huck held back. At last Tom rose slowly and softly, and

6658	started alone. But the first step he made wrung such a hideous creak
