6659	from the crazy floor that he sank down almost dead with fright. He never

6660	made a second attempt. The boys lay there counting the dragging moments

6661	till it seemed to them that time must be done and eternity growing gray;

6662	and then they were grateful to note that at last the sun was setting.

6663	

6664	Now one snore ceased. Injun Joe sat up, stared around—smiled grimly upon

6665	his comrade, whose head was drooping upon his knees—stirred him up with

6666	his foot and said:

6667	

6668	“Here! _You’re_ a watchman, ain’t you! All right, though—nothing’s

6669	happened.”

6670	

6671	“My! have I been asleep?”

6672	

6673	“Oh, partly, partly. Nearly time for us to be moving, pard. What’ll we

6674	do with what little swag we’ve got left?”

6675	

6676	“I don’t know—leave it here as we’ve always done, I reckon. No use to

6677	take it away till we start south. Six hundred and fifty in silver’s

6678	something to carry.”

6679	

6680	“Well—all right—it won’t matter to come here once more.”

6681	

6682	“No—but I’d say come in the night as we used to do—it’s better.”

6683	

6684	“Yes: but look here; it may be a good while before I get the right

6685	chance at that job; accidents might happen; ’tain’t in such a very good

6686	place; we’ll just regularly bury it—and bury it deep.”

6687	

6688	“Good idea,” said the comrade, who walked across the room, knelt down,

6689	raised one of the rearward hearth-stones and took out a bag that jingled

6690	pleasantly. He subtracted from it twenty or thirty dollars for himself

6691	and as much for Injun Joe, and passed the bag to the latter, who was on

6692	his knees in the corner, now, digging with his bowie-knife.

6693	

6694	The boys forgot all their fears, all their miseries in an instant. With

6695	gloating eyes they watched every movement. Luck!—the splendor of it was

6696	beyond all imagination! Six hundred dollars was money enough to make

6697	half a dozen boys rich! Here was treasure-hunting under the happiest

6698	auspices—there would not be any bothersome uncertainty as to where to

6699	dig. They nudged each other every moment—eloquent nudges and easily

6700	understood, for they simply meant—“Oh, but ain’t you glad _now_ we’re

6701	here!”

6702	

6703	Joe’s knife struck upon something.

6704	

6705	“Hello!” said he.

6706	

6707	“What is it?” said his comrade.

6708	

6709	“Half-rotten plank—no, it’s a box, I believe. Here—bear a hand and we’ll

6710	see what it’s here for. Never mind, I’ve broke a hole.”

6711	

6712	He reached his hand in and drew it out—

6713	

6714	“Man, it’s money!”

6715	

6716	The two men examined the handful of coins. They were gold. The boys

6717	above were as excited as themselves, and as delighted.

6718	

6719	Joe’s comrade said:

6720	

6721	“We’ll make quick work of this. There’s an old rusty pick over amongst

6722	the weeds in the corner the other side of the fireplace—I saw it a

6723	minute ago.”

6724	

6725	He ran and brought the boys’ pick and shovel. Injun Joe took the

6726	pick, looked it over critically, shook his head, muttered something to

6727	himself, and then began to use it. The box was soon unearthed. It was

6728	not very large; it was iron bound and had been very strong before the

6729	slow years had injured it. The men contemplated the treasure awhile in

6730	blissful silence.

6731	

6732	“Pard, there’s thousands of dollars here,” said Injun Joe.

6733	

6734	“’Twas always said that Murrel’s gang used to be around here one

6735	summer,” the stranger observed.

6736	

6737	“I know it,” said Injun Joe; “and this looks like it, I should say.”

6738	

6739	“Now you won’t need to do that job.”

6740	

6741	The halfbreed frowned. Said he:

6742	

6743	“You don’t know me. Least you don’t know all about that thing. ’Tain’t

6744	robbery altogether—it’s _revenge_!” and a wicked light flamed in his

6745	eyes. “I’ll need your help in it. When it’s finished—then Texas. Go home

6746	to your Nance and your kids, and stand by till you hear from me.”

6747	

6748	“Well—if you say so; what’ll we do with this—bury it again?”

6749	

6750	“Yes. [Ravishing delight overhead.] _No_! by the great Sachem, no!

6751	[Profound distress overhead.] I’d nearly forgot. That pick had fresh

6752	earth on it! [The boys were sick with terror in a moment.] What business

6753	has a pick and a shovel here? What business with fresh earth on

6754	them? Who brought them here—and where are they gone? Have you heard

6755	anybody?—seen anybody? What! bury it again and leave them to come and

6756	see the ground disturbed? Not exactly—not exactly. We’ll take it to my

6757	den.”

6758	

6759	“Why, of course! Might have thought of that before. You mean Number

6760	One?”

6761	

6762	“No—Number Two—under the cross. The other place is bad—too common.”

6763	

6764	“All right. It’s nearly dark enough to start.”

6765	

6766	Injun Joe got up and went about from window to window cautiously peeping

6767	out. Presently he said:

6768	

6769	“Who could have brought those tools here? Do you reckon they can be

6770	upstairs?”

6771	

6772	The boys’ breath forsook them. Injun Joe put his hand on his knife,

6773	halted a moment, undecided, and then turned toward the stairway. The

6774	boys thought of the closet, but their strength was gone. The steps came

6775	creaking up the stairs—the intolerable distress of the situation woke

6776	the stricken resolution of the lads—they were about to spring for the

6777	closet, when there was a crash of rotten timbers and Injun Joe landed on

6778	the ground amid the debris of the ruined stairway. He gathered himself

6779	up cursing, and his comrade said:

6780	

6781	“Now what’s the use of all that? If it’s anybody, and they’re up there,

6782	let them _stay_ there—who cares? If they want to jump down, now, and get

6783	into trouble, who objects? It will be dark in fifteen minutes—and then

6784	let them follow us if they want to. I’m willing. In my opinion, whoever

6785	hove those things in here caught a sight of us and took us for ghosts or

6786	devils or something. I’ll bet they’re running yet.”

6787	

6788	Joe grumbled awhile; then he agreed with his friend that what daylight

6789	was left ought to be economized in getting things ready for leaving.

6790	Shortly afterward they slipped out of the house in the deepening

6791	twilight, and moved toward the river with their precious box.

6792	

6793	Tom and Huck rose up, weak but vastly relieved, and stared after them

6794	through the chinks between the logs of the house. Follow? Not they. They

6795	were content to reach ground again without broken necks, and take the

6796	townward track over the hill. They did not talk much. They were too much

6797	absorbed in hating themselves—hating the ill luck that made them take

6798	the spade and the pick there. But for that, Injun Joe never would have

6799	suspected. He would have hidden the silver with the gold to wait

6800	there till his “revenge” was satisfied, and then he would have had the

6801	misfortune to find that money turn up missing. Bitter, bitter luck that

6802	the tools were ever brought there!

6803	

6804	They resolved to keep a lookout for that Spaniard when he should come to

6805	town spying out for chances to do his revengeful job, and follow him to

6806	“Number Two,” wherever that might be. Then a ghastly thought occurred to

6807	Tom.

6808	

6809	“Revenge? What if he means _us_, Huck!”

6810	

6811	“Oh, don’t!” said Huck, nearly fainting.

6812	

6813	They talked it all over, and as they entered town they agreed to believe

6814	that he might possibly mean somebody else—at least that he might at

6815	least mean nobody but Tom, since only Tom had testified.

6816	

6817	Very, very small comfort it was to Tom to be alone in danger! Company

6818	would be a palpable improvement, he thought.

6819	

6820	

6821	

6822	

6823	CHAPTER XXVII

6824	

6825	

6826	THE adventure of the day mightily tormented Tom’s dreams that night.

6827	Four times he had his hands on that rich treasure and four times

6828	it wasted to nothingness in his fingers as sleep forsook him and

6829	wakefulness brought back the hard reality of his misfortune. As he lay

6830	in the early morning recalling the incidents of his great adventure, he

6831	noticed that they seemed curiously subdued and far away—somewhat as if

6832	they had happened in another world, or in a time long gone by. Then it

6833	occurred to him that the great adventure itself must be a dream! There

6834	was one very strong argument in favor of this idea—namely, that the

6835	quantity of coin he had seen was too vast to be real. He had never seen

6836	as much as fifty dollars in one mass before, and he was like all boys of

6837	his age and station in life, in that he imagined that all references to

6838	“hundreds” and “thousands” were mere fanciful forms of speech, and that

6839	no such sums really existed in the world. He never had supposed for

6840	a moment that so large a sum as a hundred dollars was to be found in

6841	actual money in any one’s possession. If his notions of hidden treasure

6842	had been analyzed, they would have been found to consist of a handful of

6843	real dimes and a bushel of vague, splendid, ungraspable dollars.

6844	

6845	But the incidents of his adventure grew sensibly sharper and clearer

6846	under the attrition of thinking them over, and so he presently found

6847	himself leaning to the impression that the thing might not have been a

6848	dream, after all. This uncertainty must be swept away. He would snatch a

6849	hurried breakfast and go and find Huck. Huck was sitting on the gunwale

6850	of a flatboat, listlessly dangling his feet in the water and looking

6851	very melancholy. Tom concluded to let Huck lead up to the subject. If

6852	he did not do it, then the adventure would be proved to have been only a

6853	dream.

6854	

6855	“Hello, Huck!”

6856	

6857	“Hello, yourself.”

6858	

6859	Silence, for a minute.

6860	

6861	“Tom, if we’d ’a’ left the blame tools at the dead tree, we’d ’a’ got

6862	the money. Oh, ain’t it awful!”

6863	

6864	“’Tain’t a dream, then, ’tain’t a dream! Somehow I most wish it was.

6865	Dog’d if I don’t, Huck.”

6866	

6867	“What ain’t a dream?”

6868	

6869	“Oh, that thing yesterday. I been half thinking it was.”

6870	

6871	“Dream! If them stairs hadn’t broke down you’d ’a’ seen how much dream

6872	it was! I’ve had dreams enough all night—with that patch-eyed Spanish

6873	devil going for me all through ’em—rot him!”

6874	

6875	“No, not rot him. _Find_ him! Track the money!”

6876	

6877	“Tom, we’ll never find him. A feller don’t have only one chance for such

6878	a pile—and that one’s lost. I’d feel mighty shaky if I was to see him,

6879	anyway.”

6880	

6881	“Well, so’d I; but I’d like to see him, anyway—and track him out—to his

6882	Number Two.”

6883	

6884	“Number Two—yes, that’s it. I been thinking ’bout that. But I can’t make

6885	nothing out of it. What do you reckon it is?”

6886	

6887	“I dono. It’s too deep. Say, Huck—maybe it’s the number of a house!”

6888	

6889	“Goody!... No, Tom, that ain’t it. If it is, it ain’t in this one-horse

6890	town. They ain’t no numbers here.”

6891	

6892	“Well, that’s so. Lemme think a minute. Here—it’s the number of a

6893	room—in a tavern, you know!”

6894	

6895	“Oh, that’s the trick! They ain’t only two taverns. We can find out

6896	quick.”

6897	

6898	“You stay here, Huck, till I come.”

6899	

6900	Tom was off at once. He did not care to have Huck’s company in public

6901	places. He was gone half an hour. He found that in the best tavern, No.

6902	2 had long been occupied by a young lawyer, and was still so occupied.

6903	In the less ostentatious house, No. 2 was a mystery. The tavern-keeper’s

6904	young son said it was kept locked all the time, and he never saw anybody

6905	go into it or come out of it except at night; he did not know any

6906	particular reason for this state of things; had had some little

6907	curiosity, but it was rather feeble; had made the most of the mystery

6908	by entertaining himself with the idea that that room was “ha’nted”; had

6909	noticed that there was a light in there the night before.

6910	

6911	“That’s what I’ve found out, Huck. I reckon that’s the very No. 2 we’re

6912	after.”

6913	

6914	“I reckon it is, Tom. Now what you going to do?”

6915	

6916	“Lemme think.”

6917	

6918	Tom thought a long time. Then he said:

6919	

6920	“I’ll tell you. The back door of that No. 2 is the door that comes out

6921	into that little close alley between the tavern and the old rattle trap

6922	of a brick store. Now you get hold of all the doorkeys you can find, and

6923	I’ll nip all of auntie’s, and the first dark night we’ll go there and

6924	try ’em. And mind you, keep a lookout for Injun Joe, because he said he

6925	was going to drop into town and spy around once more for a chance to get

6926	his revenge. If you see him, you just follow him; and if he don’t go to

6927	that No. 2, that ain’t the place.”

6928	

6929	“Lordy, I don’t want to foller him by myself!”

6930	

6931	“Why, it’ll be night, sure. He mightn’t ever see you—and if he did,

6932	maybe he’d never think anything.”

6933	

6934	“Well, if it’s pretty dark I reckon I’ll track him. I dono—I dono. I’ll

6935	try.”

6936	

6937	“You bet I’ll follow him, if it’s dark, Huck. Why, he might ’a’ found

6938	out he couldn’t get his revenge, and be going right after that money.”

6939	

6940	“It’s so, Tom, it’s so. I’ll foller him; I will, by jingoes!”

6941	

6942	“Now you’re _talking_! Don’t you ever weaken, Huck, and I won’t.”

6943	

6944	

6945	

6946	

6947	CHAPTER XXVIII

6948	

6949	

6950	THAT night Tom and Huck were ready for their adventure. They hung about

6951	the neighborhood of the tavern until after nine, one watching the alley

6952	at a distance and the other the tavern door. Nobody entered the alley or

6953	left it; nobody resembling the Spaniard entered or left the tavern

6954	door. The night promised to be a fair one; so Tom went home with the

6955	understanding that if a considerable degree of darkness came on, Huck

6956	was to come and “maow,” whereupon he would slip out and try the keys.

6957	But the night remained clear, and Huck closed his watch and retired to

6958	bed in an empty sugar hogshead about twelve.

6959	

6960	Tuesday the boys had the same ill luck. Also Wednesday. But Thursday

6961	night promised better. Tom slipped out in good season with his aunt’s

6962	old tin lantern, and a large towel to blindfold it with. He hid the

6963	lantern in Huck’s sugar hogshead and the watch began. An hour before

6964	midnight the tavern closed up and its lights (the only ones thereabouts)

6965	were put out. No Spaniard had been seen. Nobody had entered or left the

6966	alley. Everything was auspicious. The blackness of darkness reigned,

6967	the perfect stillness was interrupted only by occasional mutterings of

6968	distant thunder.

6969	

6970	Tom got his lantern, lit it in the hogshead, wrapped it closely in the

6971	towel, and the two adventurers crept in the gloom toward the tavern.

6972	Huck stood sentry and Tom felt his way into the alley. Then there was

6973	a season of waiting anxiety that weighed upon Huck’s spirits like a

6974	mountain. He began to wish he could see a flash from the lantern—it

6975	would frighten him, but it would at least tell him that Tom was alive

6976	yet. It seemed hours since Tom had disappeared. Surely he must have

6977	fainted; maybe he was dead; maybe his heart had burst under terror and

6978	excitement. In his uneasiness Huck found himself drawing closer

6979	and closer to the alley; fearing all sorts of dreadful things, and

6980	momentarily expecting some catastrophe to happen that would take away

6981	his breath. There was not much to take away, for he seemed only able to

6982	inhale it by thimblefuls, and his heart would soon wear itself out, the

6983	way it was beating. Suddenly there was a flash of light and Tom came

6984	tearing by him: “Run!” said he; “run, for your life!”

6985	

6986	He needn’t have repeated it; once was enough; Huck was making thirty or

6987	forty miles an hour before the repetition was uttered. The boys never

6988	stopped till they reached the shed of a deserted slaughter-house at the

6989	lower end of the village. Just as they got within its shelter the storm

6990	burst and the rain poured down. As soon as Tom got his breath he said:

6991	

6992	“Huck, it was awful! I tried two of the keys, just as soft as I could;

6993	but they seemed to make such a power of racket that I couldn’t hardly

6994	get my breath I was so scared. They wouldn’t turn in the lock, either.

6995	Well, without noticing what I was doing, I took hold of the knob, and

6996	open comes the door! It warn’t locked! I hopped in, and shook off the

6997	towel, and, _Great Caesar’s Ghost!_”

6998	

6999	“What!—what’d you see, Tom?”

7000	

7001	“Huck, I most stepped onto Injun Joe’s hand!”

7002	

7003	“No!”

7004	

7005	“Yes! He was lying there, sound asleep on the floor, with his old patch

7006	on his eye and his arms spread out.”

7007	

7008	“Lordy, what did you do? Did he wake up?”

7009	

7010	“No, never budged. Drunk, I reckon. I just grabbed that towel and

7011	started!”

7012	

7013	“I’d never ’a’ thought of the towel, I bet!”

7014	

7015	“Well, I would. My aunt would make me mighty sick if I lost it.”

7016	

7017	“Say, Tom, did you see that box?”

7018	

7019	“Huck, I didn’t wait to look around. I didn’t see the box, I didn’t see

7020	the cross. I didn’t see anything but a bottle and a tin cup on the floor

7021	by Injun Joe; yes, I saw two barrels and lots more bottles in the room.

7022	Don’t you see, now, what’s the matter with that ha’nted room?”

7023	

7024	“How?”

7025	

7026	“Why, it’s ha’nted with whiskey! Maybe _all_ the Temperance Taverns have

7027	got a ha’nted room, hey, Huck?”

7028	

7029	“Well, I reckon maybe that’s so. Who’d ’a’ thought such a thing? But

7030	say, Tom, now’s a mighty good time to get that box, if Injun Joe’s

7031	drunk.”

7032	

7033	“It is, that! You try it!”

7034	

7035	Huck shuddered.

7036	

7037	“Well, no—I reckon not.”

7038	

7039	“And I reckon not, Huck. Only one bottle alongside of Injun Joe ain’t

7040	enough. If there’d been three, he’d be drunk enough and I’d do it.”

7041	

7042	There was a long pause for reflection, and then Tom said:

7043	

7044	“Lookyhere, Huck, less not try that thing any more till we know Injun

7045	Joe’s not in there. It’s too scary. Now, if we watch every night, we’ll

7046	be dead sure to see him go out, some time or other, and then we’ll

7047	snatch that box quicker’n lightning.”

7048	

7049	“Well, I’m agreed. I’ll watch the whole night long, and I’ll do it every

7050	night, too, if you’ll do the other part of the job.”

7051	

7052	“All right, I will. All you got to do is to trot up Hooper Street a

7053	block and maow—and if I’m asleep, you throw some gravel at the window

7054	and that’ll fetch me.”

7055	

7056	“Agreed, and good as wheat!”

7057	

7058	“Now, Huck, the storm’s over, and I’ll go home. It’ll begin to be

7059	daylight in a couple of hours. You go back and watch that long, will

7060	you?”

7061	

7062	“I said I would, Tom, and I will. I’ll ha’nt that tavern every night for

7063	a year! I’ll sleep all day and I’ll stand watch all night.”

7064	

7065	“That’s all right. Now, where you going to sleep?”

7066	

7067	“In Ben Rogers’ hayloft. He lets me, and so does his pap’s nigger man,

7068	Uncle Jake. I tote water for Uncle Jake whenever he wants me to, and any

7069	time I ask him he gives me a little something to eat if he can spare it.

7070	That’s a mighty good nigger, Tom. He likes me, becuz I don’t ever act as

7071	if I was above him. Sometime I’ve set right down and eat _with_ him. But

7072	you needn’t tell that. A body’s got to do things when he’s awful hungry

7073	he wouldn’t want to do as a steady thing.”

7074	

7075	“Well, if I don’t want you in the daytime, I’ll let you sleep. I won’t

7076	come bothering around. Any time you see something’s up, in the night,

7077	just skip right around and maow.”

7078	

7079	

7080	

7081	

7082	CHAPTER XXIX

7083	

7084	

7085	THE first thing Tom heard on Friday morning was a glad piece of

7086	news—Judge Thatcher’s family had come back to town the night before.

7087	Both Injun Joe and the treasure sunk into secondary importance for a

7088	moment, and Becky took the chief place in the boy’s interest. He saw her

7089	and they had an exhausting good time playing “hispy” and “gully-keeper”

7090	 with a crowd of their schoolmates. The day was completed and crowned in

7091	a peculiarly satisfactory way: Becky teased her mother to appoint

7092	the next day for the long-promised and long-delayed picnic, and she

7093	consented. The child’s delight was boundless; and Tom’s not more

7094	moderate. The invitations were sent out before sunset, and straightway

7095	the young folks of the village were thrown into a fever of preparation

7096	and pleasurable anticipation. Tom’s excitement enabled him to keep

7097	awake until a pretty late hour, and he had good hopes of hearing Huck’s

7098	“maow,” and of having his treasure to astonish Becky and the picnickers

7099	with, next day; but he was disappointed. No signal came that night.

7100	

7101	Morning came, eventually, and by ten or eleven o’clock a giddy and

7102	rollicking company were gathered at Judge Thatcher’s, and everything was

7103	ready for a start. It was not the custom for elderly people to mar the

7104	picnics with their presence. The children were considered safe enough

7105	under the wings of a few young ladies of eighteen and a few young

7106	gentlemen of twenty-three or thereabouts. The old steam ferry-boat was

7107	chartered for the occasion; presently the gay throng filed up the main

7108	street laden with provision-baskets. Sid was sick and had to miss

7109	the fun; Mary remained at home to entertain him. The last thing Mrs.

7110	Thatcher said to Becky, was:

7111	

7112	“You’ll not get back till late. Perhaps you’d better stay all night with

7113	some of the girls that live near the ferry-landing, child.”

7114	

7115	“Then I’ll stay with Susy Harper, mamma.”

7116	

7117	“Very well. And mind and behave yourself and don’t be any trouble.”

7118	

7119	Presently, as they tripped along, Tom said to Becky:

7120	

7121	“Say—I’ll tell you what we’ll do. ’Stead of going to Joe Harper’s we’ll

7122	climb right up the hill and stop at the Widow Douglas’. She’ll have

7123	ice-cream! She has it most every day—dead loads of it. And she’ll be

7124	awful glad to have us.”

7125	

7126	“Oh, that will be fun!”

7127	

7128	Then Becky reflected a moment and said:

7129	

7130	“But what will mamma say?”

7131	

7132	“How’ll she ever know?”

7133	

7134	The girl turned the idea over in her mind, and said reluctantly:

7135	

7136	“I reckon it’s wrong—but—”

7137	

7138	“But shucks! Your mother won’t know, and so what’s the harm? All she

7139	wants is that you’ll be safe; and I bet you she’d ’a’ said go there if

7140	she’d ’a’ thought of it. I know she would!”

7141	

7142	The Widow Douglas’ splendid hospitality was a tempting bait. It and

7143	Tom’s persuasions presently carried the day. So it was decided to say

7144	nothing to anybody about the night’s programme. Presently it occurred to

7145	Tom that maybe Huck might come this very night and give the signal. The

7146	thought took a deal of the spirit out of his anticipations. Still he

7147	could not bear to give up the fun at Widow Douglas’. And why should he

7148	give it up, he reasoned—the signal did not come the night before, so

7149	why should it be any more likely to come tonight? The sure fun of the

7150	evening outweighed the uncertain treasure; and, boy-like, he determined

7151	to yield to the stronger inclination and not allow himself to think of

7152	the box of money another time that day.

7153	

7154	Three miles below town the ferryboat stopped at the mouth of a woody

7155	hollow and tied up. The crowd swarmed ashore and soon the forest

7156	distances and craggy heights echoed far and near with shoutings and

7157	laughter. All the different ways of getting hot and tired were gone

7158	through with, and by-and-by the rovers straggled back to camp fortified

7159	with responsible appetites, and then the destruction of the good things

7160	began. After the feast there was a refreshing season of rest and chat in

7161	the shade of spreading oaks. By-and-by somebody shouted:

7162	

7163	“Who’s ready for the cave?”

7164	

7165	Everybody was. Bundles of candles were procured, and straightway there

7166	was a general scamper up the hill. The mouth of the cave was up the

7167	hillside—an opening shaped like a letter A. Its massive oaken door stood

7168	unbarred. Within was a small chamber, chilly as an icehouse, and walled

7169	by Nature with solid limestone that was dewy with a cold sweat. It was

7170	romantic and mysterious to stand here in the deep gloom and look out

7171	upon the green valley shining in the sun. But the impressiveness of the

7172	situation quickly wore off, and the romping began again. The moment

7173	a candle was lighted there was a general rush upon the owner of it; a

7174	struggle and a gallant defence followed, but the candle was soon knocked

7175	down or blown out, and then there was a glad clamor of laughter and a

7176	new chase. But all things have an end. By-and-by the procession went

7177	filing down the steep descent of the main avenue, the flickering rank of

7178	lights dimly revealing the lofty walls of rock almost to their point of

7179	junction sixty feet overhead. This main avenue was not more than

7180	eight or ten feet wide. Every few steps other lofty and still narrower

7181	crevices branched from it on either hand—for McDougal’s cave was but a

7182	vast labyrinth of crooked aisles that ran into each other and out again

7183	and led nowhere. It was said that one might wander days and nights

7184	together through its intricate tangle of rifts and chasms, and never

7185	find the end of the cave; and that he might go down, and down, and

7186	still down, into the earth, and it was just the same—labyrinth under

7187	labyrinth, and no end to any of them. No man “knew” the cave. That was

7188	an impossible thing. Most of the young men knew a portion of it, and it

7189	was not customary to venture much beyond this known portion. Tom Sawyer

7190	knew as much of the cave as any one.

7191	

7192	The procession moved along the main avenue some three-quarters of

7193	a mile, and then groups and couples began to slip aside into branch

7194	avenues, fly along the dismal corridors, and take each other by surprise

7195	at points where the corridors joined again. Parties were able to elude

7196	each other for the space of half an hour without going beyond the

7197	“known” ground.

7198	

7199	By-and-by, one group after another came straggling back to the mouth

7200	of the cave, panting, hilarious, smeared from head to foot with tallow

7201	drippings, daubed with clay, and entirely delighted with the success of

7202	the day. Then they were astonished to find that they had been taking

7203	no note of time and that night was about at hand. The clanging bell had

7204	been calling for half an hour. However, this sort of close to the day’s

7205	adventures was romantic and therefore satisfactory. When the ferryboat

7206	with her wild freight pushed into the stream, nobody cared sixpence for

7207	the wasted time but the captain of the craft.

7208	

7209	Huck was already upon his watch when the ferryboat’s lights went

7210	glinting past the wharf. He heard no noise on board, for the young

7211	people were as subdued and still as people usually are who are nearly

7212	tired to death. He wondered what boat it was, and why she did not

7213	stop at the wharf—and then he dropped her out of his mind and put his

7214	attention upon his business. The night was growing cloudy and dark. Ten

7215	o’clock came, and the noise of vehicles ceased, scattered lights began

7216	to wink out, all straggling foot-passengers disappeared, the village

7217	betook itself to its slumbers and left the small watcher alone with the

7218	silence and the ghosts. Eleven o’clock came, and the tavern lights were

7219	put out; darkness everywhere, now. Huck waited what seemed a weary long

7220	time, but nothing happened. His faith was weakening. Was there any use?

7221	Was there really any use? Why not give it up and turn in?

7222	

7223	A noise fell upon his ear. He was all attention in an instant. The alley

7224	door closed softly. He sprang to the corner of the brick store. The next

7225	moment two men brushed by him, and one seemed to have something under

7226	his arm. It must be that box! So they were going to remove the treasure.

7227	Why call Tom now? It would be absurd—the men would get away with the box

7228	and never be found again. No, he would stick to their wake and follow

7229	them; he would trust to the darkness for security from discovery. So

7230	communing with himself, Huck stepped out and glided along behind the

7231	men, cat-like, with bare feet, allowing them to keep just far enough

7232	ahead not to be invisible.

7233	

7234	They moved up the river street three blocks, then turned to the left up

7235	a crossstreet. They went straight ahead, then, until they came to the

7236	path that led up Cardiff Hill; this they took. They passed by the old

7237	Welshman’s house, halfway up the hill, without hesitating, and still

7238	climbed upward. Good, thought Huck, they will bury it in the old quarry.

7239	But they never stopped at the quarry. They passed on, up the summit.

7240	They plunged into the narrow path between the tall sumach bushes, and

7241	were at once hidden in the gloom. Huck closed up and shortened his

7242	distance, now, for they would never be able to see him. He trotted along

7243	awhile; then slackened his pace, fearing he was gaining too fast; moved

7244	on a piece, then stopped altogether; listened; no sound; none, save that

7245	he seemed to hear the beating of his own heart. The hooting of an

7246	owl came over the hill—ominous sound! But no footsteps. Heavens, was

7247	everything lost! He was about to spring with winged feet, when a man

7248	cleared his throat not four feet from him! Huck’s heart shot into his

7249	throat, but he swallowed it again; and then he stood there shaking as

7250	if a dozen agues had taken charge of him at once, and so weak that he

7251	thought he must surely fall to the ground. He knew where he was. He

7252	knew he was within five steps of the stile leading into Widow Douglas’

7253	grounds. Very well, he thought, let them bury it there; it won’t be hard

7254	to find.

7255	

7256	Now there was a voice—a very low voice—Injun Joe’s:

7257	

7258	“Damn her, maybe she’s got company—there’s lights, late as it is.”

7259	

7260	“I can’t see any.”

7261	

7262	This was that stranger’s voice—the stranger of the haunted house. A

7263	deadly chill went to Huck’s heart—this, then, was the “revenge” job! His

7264	thought was, to fly. Then he remembered that the Widow Douglas had been

7265	kind to him more than once, and maybe these men were going to murder

7266	her. He wished he dared venture to warn her; but he knew he didn’t

7267	dare—they might come and catch him. He thought all this and more in

7268	the moment that elapsed between the stranger’s remark and Injun Joe’s

7269	next—which was—

7270	

7271	“Because the bush is in your way. Now—this way—now you see, don’t you?”

7272	

7273	“Yes. Well, there _is_ company there, I reckon. Better give it up.”

7274	

7275	“Give it up, and I just leaving this country forever! Give it up and

7276	maybe never have another chance. I tell you again, as I’ve told you

7277	before, I don’t care for her swag—you may have it. But her husband was

7278	rough on me—many times he was rough on me—and mainly he was the justice

7279	of the peace that jugged me for a vagrant. And that ain’t all. It ain’t

7280	a millionth part of it! He had me _horsewhipped_!—horsewhipped in

7281	front of the jail, like a nigger!—with all the town looking on!

7282	_Horsewhipped_!—do you understand? He took advantage of me and died. But

7283	I’ll take it out of _her_.”

7284	

7285	“Oh, don’t kill her! Don’t do that!”

7286	

7287	“Kill? Who said anything about killing? I would kill _him_ if he was

7288	here; but not her. When you want to get revenge on a woman you don’t

7289	kill her—bosh! you go for her looks. You slit her nostrils—you notch her

7290	ears like a sow!”

7291	

7292	“By God, that’s—”

7293	

7294	“Keep your opinion to yourself! It will be safest for you. I’ll tie her

7295	to the bed. If she bleeds to death, is that my fault? I’ll not cry, if

7296	she does. My friend, you’ll help me in this thing—for _my_ sake—that’s

7297	why you’re here—I mightn’t be able alone. If you flinch, I’ll kill you.

7298	Do you understand that? And if I have to kill you, I’ll kill her—and

7299	then I reckon nobody’ll ever know much about who done this business.”

7300	

7301	“Well, if it’s got to be done, let’s get at it. The quicker the

7302	better—I’m all in a shiver.”

7303	

7304	“Do it _now_? And company there? Look here—I’ll get suspicious of you,

7305	first thing you know. No—we’ll wait till the lights are out—there’s no

7306	hurry.”

7307	

7308	Huck felt that a silence was going to ensue—a thing still more awful

7309	than any amount of murderous talk; so he held his breath and stepped

7310	gingerly back; planted his foot carefully and firmly, after balancing,

7311	one-legged, in a precarious way and almost toppling over, first on one

7312	side and then on the other. He took another step back, with the same

7313	elaboration and the same risks; then another and another, and—a twig

7314	snapped under his foot! His breath stopped and he listened. There was no

7315	sound—the stillness was perfect. His gratitude was measureless. Now he

7316	turned in his tracks, between the walls of sumach bushes—turned

7317	himself as carefully as if he were a ship—and then stepped quickly but

7318	cautiously along. When he emerged at the quarry he felt secure, and

7319	so he picked up his nimble heels and flew. Down, down he sped, till he

7320	reached the Welshman’s. He banged at the door, and presently the heads

7321	of the old man and his two stalwart sons were thrust from windows.

7322	

7323	“What’s the row there? Who’s banging? What do you want?”

7324	

7325	“Let me in—quick! I’ll tell everything.”

7326	

7327	“Why, who are you?”

7328	

7329	“Huckleberry Finn—quick, let me in!”

7330	

7331	“Huckleberry Finn, indeed! It ain’t a name to open many doors, I judge!

7332	But let him in, lads, and let’s see what’s the trouble.”

7333	

7334	“Please don’t ever tell I told you,” were Huck’s first words when he got

7335	in. “Please don’t—I’d be killed, sure—but the widow’s been good friends

7336	to me sometimes, and I want to tell—I _will_ tell if you’ll promise you

7337	won’t ever say it was me.”

7338	

7339	“By George, he _has_ got something to tell, or he wouldn’t act so!”

7340	 exclaimed the old man; “out with it and nobody here’ll ever tell, lad.”

7341	

7342	Three minutes later the old man and his sons, well armed, were up the

7343	hill, and just entering the sumach path on tiptoe, their weapons in

7344	their hands. Huck accompanied them no further. He hid behind a great

7345	bowlder and fell to listening. There was a lagging, anxious silence, and

7346	then all of a sudden there was an explosion of firearms and a cry.

7347	

7348	Huck waited for no particulars. He sprang away and sped down the hill as

7349	fast as his legs could carry him.

7350	

7351	

7352	

7353	

7354	CHAPTER XXX

7355	

7356	

7357	AS the earliest suspicion of dawn appeared on Sunday morning, Huck came

7358	groping up the hill and rapped gently at the old Welshman’s door. The

7359	inmates were asleep, but it was a sleep that was set on a hair-trigger,

7360	on account of the exciting episode of the night. A call came from a

7361	window:

7362	

7363	“Who’s there!”

7364	

7365	Huck’s scared voice answered in a low tone:

7366	

7367	“Please let me in! It’s only Huck Finn!”

7368	

7369	“It’s a name that can open this door night or day, lad!—and welcome!”

7370	

7371	These were strange words to the vagabond boy’s ears, and the pleasantest

7372	he had ever heard. He could not recollect that the closing word had ever

7373	been applied in his case before. The door was quickly unlocked, and he

7374	entered. Huck was given a seat and the old man and his brace of tall

7375	sons speedily dressed themselves.

7376	

7377	“Now, my boy, I hope you’re good and hungry, because breakfast will be

7378	ready as soon as the sun’s up, and we’ll have a piping hot one, too—make

7379	yourself easy about that! I and the boys hoped you’d turn up and stop

7380	here last night.”

7381	

7382	“I was awful scared,” said Huck, “and I run. I took out when the pistols

7383	went off, and I didn’t stop for three mile. I’ve come now becuz I wanted

7384	to know about it, you know; and I come before daylight becuz I didn’t

7385	want to run across them devils, even if they was dead.”

7386	

7387	“Well, poor chap, you do look as if you’d had a hard night of it—but

7388	there’s a bed here for you when you’ve had your breakfast. No, they

7389	ain’t dead, lad—we are sorry enough for that. You see we knew right

7390	where to put our hands on them, by your description; so we crept along

7391	on tiptoe till we got within fifteen feet of them—dark as a cellar that

7392	sumach path was—and just then I found I was going to sneeze. It was the

7393	meanest kind of luck! I tried to keep it back, but no use—’twas bound to

7394	come, and it did come! I was in the lead with my pistol raised, and when

7395	the sneeze started those scoundrels a-rustling to get out of the path,

7396	I sung out, ‘Fire boys!’ and blazed away at the place where the rustling

7397	was. So did the boys. But they were off in a jiffy, those villains, and

7398	we after them, down through the woods. I judge we never touched them.

7399	They fired a shot apiece as they started, but their bullets whizzed by

7400	and didn’t do us any harm. As soon as we lost the sound of their feet

7401	we quit chasing, and went down and stirred up the constables. They got a

7402	posse together, and went off to guard the river bank, and as soon as it

7403	is light the sheriff and a gang are going to beat up the woods. My boys

7404	will be with them presently. I wish we had some sort of description of

7405	those rascals—’twould help a good deal. But you couldn’t see what they

7406	were like, in the dark, lad, I suppose?”

7407	

7408	“Oh yes; I saw them downtown and follered them.”

7409	

7410	“Splendid! Describe them—describe them, my boy!”

7411	

7412	“One’s the old deaf and dumb Spaniard that’s ben around here once or

7413	twice, and t’other’s a mean-looking, ragged—”

7414	

7415	“That’s enough, lad, we know the men! Happened on them in the woods back

7416	of the widow’s one day, and they slunk away. Off with you, boys, and

7417	tell the sheriff—get your breakfast tomorrow morning!”

7418	

7419	The Welshman’s sons departed at once. As they were leaving the room Huck

7420	sprang up and exclaimed:

7421	

7422	“Oh, please don’t tell _any_body it was me that blowed on them! Oh,

7423	please!”

7424	

7425	“All right if you say it, Huck, but you ought to have the credit of what

7426	you did.”

7427	

7428	“Oh no, no! Please don’t tell!”

7429	

7430	When the young men were gone, the old Welshman said:

7431	

7432	“They won’t tell—and I won’t. But why don’t you want it known?”

7433	

7434	Huck would not explain, further than to say that he already knew too

7435	much about one of those men and would not have the man know that he knew

7436	anything against him for the whole world—he would be killed for knowing

7437	it, sure.

7438	

7439	The old man promised secrecy once more, and said:

7440	

7441	“How did you come to follow these fellows, lad? Were they looking

7442	suspicious?”

7443	

7444	Huck was silent while he framed a duly cautious reply. Then he said:

7445	

7446	“Well, you see, I’m a kind of a hard lot,—least everybody says so, and

7447	I don’t see nothing agin it—and sometimes I can’t sleep much, on account

7448	of thinking about it and sort of trying to strike out a new way of

7449	doing. That was the way of it last night. I couldn’t sleep, and so I

7450	come along upstreet ’bout midnight, a-turning it all over, and when I

7451	got to that old shackly brick store by the Temperance Tavern, I backed

7452	up agin the wall to have another think. Well, just then along comes

7453	these two chaps slipping along close by me, with something under their

7454	arm, and I reckoned they’d stole it. One was a-smoking, and t’other one

7455	wanted a light; so they stopped right before me and the cigars lit up

7456	their faces and I see that the big one was the deaf and dumb Spaniard,

7457	by his white whiskers and the patch on his eye, and t’other one was a

7458	rusty, ragged-looking devil.”

7459	

7460	“Could you see the rags by the light of the cigars?”

7461	

7462	This staggered Huck for a moment. Then he said:

7463	

7464	“Well, I don’t know—but somehow it seems as if I did.”

7465	

7466	“Then they went on, and you—”

7467	

7468	“Follered ’em—yes. That was it. I wanted to see what was up—they sneaked

7469	along so. I dogged ’em to the widder’s stile, and stood in the dark and

7470	heard the ragged one beg for the widder, and the Spaniard swear he’d

7471	spile her looks just as I told you and your two—”

7472	

7473	“What! The _deaf and dumb_ man said all that!”

7474	

7475	Huck had made another terrible mistake! He was trying his best to keep

7476	the old man from getting the faintest hint of who the Spaniard might be,

7477	and yet his tongue seemed determined to get him into trouble in spite of

7478	all he could do. He made several efforts to creep out of his scrape,

7479	but the old man’s eye was upon him and he made blunder after blunder.

7480	Presently the Welshman said:

7481	

7482	“My boy, don’t be afraid of me. I wouldn’t hurt a hair of your head for

7483	all the world. No—I’d protect you—I’d protect you. This Spaniard is

7484	not deaf and dumb; you’ve let that slip without intending it; you can’t

7485	cover that up now. You know something about that Spaniard that you want

7486	to keep dark. Now trust me—tell me what it is, and trust me—I won’t

7487	betray you.”

7488	

7489	Huck looked into the old man’s honest eyes a moment, then bent over and

7490	whispered in his ear:

7491	

7492	“’Tain’t a Spaniard—it’s Injun Joe!”

7493	

7494	The Welshman almost jumped out of his chair. In a moment he said:

7495	

7496	“It’s all plain enough, now. When you talked about notching ears and

7497	slitting noses I judged that that was your own embellishment, because

7498	white men don’t take that sort of revenge. But an Injun! That’s a

7499	different matter altogether.”

7500	

7501	During breakfast the talk went on, and in the course of it the old man

7502	said that the last thing which he and his sons had done, before going

7503	to bed, was to get a lantern and examine the stile and its vicinity for

7504	marks of blood. They found none, but captured a bulky bundle of—

7505	

7506	“Of _what_?”

7507	

7508	If the words had been lightning they could not have leaped with a more

7509	stunning suddenness from Huck’s blanched lips. His eyes were staring

7510	wide, now, and his breath suspended—waiting for the answer. The Welshman

7511	started—stared in return—three seconds—five seconds—ten—then replied:

7512	

7513	“Of burglar’s tools. Why, what’s the _matter_ with you?”

7514	

7515	Huck sank back, panting gently, but deeply, unutterably grateful. The

7516	Welshman eyed him gravely, curiously—and presently said:

7517	

7518	“Yes, burglar’s tools. That appears to relieve you a good deal. But what

7519	did give you that turn? What were _you_ expecting we’d found?”

7520	

7521	Huck was in a close place—the inquiring eye was upon him—he would have

7522	given anything for material for a plausible answer—nothing suggested

7523	itself—the inquiring eye was boring deeper and deeper—a senseless

7524	reply offered—there was no time to weigh it, so at a venture he uttered

7525	it—feebly:

7526	

7527	“Sunday-school books, maybe.”

7528	

7529	Poor Huck was too distressed to smile, but the old man laughed loud and

7530	joyously, shook up the details of his anatomy from head to foot, and

7531	ended by saying that such a laugh was money in a-man’s pocket, because

7532	it cut down the doctor’s bill like everything. Then he added:

7533	

7534	“Poor old chap, you’re white and jaded—you ain’t well a bit—no wonder

7535	you’re a little flighty and off your balance. But you’ll come out of it.

7536	Rest and sleep will fetch you out all right, I hope.”

7537	

7538	Huck was irritated to think he had been such a goose and betrayed such

7539	a suspicious excitement, for he had dropped the idea that the parcel

7540	brought from the tavern was the treasure, as soon as he had heard the

7541	talk at the widow’s stile. He had only thought it was not the treasure,

7542	however—he had not known that it wasn’t—and so the suggestion of a

7543	captured bundle was too much for his self-possession. But on the whole

7544	he felt glad the little episode had happened, for now he knew beyond all

7545	question that that bundle was not _the_ bundle, and so his mind was

7546	at rest and exceedingly comfortable. In fact, everything seemed to be

7547	drifting just in the right direction, now; the treasure must be still

7548	in No. 2, the men would be captured and jailed that day, and he and

7549	Tom could seize the gold that night without any trouble or any fear of

7550	interruption.

7551	

7552	Just as breakfast was completed there was a knock at the door. Huck

7553	jumped for a hiding-place, for he had no mind to be connected even

7554	remotely with the late event. The Welshman admitted several ladies and

7555	gentlemen, among them the Widow Douglas, and noticed that groups of

7556	citizens were climbing up the hill—to stare at the stile. So the news

7557	had spread. The Welshman had to tell the story of the night to the

7558	visitors. The widow’s gratitude for her preservation was outspoken.

7559	

7560	“Don’t say a word about it, madam. There’s another that you’re more

7561	beholden to than you are to me and my boys, maybe, but he don’t allow me

7562	to tell his name. We wouldn’t have been there but for him.”

7563	

7564	Of course this excited a curiosity so vast that it almost belittled the

7565	main matter—but the Welshman allowed it to eat into the vitals of his

7566	visitors, and through them be transmitted to the whole town, for he

7567	refused to part with his secret. When all else had been learned, the

7568	widow said:

7569	

7570	“I went to sleep reading in bed and slept straight through all that

7571	noise. Why didn’t you come and wake me?”

7572	

7573	“We judged it warn’t worth while. Those fellows warn’t likely to come

7574	again—they hadn’t any tools left to work with, and what was the use of

7575	waking you up and scaring you to death? My three negro men stood guard

7576	at your house all the rest of the night. They’ve just come back.”

7577	

7578	More visitors came, and the story had to be told and retold for a couple

7579	of hours more.

7580	

7581	There was no Sabbath-school during day-school vacation, but everybody

7582	was early at church. The stirring event was well canvassed. News came

7583	that not a sign of the two villains had been yet discovered. When the

7584	sermon was finished, Judge Thatcher’s wife dropped alongside of Mrs.

7585	Harper as she moved down the aisle with the crowd and said:

7586	

7587	“Is my Becky going to sleep all day? I just expected she would be tired

7588	to death.”

7589	

7590	“Your Becky?”

7591	

7592	“Yes,” with a startled look—“didn’t she stay with you last night?”

7593	

7594	“Why, no.”

7595	

7596	Mrs. Thatcher turned pale, and sank into a pew, just as Aunt Polly,

7597	talking briskly with a friend, passed by. Aunt Polly said:

7598	

7599	“Goodmorning, Mrs. Thatcher. Goodmorning, Mrs. Harper. I’ve got a boy

7600	that’s turned up missing. I reckon my Tom stayed at your house last

7601	night—one of you. And now he’s afraid to come to church. I’ve got to

7602	settle with him.”

7603	

7604	Mrs. Thatcher shook her head feebly and turned paler than ever.

7605	

7606	“He didn’t stay with us,” said Mrs. Harper, beginning to look uneasy. A

7607	marked anxiety came into Aunt Polly’s face.

7608	

7609	“Joe Harper, have you seen my Tom this morning?”

7610	

7611	“No’m.”

7612	

7613	“When did you see him last?”

7614	

7615	Joe tried to remember, but was not sure he could say. The people had

7616	stopped moving out of church. Whispers passed along, and a boding

7617	uneasiness took possession of every countenance. Children were anxiously

7618	questioned, and young teachers. They all said they had not noticed

7619	whether Tom and Becky were on board the ferryboat on the homeward trip;

7620	it was dark; no one thought of inquiring if any one was missing. One

7621	young man finally blurted out his fear that they were still in the cave!

7622	Mrs. Thatcher swooned away. Aunt Polly fell to crying and wringing her

7623	hands.

7624	

7625	The alarm swept from lip to lip, from group to group, from street to

7626	street, and within five minutes the bells were wildly clanging and

7627	the whole town was up! The Cardiff Hill episode sank into instant

7628	insignificance, the burglars were forgotten, horses were saddled, skiffs

7629	were manned, the ferryboat ordered out, and before the horror was half

7630	an hour old, two hundred men were pouring down highroad and river toward

7631	the cave.

7632	

7633	All the long afternoon the village seemed empty and dead. Many women

7634	visited Aunt Polly and Mrs. Thatcher and tried to comfort them. They

7635	cried with them, too, and that was still better than words. All the

7636	tedious night the town waited for news; but when the morning dawned at

7637	last, all the word that came was, “Send more candles—and send food.”

7638	 Mrs. Thatcher was almost crazed; and Aunt Polly, also. Judge Thatcher

7639	sent messages of hope and encouragement from the cave, but they conveyed

7640	no real cheer.

7641	

7642	The old Welshman came home toward daylight, spattered with

7643	candle-grease, smeared with clay, and almost worn out. He found Huck

7644	still in the bed that had been provided for him, and delirious with

7645	fever. The physicians were all at the cave, so the Widow Douglas came

7646	and took charge of the patient. She said she would do her best by him,

7647	because, whether he was good, bad, or indifferent, he was the Lord’s,

7648	and nothing that was the Lord’s was a thing to be neglected. The

7649	Welshman said Huck had good spots in him, and the widow said:

7650	

7651	“You can depend on it. That’s the Lord’s mark. He don’t leave it off.

7652	He never does. Puts it somewhere on every creature that comes from his

7653	hands.”

7654	

7655	Early in the forenoon parties of jaded men began to straggle into the

7656	village, but the strongest of the citizens continued searching. All the

7657	news that could be gained was that remotenesses of the cavern were being

7658	ransacked that had never been visited before; that every corner and

7659	crevice was going to be thoroughly searched; that wherever one wandered

7660	through the maze of passages, lights were to be seen flitting hither

7661	and thither in the distance, and shoutings and pistol-shots sent their

7662	hollow reverberations to the ear down the sombre aisles. In one place,

7663	far from the section usually traversed by tourists, the names “BECKY &

7664	TOM” had been found traced upon the rocky wall with candle-smoke, and

7665	near at hand a grease-soiled bit of ribbon. Mrs. Thatcher recognized the

7666	ribbon and cried over it. She said it was the last relic she should ever

7667	have of her child; and that no other memorial of her could ever be so

7668	precious, because this one parted latest from the living body before the

7669	awful death came. Some said that now and then, in the cave, a far-away

7670	speck of light would glimmer, and then a glorious shout would burst

7671	forth and a score of men go trooping down the echoing aisle—and then a

7672	sickening disappointment always followed; the children were not there;

7673	it was only a searcher’s light.

7674	

7675	Three dreadful days and nights dragged their tedious hours along, and

7676	the village sank into a hopeless stupor. No one had heart for anything.

7677	The accidental discovery, just made, that the proprietor of the

7678	Temperance Tavern kept liquor on his premises, scarcely fluttered the

7679	public pulse, tremendous as the fact was. In a lucid interval, Huck

7680	feebly led up to the subject of taverns, and finally asked—dimly

7681	dreading the worst—if anything had been discovered at the Temperance

7682	Tavern since he had been ill.

7683	

7684	“Yes,” said the widow.

7685	

7686	Huck started up in bed, wildeyed:

7687	

7688	“What? What was it?”

7689	

7690	“Liquor!—and the place has been shut up. Lie down, child—what a turn you

7691	did give me!”

7692	

7693	“Only tell me just one thing—only just one—please! Was it Tom Sawyer

7694	that found it?”

7695	

7696	The widow burst into tears. “Hush, hush, child, hush! I’ve told you

7697	before, you must _not_ talk. You are very, very sick!”

7698	

7699	Then nothing but liquor had been found; there would have been a great

7700	powwow if it had been the gold. So the treasure was gone forever—gone

7701	forever! But what could she be crying about? Curious that she should

7702	cry.

7703	

7704	These thoughts worked their dim way through Huck’s mind, and under the

7705	weariness they gave him he fell asleep. The widow said to herself:

7706	

7707	“There—he’s asleep, poor wreck. Tom Sawyer find it! Pity but somebody

7708	could find Tom Sawyer! Ah, there ain’t many left, now, that’s got hope

7709	enough, or strength enough, either, to go on searching.”

7710	

7711	

7712	

7713	

7714	CHAPTER XXXI

7715	

7716	

7717	NOW to return to Tom and Becky’s share in the picnic. They tripped along

7718	the murky aisles with the rest of the company, visiting the familiar

7719	wonders of the cave—wonders dubbed with rather over-descriptive names,

7720	such as “The Drawing-Room,” “The Cathedral,” “Aladdin’s Palace,” and

7721	so on. Presently the hide-and-seek frolicking began, and Tom and Becky

7722	engaged in it with zeal until the exertion began to grow a trifle

7723	wearisome; then they wandered down a sinuous avenue holding their

7724	candles aloft and reading the tangled webwork of names, dates,

7725	postoffice addresses, and mottoes with which the rocky walls had been

7726	frescoed (in candle-smoke). Still drifting along and talking, they

7727	scarcely noticed that they were now in a part of the cave whose walls

7728	were not frescoed. They smoked their own names under an overhanging

7729	shelf and moved on. Presently they came to a place where a little stream

7730	of water, trickling over a ledge and carrying a limestone sediment with

7731	it, had, in the slow-dragging ages, formed a laced and ruffled Niagara

7732	in gleaming and imperishable stone. Tom squeezed his small body behind

7733	it in order to illuminate it for Becky’s gratification. He found that

7734	it curtained a sort of steep natural stairway which was enclosed between

7735	narrow walls, and at once the ambition to be a discoverer seized him.

7736	

7737	Becky responded to his call, and they made a smoke-mark for future

7738	guidance, and started upon their quest. They wound this way and that,

7739	far down into the secret depths of the cave, made another mark, and

7740	branched off in search of novelties to tell the upper world about. In

7741	one place they found a spacious cavern, from whose ceiling depended a

7742	multitude of shining stalactites of the length and circumference of

7743	a man’s leg; they walked all about it, wondering and admiring, and

7744	presently left it by one of the numerous passages that opened into

7745	it. This shortly brought them to a bewitching spring, whose basin was

7746	incrusted with a frostwork of glittering crystals; it was in the midst

7747	of a cavern whose walls were supported by many fantastic pillars which

7748	had been formed by the joining of great stalactites and stalagmites

7749	together, the result of the ceaseless water-drip of centuries. Under the

7750	roof vast knots of bats had packed themselves together, thousands in a

7751	bunch; the lights disturbed the creatures and they came flocking down by

7752	hundreds, squeaking and darting furiously at the candles. Tom knew their

7753	ways and the danger of this sort of conduct. He seized Becky’s hand and

7754	hurried her into the first corridor that offered; and none too soon, for

7755	a bat struck Becky’s light out with its wing while she was passing out

7756	of the cavern. The bats chased the children a good distance; but the

7757	fugitives plunged into every new passage that offered, and at last got

7758	rid of the perilous things. Tom found a subterranean lake, shortly,

7759	which stretched its dim length away until its shape was lost in the

7760	shadows. He wanted to explore its borders, but concluded that it would

7761	be best to sit down and rest awhile, first. Now, for the first time, the

7762	deep stillness of the place laid a clammy hand upon the spirits of the

7763	children. Becky said:

7764	

7765	“Why, I didn’t notice, but it seems ever so long since I heard any of

7766	the others.”

7767	

7768	“Come to think, Becky, we are away down below them—and I don’t know how

7769	far away north, or south, or east, or whichever it is. We couldn’t hear

7770	them here.”

7771	

7772	Becky grew apprehensive.

7773	

7774	“I wonder how long we’ve been down here, Tom? We better start back.”

7775	

7776	“Yes, I reckon we better. P’raps we better.”

7777	

7778	“Can you find the way, Tom? It’s all a mixed-up crookedness to me.”

7779	

7780	“I reckon I could find it—but then the bats. If they put our candles

7781	out it will be an awful fix. Let’s try some other way, so as not to go

7782	through there.”

7783	

7784	“Well. But I hope we won’t get lost. It would be so awful!” and the girl

7785	shuddered at the thought of the dreadful possibilities.

7786	

7787	They started through a corridor, and traversed it in silence a long

7788	way, glancing at each new opening, to see if there was anything familiar

7789	about the look of it; but they were all strange. Every time Tom made an

7790	examination, Becky would watch his face for an encouraging sign, and he

7791	would say cheerily:

7792	

7793	“Oh, it’s all right. This ain’t the one, but we’ll come to it right

7794	away!”

7795	

7796	But he felt less and less hopeful with each failure, and presently began

7797	to turn off into diverging avenues at sheer random, in desperate hope of

7798	finding the one that was wanted. He still said it was “all right,” but

7799	there was such a leaden dread at his heart that the words had lost their

7800	ring and sounded just as if he had said, “All is lost!” Becky clung to

7801	his side in an anguish of fear, and tried hard to keep back the tears,

7802	but they would come. At last she said:

7803	

7804	“Oh, Tom, never mind the bats, let’s go back that way! We seem to get

7805	worse and worse off all the time.”

7806	

7807	“Listen!” said he.

7808	

7809	Profound silence; silence so deep that even their breathings were

7810	conspicuous in the hush. Tom shouted. The call went echoing down

7811	the empty aisles and died out in the distance in a faint sound that

7812	resembled a ripple of mocking laughter.

7813	

7814	“Oh, don’t do it again, Tom, it is too horrid,” said Becky.

7815	

7816	“It is horrid, but I better, Becky; they might hear us, you know,” and

7817	he shouted again.

7818	

7819	The “might” was even a chillier horror than the ghostly laughter, it so

7820	confessed a perishing hope. The children stood still and listened; but

7821	there was no result. Tom turned upon the back track at once, and hurried

7822	his steps. It was but a little while before a certain indecision in his

7823	manner revealed another fearful fact to Becky—he could not find his way

7824	back!

7825	

7826	“Oh, Tom, you didn’t make any marks!”

7827	

7828	“Becky, I was such a fool! Such a fool! I never thought we might want to

7829	come back! No—I can’t find the way. It’s all mixed up.”

7830	

7831	“Tom, Tom, we’re lost! we’re lost! We never can get out of this awful

7832	place! Oh, why _did_ we ever leave the others!”

7833	

7834	She sank to the ground and burst into such a frenzy of crying that Tom

7835	was appalled with the idea that she might die, or lose her reason. He

7836	sat down by her and put his arms around her; she buried her face in

7837	his bosom, she clung to him, she poured out her terrors, her unavailing

7838	regrets, and the far echoes turned them all to jeering laughter. Tom

7839	begged her to pluck up hope again, and she said she could not. He fell

7840	to blaming and abusing himself for getting her into this miserable

7841	situation; this had a better effect. She said she would try to hope

7842	again, she would get up and follow wherever he might lead if only he

7843	would not talk like that any more. For he was no more to blame than she,

7844	she said.

7845	

7846	So they moved on again—aimlessly—simply at random—all they could do

7847	was to move, keep moving. For a little while, hope made a show of

7848	reviving—not with any reason to back it, but only because it is its

7849	nature to revive when the spring has not been taken out of it by age and

7850	familiarity with failure.

7851	

7852	By-and-by Tom took Becky’s candle and blew it out. This economy meant so

7853	much! Words were not needed. Becky understood, and her hope died again.

7854	She knew that Tom had a whole candle and three or four pieces in his

7855	pockets—yet he must economize.

7856	

7857	By-and-by, fatigue began to assert its claims; the children tried to pay

7858	attention, for it was dreadful to think of sitting down when time was

7859	grown to be so precious, moving, in some direction, in any direction,

7860	was at least progress and might bear fruit; but to sit down was to

7861	invite death and shorten its pursuit.

7862	

7863	At last Becky’s frail limbs refused to carry her farther. She sat down.

7864	Tom rested with her, and they talked of home, and the friends there,

7865	and the comfortable beds and, above all, the light! Becky cried, and Tom

7866	tried to think of some way of comforting her, but all his encouragements

7867	were grown thread-bare with use, and sounded like sarcasms. Fatigue bore

7868	so heavily upon Becky that she drowsed off to sleep. Tom was grateful.

7869	He sat looking into her drawn face and saw it grow smooth and natural

7870	under the influence of pleasant dreams; and by-and-by a smile dawned and

7871	rested there. The peaceful face reflected somewhat of peace and healing

7872	into his own spirit, and his thoughts wandered away to bygone times and

7873	dreamy memories. While he was deep in his musings, Becky woke up with a

7874	breezy little laugh—but it was stricken dead upon her lips, and a groan

7875	followed it.

7876	

7877	“Oh, how _could_ I sleep! I wish I never, never had waked! No! No, I

7878	don’t, Tom! Don’t look so! I won’t say it again.”

7879	

7880	“I’m glad you’ve slept, Becky; you’ll feel rested, now, and we’ll find

7881	the way out.”

7882	

7883	“We can try, Tom; but I’ve seen such a beautiful country in my dream. I

7884	reckon we are going there.”

7885	

7886	“Maybe not, maybe not. Cheer up, Becky, and let’s go on trying.”

7887	

7888	They rose up and wandered along, hand in hand and hopeless. They tried

7889	to estimate how long they had been in the cave, but all they knew was

7890	that it seemed days and weeks, and yet it was plain that this could not

7891	be, for their candles were not gone yet. A long time after this—they

7892	could not tell how long—Tom said they must go softly and listen for

7893	dripping water—they must find a spring. They found one presently, and

7894	Tom said it was time to rest again. Both were cruelly tired, yet Becky

7895	said she thought she could go a little farther. She was surprised to

7896	hear Tom dissent. She could not understand it. They sat down, and Tom

7897	fastened his candle to the wall in front of them with some clay. Thought

7898	was soon busy; nothing was said for some time. Then Becky broke the

7899	silence:

7900	

7901	“Tom, I am so hungry!”

7902	

7903	Tom took something out of his pocket.

7904	

7905	“Do you remember this?” said he.

7906	

7907	Becky almost smiled.

7908	

7909	“It’s our wedding-cake, Tom.”

7910	

7911	“Yes—I wish it was as big as a barrel, for it’s all we’ve got.”

7912	

7913	“I saved it from the picnic for us to dream on, Tom, the way grownup

7914	people do with wedding-cake—but it’ll be our—”

7915	

7916	She dropped the sentence where it was. Tom divided the cake and Becky

7917	ate with good appetite, while Tom nibbled at his moiety. There was

7918	abundance of cold water to finish the feast with. By-and-by Becky

7919	suggested that they move on again. Tom was silent a moment. Then he

7920	said:

7921	

7922	“Becky, can you bear it if I tell you something?”

7923	

7924	Becky’s face paled, but she thought she could.

7925	

7926	“Well, then, Becky, we must stay here, where there’s water to drink.

7927	That little piece is our last candle!”

7928	

7929	Becky gave loose to tears and wailings. Tom did what he could to comfort

7930	her, but with little effect. At length Becky said:

7931	

7932	“Tom!”

7933	

7934	“Well, Becky?”

7935	

7936	“They’ll miss us and hunt for us!”

7937	

7938	“Yes, they will! Certainly they will!”

7939	

7940	“Maybe they’re hunting for us now, Tom.”

7941	

7942	“Why, I reckon maybe they are. I hope they are.”

7943	

7944	“When would they miss us, Tom?”

7945	

7946	“When they get back to the boat, I reckon.”

7947	

7948	“Tom, it might be dark then—would they notice we hadn’t come?”

7949	

7950	“I don’t know. But anyway, your mother would miss you as soon as they

7951	got home.”

7952	

7953	A frightened look in Becky’s face brought Tom to his senses and he saw

7954	that he had made a blunder. Becky was not to have gone home that night!

7955	The children became silent and thoughtful. In a moment a new burst of

7956	grief from Becky showed Tom that the thing in his mind had struck hers

7957	also—that the Sabbath morning might be half spent before Mrs. Thatcher

7958	discovered that Becky was not at Mrs. Harper’s.

7959	

7960	The children fastened their eyes upon their bit of candle and watched it

7961	melt slowly and pitilessly away; saw the half inch of wick stand alone

7962	at last; saw the feeble flame rise and fall, climb the thin column of

7963	smoke, linger at its top a moment, and then—the horror of utter darkness

7964	reigned!

7965	

7966	How long afterward it was that Becky came to a slow consciousness that

7967	she was crying in Tom’s arms, neither could tell. All that they knew

7968	was, that after what seemed a mighty stretch of time, both awoke out of

7969	a dead stupor of sleep and resumed their miseries once more. Tom said

7970	it might be Sunday, now—maybe Monday. He tried to get Becky to talk, but

7971	her sorrows were too oppressive, all her hopes were gone. Tom said that

7972	they must have been missed long ago, and no doubt the search was going

7973	on. He would shout and maybe some one would come. He tried it; but in

7974	the darkness the distant echoes sounded so hideously that he tried it no

7975	more.

7976	

7977	The hours wasted away, and hunger came to torment the captives again. A

7978	portion of Tom’s half of the cake was left; they divided and ate it. But

7979	they seemed hungrier than before. The poor morsel of food only whetted

7980	desire.

7981	

7982	By-and-by Tom said:

7983	

7984	“SH! Did you hear that?”

7985	

7986	Both held their breath and listened. There was a sound like the

7987	faintest, far-off shout. Instantly Tom answered it, and leading Becky by

7988	the hand, started groping down the corridor in its direction. Presently

7989	he listened again; again the sound was heard, and apparently a little

7990	nearer.

7991	

7992	“It’s them!” said Tom; “they’re coming! Come along, Becky—we’re all

7993	right now!”

7994	

7995	The joy of the prisoners was almost overwhelming. Their speed was slow,

7996	however, because pitfalls were somewhat common, and had to be guarded

7997	against. They shortly came to one and had to stop. It might be three

7998	feet deep, it might be a hundred—there was no passing it at any rate.

7999	Tom got down on his breast and reached as far down as he could. No

8000	bottom. They must stay there and wait until the searchers came. They

8001	listened; evidently the distant shoutings were growing more distant!

8002	a moment or two more and they had gone altogether. The heart-sinking

8003	misery of it! Tom whooped until he was hoarse, but it was of no use. He

8004	talked hopefully to Becky; but an age of anxious waiting passed and no

8005	sounds came again.

8006	

8007	The children groped their way back to the spring. The weary time dragged

8008	on; they slept again, and awoke famished and woe-stricken. Tom believed

8009	it must be Tuesday by this time.

8010	

8011	Now an idea struck him. There were some side passages near at hand. It

8012	would be better to explore some of these than bear the weight of the

8013	heavy time in idleness. He took a kite-line from his pocket, tied it to

8014	a projection, and he and Becky started, Tom in the lead, unwinding the

8015	line as he groped along. At the end of twenty steps the corridor ended

8016	in a “jumping-off place.” Tom got down on his knees and felt below,

8017	and then as far around the corner as he could reach with his hands

8018	conveniently; he made an effort to stretch yet a little farther to the

8019	right, and at that moment, not twenty yards away, a human hand, holding

8020	a candle, appeared from behind a rock! Tom lifted up a glorious shout,

8021	and instantly that hand was followed by the body it belonged to—Injun

8022	Joe’s! Tom was paralyzed; he could not move. He was vastly gratified the

8023	next moment, to see the “Spaniard” take to his heels and get himself out

8024	of sight. Tom wondered that Joe had not recognized his voice and come

8025	over and killed him for testifying in court. But the echoes must have

8026	disguised the voice. Without doubt, that was it, he reasoned. Tom’s

8027	fright weakened every muscle in his body. He said to himself that if he

8028	had strength enough to get back to the spring he would stay there, and

8029	nothing should tempt him to run the risk of meeting Injun Joe again. He

8030	was careful to keep from Becky what it was he had seen. He told her he

8031	had only shouted “for luck.”

8032	

8033	But hunger and wretchedness rise superior to fears in the long run.

8034	Another tedious wait at the spring and another long sleep brought

8035	changes. The children awoke tortured with a raging hunger. Tom believed

8036	that it must be Wednesday or Thursday or even Friday or Saturday, now,

8037	and that the search had been given over. He proposed to explore another

8038	passage. He felt willing to risk Injun Joe and all other terrors. But

8039	Becky was very weak. She had sunk into a dreary apathy and would not be

8040	roused. She said she would wait, now, where she was, and die—it would

8041	not be long. She told Tom to go with the kite-line and explore if he

8042	chose; but she implored him to come back every little while and speak

8043	to her; and she made him promise that when the awful time came, he would

8044	stay by her and hold her hand until all was over.

8045	

8046	Tom kissed her, with a choking sensation in his throat, and made a show

8047	of being confident of finding the searchers or an escape from the cave;

8048	then he took the kite-line in his hand and went groping down one of the

8049	passages on his hands and knees, distressed with hunger and sick with

8050	bodings of coming doom.

8051	

8052	

8053	

8054	

8055	CHAPTER XXXII

8056	

8057	

8058	TUESDAY afternoon came, and waned to the twilight. The village of St.

8059	Petersburg still mourned. The lost children had not been found. Public

8060	prayers had been offered up for them, and many and many a private prayer

8061	that had the petitioner’s whole heart in it; but still no good news came

8062	from the cave. The majority of the searchers had given up the quest

8063	and gone back to their daily avocations, saying that it was plain the

8064	children could never be found. Mrs. Thatcher was very ill, and a great

8065	part of the time delirious. People said it was heartbreaking to hear her

8066	call her child, and raise her head and listen a whole minute at a time,

8067	then lay it wearily down again with a moan. Aunt Polly had drooped into

8068	a settled melancholy, and her gray hair had grown almost white. The

8069	village went to its rest on Tuesday night, sad and forlorn.

8070	

8071	Away in the middle of the night a wild peal burst from the village

8072	bells, and in a moment the streets were swarming with frantic half-clad

8073	people, who shouted, “Turn out! turn out! they’re found! they’re found!”

8074	 Tin pans and horns were added to the din, the population massed itself

8075	and moved toward the river, met the children coming in an open carriage

8076	drawn by shouting citizens, thronged around it, joined its homeward

8077	march, and swept magnificently up the main street roaring huzzah after

8078	huzzah!

8079	

8080	The village was illuminated; nobody went to bed again; it was the

8081	greatest night the little town had ever seen. During the first half-hour

8082	a procession of villagers filed through Judge Thatcher’s house, seized

8083	the saved ones and kissed them, squeezed Mrs. Thatcher’s hand, tried to

8084	speak but couldn’t—and drifted out raining tears all over the place.

8085	

8086	Aunt Polly’s happiness was complete, and Mrs. Thatcher’s nearly so. It

8087	would be complete, however, as soon as the messenger dispatched with the

8088	great news to the cave should get the word to her husband. Tom lay upon

8089	a sofa with an eager auditory about him and told the history of the

8090	wonderful adventure, putting in many striking additions to adorn it

8091	withal; and closed with a description of how he left Becky and went

8092	on an exploring expedition; how he followed two avenues as far as his

8093	kite-line would reach; how he followed a third to the fullest stretch

8094	of the kite-line, and was about to turn back when he glimpsed a far-off

8095	speck that looked like daylight; dropped the line and groped toward it,

8096	pushed his head and shoulders through a small hole, and saw the broad

8097	Mississippi rolling by!

8098	

8099	And if it had only happened to be night he would not have seen that

8100	speck of daylight and would not have explored that passage any more! He

8101	told how he went back for Becky and broke the good news and she told

8102	him not to fret her with such stuff, for she was tired, and knew she was

8103	going to die, and wanted to. He described how he labored with her and

8104	convinced her; and how she almost died for joy when she had groped to

8105	where she actually saw the blue speck of daylight; how he pushed his way

8106	out at the hole and then helped her out; how they sat there and cried

8107	for gladness; how some men came along in a skiff and Tom hailed them

8108	and told them their situation and their famished condition; how the men

8109	didn’t believe the wild tale at first, “because,” said they, “you are

8110	five miles down the river below the valley the cave is in”—then took

8111	them aboard, rowed to a house, gave them supper, made them rest till two

8112	or three hours after dark and then brought them home.

8113	

8114	Before day-dawn, Judge Thatcher and the handful of searchers with him

8115	were tracked out, in the cave, by the twine clews they had strung behind

8116	them, and informed of the great news.

8117	

8118	Three days and nights of toil and hunger in the cave were not to

8119	be shaken off at once, as Tom and Becky soon discovered. They were

8120	bedridden all of Wednesday and Thursday, and seemed to grow more and

8121	more tired and worn, all the time. Tom got about, a little, on Thursday,

8122	was downtown Friday, and nearly as whole as ever Saturday; but Becky

8123	did not leave her room until Sunday, and then she looked as if she had

8124	passed through a wasting illness.

8125	

8126	Tom learned of Huck’s sickness and went to see him on Friday, but could

8127	not be admitted to the bedroom; neither could he on Saturday or Sunday.

8128	He was admitted daily after that, but was warned to keep still about his

8129	adventure and introduce no exciting topic. The Widow Douglas stayed by

8130	to see that he obeyed. At home Tom learned of the Cardiff Hill event;

8131	also that the “ragged man’s” body had eventually been found in the river

8132	near the ferry-landing; he had been drowned while trying to escape,

8133	perhaps.

8134	

8135	About a fortnight after Tom’s rescue from the cave, he started off to

8136	visit Huck, who had grown plenty strong enough, now, to hear exciting

8137	talk, and Tom had some that would interest him, he thought. Judge

8138	Thatcher’s house was on Tom’s way, and he stopped to see Becky. The

8139	Judge and some friends set Tom to talking, and some one asked him

8140	ironically if he wouldn’t like to go to the cave again. Tom said he

8141	thought he wouldn’t mind it. The Judge said:

8142	

8143	“Well, there are others just like you, Tom, I’ve not the least doubt.

8144	But we have taken care of that. Nobody will get lost in that cave any

8145	more.”

8146	

8147	“Why?”

8148	

8149	“Because I had its big door sheathed with boiler iron two weeks ago, and

8150	triple-locked—and I’ve got the keys.”

8151	

8152	Tom turned as white as a sheet.

8153	

8154	“What’s the matter, boy! Here, run, somebody! Fetch a glass of water!”

8155	

8156	The water was brought and thrown into Tom’s face.

8157	

8158	“Ah, now you’re all right. What was the matter with you, Tom?”

8159	

8160	“Oh, Judge, Injun Joe’s in the cave!”

8161	

8162	

8163	

8164	

8165	CHAPTER XXXIII

8166	

8167	

8168	WITHIN a few minutes the news had spread, and a dozen skiff-loads of

8169	men were on their way to McDougal’s cave, and the ferryboat, well filled

8170	with passengers, soon followed. Tom Sawyer was in the skiff that bore

8171	Judge Thatcher.

8172	

8173	When the cave door was unlocked, a sorrowful sight presented itself in
