8174	the dim twilight of the place. Injun Joe lay stretched upon the ground,

8175	dead, with his face close to the crack of the door, as if his longing

8176	eyes had been fixed, to the latest moment, upon the light and the cheer

8177	of the free world outside. Tom was touched, for he knew by his own

8178	experience how this wretch had suffered. His pity was moved, but

8179	nevertheless he felt an abounding sense of relief and security, now,

8180	which revealed to him in a degree which he had not fully appreciated

8181	before how vast a weight of dread had been lying upon him since the day

8182	he lifted his voice against this bloody-minded outcast.

8183	

8184	Injun Joe’s bowie-knife lay close by, its blade broken in two. The great

8185	foundation-beam of the door had been chipped and hacked through, with

8186	tedious labor; useless labor, too, it was, for the native rock formed a

8187	sill outside it, and upon that stubborn material the knife had wrought

8188	no effect; the only damage done was to the knife itself. But if there

8189	had been no stony obstruction there the labor would have been useless

8190	still, for if the beam had been wholly cut away Injun Joe could not have

8191	squeezed his body under the door, and he knew it. So he had only hacked

8192	that place in order to be doing something—in order to pass the weary

8193	time—in order to employ his tortured faculties. Ordinarily one could

8194	find half a dozen bits of candle stuck around in the crevices of this

8195	vestibule, left there by tourists; but there were none now. The prisoner

8196	had searched them out and eaten them. He had also contrived to catch a

8197	few bats, and these, also, he had eaten, leaving only their claws. The

8198	poor unfortunate had starved to death. In one place, near at hand, a

8199	stalagmite had been slowly growing up from the ground for ages, builded

8200	by the water-drip from a stalactite overhead. The captive had broken off

8201	the stalagmite, and upon the stump had placed a stone, wherein he had

8202	scooped a shallow hollow to catch the precious drop that fell once

8203	in every three minutes with the dreary regularity of a clock-tick—a

8204	dessertspoonful once in four and twenty hours. That drop was falling

8205	when the Pyramids were new; when Troy fell; when the foundations of Rome

8206	were laid; when Christ was crucified; when the Conqueror created the

8207	British empire; when Columbus sailed; when the massacre at Lexington was

8208	“news.”

8209	

8210	It is falling now; it will still be falling when all these things shall

8211	have sunk down the afternoon of history, and the twilight of tradition,

8212	and been swallowed up in the thick night of oblivion. Has everything a

8213	purpose and a mission? Did this drop fall patiently during five thousand

8214	years to be ready for this flitting human insect’s need? and has it

8215	another important object to accomplish ten thousand years to come? No

8216	matter. It is many and many a year since the hapless half-breed scooped

8217	out the stone to catch the priceless drops, but to this day the tourist

8218	stares longest at that pathetic stone and that slow-dropping water when

8219	he comes to see the wonders of McDougal’s cave. Injun Joe’s cup stands

8220	first in the list of the cavern’s marvels; even “Aladdin’s Palace”

8221	 cannot rival it.

8222	

8223	Injun Joe was buried near the mouth of the cave; and people flocked

8224	there in boats and wagons from the towns and from all the farms and

8225	hamlets for seven miles around; they brought their children, and

8226	all sorts of provisions, and confessed that they had had almost as

8227	satisfactory a time at the funeral as they could have had at the

8228	hanging.

8229	

8230	This funeral stopped the further growth of one thing—the petition to the

8231	governor for Injun Joe’s pardon. The petition had been largely signed;

8232	many tearful and eloquent meetings had been held, and a committee of

8233	sappy women been appointed to go in deep mourning and wail around the

8234	governor, and implore him to be a merciful ass and trample his duty

8235	under foot. Injun Joe was believed to have killed five citizens of the

8236	village, but what of that? If he had been Satan himself there would

8237	have been plenty of weaklings ready to scribble their names to a

8238	pardon-petition, and drip a tear on it from their permanently impaired

8239	and leaky water-works.

8240	

8241	The morning after the funeral Tom took Huck to a private place to have

8242	an important talk. Huck had learned all about Tom’s adventure from the

8243	Welshman and the Widow Douglas, by this time, but Tom said he reckoned

8244	there was one thing they had not told him; that thing was what he wanted

8245	to talk about now. Huck’s face saddened. He said:

8246	

8247	“I know what it is. You got into No. 2 and never found anything but

8248	whiskey. Nobody told me it was you; but I just knowed it must ’a’ ben

8249	you, soon as I heard ’bout that whiskey business; and I knowed you

8250	hadn’t got the money becuz you’d ’a’ got at me some way or other and

8251	told me even if you was mum to everybody else. Tom, something’s always

8252	told me we’d never get holt of that swag.”

8253	

8254	“Why, Huck, I never told on that tavern-keeper. _You_ know his tavern

8255	was all right the Saturday I went to the picnic. Don’t you remember you

8256	was to watch there that night?”

8257	

8258	“Oh yes! Why, it seems ’bout a year ago. It was that very night that I

8259	follered Injun Joe to the widder’s.”

8260	

8261	“_You_ followed him?”

8262	

8263	“Yes—but you keep mum. I reckon Injun Joe’s left friends behind him, and

8264	I don’t want ’em souring on me and doing me mean tricks. If it hadn’t

8265	ben for me he’d be down in Texas now, all right.”

8266	

8267	Then Huck told his entire adventure in confidence to Tom, who had only

8268	heard of the Welshman’s part of it before.

8269	

8270	“Well,” said Huck, presently, coming back to the main question, “whoever

8271	nipped the whiskey in No. 2, nipped the money, too, I reckon—anyways

8272	it’s a goner for us, Tom.”

8273	

8274	“Huck, that money wasn’t ever in No. 2!”

8275	

8276	“What!” Huck searched his comrade’s face keenly. “Tom, have you got on

8277	the track of that money again?”

8278	

8279	“Huck, it’s in the cave!”

8280	

8281	Huck’s eyes blazed.

8282	

8283	“Say it again, Tom.”

8284	

8285	“The money’s in the cave!”

8286	

8287	“Tom—honest injun, now—is it fun, or earnest?”

8288	

8289	“Earnest, Huck—just as earnest as ever I was in my life. Will you go in

8290	there with me and help get it out?”

8291	

8292	“I bet I will! I will if it’s where we can blaze our way to it and not

8293	get lost.”

8294	

8295	“Huck, we can do that without the least little bit of trouble in the

8296	world.”

8297	

8298	“Good as wheat! What makes you think the money’s—”

8299	

8300	“Huck, you just wait till we get in there. If we don’t find it I’ll

8301	agree to give you my drum and every thing I’ve got in the world. I will,

8302	by jings.”

8303	

8304	“All right—it’s a whiz. When do you say?”

8305	

8306	“Right now, if you say it. Are you strong enough?”

8307	

8308	“Is it far in the cave? I ben on my pins a little, three or four days,

8309	now, but I can’t walk more’n a mile, Tom—least I don’t think I could.”

8310	

8311	“It’s about five mile into there the way anybody but me would go, Huck,

8312	but there’s a mighty short cut that they don’t anybody but me know

8313	about. Huck, I’ll take you right to it in a skiff. I’ll float the skiff

8314	down there, and I’ll pull it back again all by myself. You needn’t ever

8315	turn your hand over.”

8316	

8317	“Less start right off, Tom.”

8318	

8319	“All right. We want some bread and meat, and our pipes, and a little

8320	bag or two, and two or three kite-strings, and some of these new-fangled

8321	things they call lucifer matches. I tell you, many’s the time I wished I

8322	had some when I was in there before.”

8323	

8324	A trifle after noon the boys borrowed a small skiff from a citizen who

8325	was absent, and got under way at once. When they were several miles

8326	below “Cave Hollow,” Tom said:

8327	

8328	“Now you see this bluff here looks all alike all the way down from the

8329	cave hollow—no houses, no wood-yards, bushes all alike. But do you see

8330	that white place up yonder where there’s been a landslide? Well, that’s

8331	one of my marks. We’ll get ashore, now.”

8332	

8333	They landed.

8334	

8335	“Now, Huck, where we’re a-standing you could touch that hole I got out

8336	of with a fishing-pole. See if you can find it.”

8337	

8338	Huck searched all the place about, and found nothing. Tom proudly

8339	marched into a thick clump of sumach bushes and said:

8340	

8341	“Here you are! Look at it, Huck; it’s the snuggest hole in this country.

8342	You just keep mum about it. All along I’ve been wanting to be a robber,

8343	but I knew I’d got to have a thing like this, and where to run across

8344	it was the bother. We’ve got it now, and we’ll keep it quiet, only we’ll

8345	let Joe Harper and Ben Rogers in—because of course there’s got to be a

8346	Gang, or else there wouldn’t be any style about it. Tom Sawyer’s Gang—it

8347	sounds splendid, don’t it, Huck?”

8348	

8349	“Well, it just does, Tom. And who’ll we rob?”

8350	

8351	“Oh, most anybody. Waylay people—that’s mostly the way.”

8352	

8353	“And kill them?”

8354	

8355	“No, not always. Hive them in the cave till they raise a ransom.”

8356	

8357	“What’s a ransom?”

8358	

8359	“Money. You make them raise all they can, off’n their friends; and after

8360	you’ve kept them a year, if it ain’t raised then you kill them. That’s

8361	the general way. Only you don’t kill the women. You shut up the women,

8362	but you don’t kill them. They’re always beautiful and rich, and awfully

8363	scared. You take their watches and things, but you always take your hat

8364	off and talk polite. They ain’t anybody as polite as robbers—you’ll see

8365	that in any book. Well, the women get to loving you, and after they’ve

8366	been in the cave a week or two weeks they stop crying and after that

8367	you couldn’t get them to leave. If you drove them out they’d turn right

8368	around and come back. It’s so in all the books.”

8369	

8370	“Why, it’s real bully, Tom. I believe it’s better’n to be a pirate.”

8371	

8372	“Yes, it’s better in some ways, because it’s close to home and circuses

8373	and all that.”

8374	

8375	By this time everything was ready and the boys entered the hole, Tom in

8376	the lead. They toiled their way to the farther end of the tunnel, then

8377	made their spliced kite-strings fast and moved on. A few steps brought

8378	them to the spring, and Tom felt a shudder quiver all through him.

8379	He showed Huck the fragment of candle-wick perched on a lump of clay

8380	against the wall, and described how he and Becky had watched the flame

8381	struggle and expire.

8382	

8383	The boys began to quiet down to whispers, now, for the stillness and

8384	gloom of the place oppressed their spirits. They went on, and presently

8385	entered and followed Tom’s other corridor until they reached the

8386	“jumping-off place.” The candles revealed the fact that it was not

8387	really a precipice, but only a steep clay hill twenty or thirty feet

8388	high. Tom whispered:

8389	

8390	“Now I’ll show you something, Huck.”

8391	

8392	He held his candle aloft and said:

8393	

8394	“Look as far around the corner as you can. Do you see that? There—on the

8395	big rock over yonder—done with candle-smoke.”

8396	

8397	“Tom, it’s a _cross_!”

8398	

8399	“_Now_ where’s your Number Two? ‘_under the cross_,’ hey? Right yonder’s

8400	where I saw Injun Joe poke up his candle, Huck!”

8401	

8402	Huck stared at the mystic sign awhile, and then said with a shaky voice:

8403	

8404	“Tom, less git out of here!”

8405	

8406	“What! and leave the treasure?”

8407	

8408	“Yes—leave it. Injun Joe’s ghost is round about there, certain.”

8409	

8410	“No it ain’t, Huck, no it ain’t. It would ha’nt the place where he

8411	died—away out at the mouth of the cave—five mile from here.”

8412	

8413	“No, Tom, it wouldn’t. It would hang round the money. I know the ways of

8414	ghosts, and so do you.”

8415	

8416	Tom began to fear that Huck was right. Mis-givings gathered in his mind.

8417	But presently an idea occurred to him—

8418	

8419	“Lookyhere, Huck, what fools we’re making of ourselves! Injun Joe’s

8420	ghost ain’t a going to come around where there’s a cross!”

8421	

8422	The point was well taken. It had its effect.

8423	

8424	“Tom, I didn’t think of that. But that’s so. It’s luck for us, that

8425	cross is. I reckon we’ll climb down there and have a hunt for that box.”

8426	

8427	Tom went first, cutting rude steps in the clay hill as he descended.

8428	Huck followed. Four avenues opened out of the small cavern which the

8429	great rock stood in. The boys examined three of them with no result.

8430	They found a small recess in the one nearest the base of the rock, with

8431	a pallet of blankets spread down in it; also an old suspender, some

8432	bacon rind, and the well-gnawed bones of two or three fowls. But there

8433	was no moneybox. The lads searched and researched this place, but in

8434	vain. Tom said:

8435	

8436	“He said _under_ the cross. Well, this comes nearest to being under the

8437	cross. It can’t be under the rock itself, because that sets solid on the

8438	ground.”

8439	

8440	They searched everywhere once more, and then sat down discouraged. Huck

8441	could suggest nothing. By-and-by Tom said:

8442	

8443	“Lookyhere, Huck, there’s footprints and some candle-grease on the clay

8444	about one side of this rock, but not on the other sides. Now, what’s

8445	that for? I bet you the money _is_ under the rock. I’m going to dig in

8446	the clay.”

8447	

8448	“That ain’t no bad notion, Tom!” said Huck with animation.

8449	

8450	Tom’s “real Barlow” was out at once, and he had not dug four inches

8451	before he struck wood.

8452	

8453	“Hey, Huck!—you hear that?”

8454	

8455	Huck began to dig and scratch now. Some boards were soon uncovered and

8456	removed. They had concealed a natural chasm which led under the rock.

8457	Tom got into this and held his candle as far under the rock as he

8458	could, but said he could not see to the end of the rift. He proposed

8459	to explore. He stooped and passed under; the narrow way descended

8460	gradually. He followed its winding course, first to the right, then to

8461	the left, Huck at his heels. Tom turned a short curve, by-and-by, and

8462	exclaimed:

8463	

8464	“My goodness, Huck, lookyhere!”

8465	

8466	It was the treasure-box, sure enough, occupying a snug little cavern,

8467	along with an empty powder-keg, a couple of guns in leather cases, two

8468	or three pairs of old moccasins, a leather belt, and some other rubbish

8469	well soaked with the water-drip.

8470	

8471	“Got it at last!” said Huck, ploughing among the tarnished coins with

8472	his hand. “My, but we’re rich, Tom!”

8473	

8474	“Huck, I always reckoned we’d get it. It’s just too good to believe, but

8475	we _have_ got it, sure! Say—let’s not fool around here. Let’s snake it

8476	out. Lemme see if I can lift the box.”

8477	

8478	It weighed about fifty pounds. Tom could lift it, after an awkward

8479	fashion, but could not carry it conveniently.

8480	

8481	“I thought so,” he said; “_They_ carried it like it was heavy, that day

8482	at the ha’nted house. I noticed that. I reckon I was right to think of

8483	fetching the little bags along.”

8484	

8485	The money was soon in the bags and the boys took it up to the cross

8486	rock.

8487	

8488	“Now less fetch the guns and things,” said Huck.

8489	

8490	“No, Huck—leave them there. They’re just the tricks to have when we

8491	go to robbing. We’ll keep them there all the time, and we’ll hold our

8492	orgies there, too. It’s an awful snug place for orgies.”

8493	

8494	“What orgies?”

8495	

8496	“I dono. But robbers always have orgies, and of course we’ve got to

8497	have them, too. Come along, Huck, we’ve been in here a long time. It’s

8498	getting late, I reckon. I’m hungry, too. We’ll eat and smoke when we get

8499	to the skiff.”

8500	

8501	They presently emerged into the clump of sumach bushes, looked warily

8502	out, found the coast clear, and were soon lunching and smoking in the

8503	skiff. As the sun dipped toward the horizon they pushed out and got

8504	under way. Tom skimmed up the shore through the long twilight, chatting

8505	cheerily with Huck, and landed shortly after dark.

8506	

8507	“Now, Huck,” said Tom, “we’ll hide the money in the loft of the widow’s

8508	woodshed, and I’ll come up in the morning and we’ll count it and divide,

8509	and then we’ll hunt up a place out in the woods for it where it will be

8510	safe. Just you lay quiet here and watch the stuff till I run and hook

8511	Benny Taylor’s little wagon; I won’t be gone a minute.”

8512	

8513	He disappeared, and presently returned with the wagon, put the two small

8514	sacks into it, threw some old rags on top of them, and started off,

8515	dragging his cargo behind him. When the boys reached the Welshman’s

8516	house, they stopped to rest. Just as they were about to move on, the

8517	Welshman stepped out and said:

8518	

8519	“Hallo, who’s that?”

8520	

8521	“Huck and Tom Sawyer.”

8522	

8523	“Good! Come along with me, boys, you are keeping everybody waiting.

8524	Here—hurry up, trot ahead—I’ll haul the wagon for you. Why, it’s not as

8525	light as it might be. Got bricks in it?—or old metal?”

8526	

8527	“Old metal,” said Tom.

8528	

8529	“I judged so; the boys in this town will take more trouble and fool away

8530	more time hunting up six bits’ worth of old iron to sell to the foundry

8531	than they would to make twice the money at regular work. But that’s

8532	human nature—hurry along, hurry along!”

8533	

8534	The boys wanted to know what the hurry was about.

8535	

8536	“Never mind; you’ll see, when we get to the Widow Douglas’.”

8537	

8538	Huck said with some apprehension—for he was long used to being falsely

8539	accused:

8540	

8541	“Mr. Jones, we haven’t been doing nothing.”

8542	

8543	The Welshman laughed.

8544	

8545	“Well, I don’t know, Huck, my boy. I don’t know about that. Ain’t you

8546	and the widow good friends?”

8547	

8548	“Yes. Well, she’s ben good friends to me, anyway.”

8549	

8550	“All right, then. What do you want to be afraid for?”

8551	

8552	This question was not entirely answered in Huck’s slow mind before he

8553	found himself pushed, along with Tom, into Mrs. Douglas’ drawing-room.

8554	Mr. Jones left the wagon near the door and followed.

8555	

8556	The place was grandly lighted, and everybody that was of any consequence

8557	in the village was there. The Thatchers were there, the Harpers, the

8558	Rogerses, Aunt Polly, Sid, Mary, the minister, the editor, and a great

8559	many more, and all dressed in their best. The widow received the boys

8560	as heartily as any one could well receive two such looking beings. They

8561	were covered with clay and candle-grease. Aunt Polly blushed crimson

8562	with humiliation, and frowned and shook her head at Tom. Nobody suffered

8563	half as much as the two boys did, however. Mr. Jones said:

8564	

8565	“Tom wasn’t at home, yet, so I gave him up; but I stumbled on him and

8566	Huck right at my door, and so I just brought them along in a hurry.”

8567	

8568	“And you did just right,” said the widow. “Come with me, boys.”

8569	

8570	She took them to a bedchamber and said:

8571	

8572	“Now wash and dress yourselves. Here are two new suits of

8573	clothes—shirts, socks, everything complete. They’re Huck’s—no, no

8574	thanks, Huck—Mr. Jones bought one and I the other. But they’ll fit both

8575	of you. Get into them. We’ll wait—come down when you are slicked up

8576	enough.”

8577	

8578	Then she left.

8579	

8580	

8581	

8582	

8583	CHAPTER XXXIV

8584	

8585	

8586	HUCK said: “Tom, we can slope, if we can find a rope. The window ain’t

8587	high from the ground.”

8588	

8589	“Shucks! what do you want to slope for?”

8590	

8591	“Well, I ain’t used to that kind of a crowd. I can’t stand it. I ain’t

8592	going down there, Tom.”

8593	

8594	“Oh, bother! It ain’t anything. I don’t mind it a bit. I’ll take care of

8595	you.”

8596	

8597	Sid appeared.

8598	

8599	“Tom,” said he, “auntie has been waiting for you all the afternoon. Mary

8600	got your Sunday clothes ready, and everybody’s been fretting about you.

8601	Say—ain’t this grease and clay, on your clothes?”

8602	

8603	“Now, Mr. Siddy, you jist ’tend to your own business. What’s all this

8604	blowout about, anyway?”

8605	

8606	“It’s one of the widow’s parties that she’s always having. This time

8607	it’s for the Welshman and his sons, on account of that scrape they

8608	helped her out of the other night. And say—I can tell you something, if

8609	you want to know.”

8610	

8611	“Well, what?”

8612	

8613	“Why, old Mr. Jones is going to try to spring something on the people

8614	here tonight, but I overheard him tell auntie today about it, as a

8615	secret, but I reckon it’s not much of a secret now. Everybody knows—the

8616	widow, too, for all she tries to let on she don’t. Mr. Jones was bound

8617	Huck should be here—couldn’t get along with his grand secret without

8618	Huck, you know!”

8619	

8620	“Secret about what, Sid?”

8621	

8622	“About Huck tracking the robbers to the widow’s. I reckon Mr. Jones was

8623	going to make a grand time over his surprise, but I bet you it will drop

8624	pretty flat.”

8625	

8626	Sid chuckled in a very contented and satisfied way.

8627	

8628	“Sid, was it you that told?”

8629	

8630	“Oh, never mind who it was. _Somebody_ told—that’s enough.”

8631	

8632	“Sid, there’s only one person in this town mean enough to do that, and

8633	that’s you. If you had been in Huck’s place you’d ’a’ sneaked down the

8634	hill and never told anybody on the robbers. You can’t do any but mean

8635	things, and you can’t bear to see anybody praised for doing good ones.

8636	There—no thanks, as the widow says”—and Tom cuffed Sid’s ears and helped

8637	him to the door with several kicks. “Now go and tell auntie if you

8638	dare—and tomorrow you’ll catch it!”

8639	

8640	Some minutes later the widow’s guests were at the supper-table, and a

8641	dozen children were propped up at little side-tables in the same room,

8642	after the fashion of that country and that day. At the proper time Mr.

8643	Jones made his little speech, in which he thanked the widow for the

8644	honor she was doing himself and his sons, but said that there was

8645	another person whose modesty—

8646	

8647	And so forth and so on. He sprung his secret about Huck’s share in

8648	the adventure in the finest dramatic manner he was master of, but the

8649	surprise it occasioned was largely counterfeit and not as clamorous and

8650	effusive as it might have been under happier circumstances. However,

8651	the widow made a pretty fair show of astonishment, and heaped so many

8652	compliments and so much gratitude upon Huck that he almost forgot

8653	the nearly intolerable discomfort of his new clothes in the entirely

8654	intolerable discomfort of being set up as a target for everybody’s gaze

8655	and everybody’s laudations.

8656	

8657	The widow said she meant to give Huck a home under her roof and have him

8658	educated; and that when she could spare the money she would start him in

8659	business in a modest way. Tom’s chance was come. He said:

8660	

8661	“Huck don’t need it. Huck’s rich.”

8662	

8663	Nothing but a heavy strain upon the good manners of the company kept

8664	back the due and proper complimentary laugh at this pleasant joke. But

8665	the silence was a little awkward. Tom broke it:

8666	

8667	“Huck’s got money. Maybe you don’t believe it, but he’s got lots of it.

8668	Oh, you needn’t smile—I reckon I can show you. You just wait a minute.”

8669	

8670	Tom ran out of doors. The company looked at each other with a perplexed

8671	interest—and inquiringly at Huck, who was tongue-tied.

8672	

8673	“Sid, what ails Tom?” said Aunt Polly. “He—well, there ain’t ever any

8674	making of that boy out. I never—”

8675	

8676	Tom entered, struggling with the weight of his sacks, and Aunt Polly

8677	did not finish her sentence. Tom poured the mass of yellow coin upon the

8678	table and said:

8679	

8680	“There—what did I tell you? Half of it’s Huck’s and half of it’s mine!”

8681	

8682	The spectacle took the general breath away. All gazed, nobody spoke for

8683	a moment. Then there was a unanimous call for an explanation. Tom said

8684	he could furnish it, and he did. The tale was long, but brimful of

8685	interest. There was scarcely an interruption from any one to break the

8686	charm of its flow. When he had finished, Mr. Jones said:

8687	

8688	“I thought I had fixed up a little surprise for this occasion, but it

8689	don’t amount to anything now. This one makes it sing mighty small, I’m

8690	willing to allow.”

8691	

8692	The money was counted. The sum amounted to a little over twelve thousand

8693	dollars. It was more than any one present had ever seen at one time

8694	before, though several persons were there who were worth considerably

8695	more than that in property.

8696	

8697	

8698	

8699	

8700	CHAPTER XXXV

8701	

8702	

8703	THE reader may rest satisfied that Tom’s and Huck’s windfall made a

8704	mighty stir in the poor little village of St. Petersburg. So vast a

8705	sum, all in actual cash, seemed next to incredible. It was talked

8706	about, gloated over, glorified, until the reason of many of the citizens

8707	tottered under the strain of the unhealthy excitement. Every “haunted”

8708	 house in St. Petersburg and the neighboring villages was dissected,

8709	plank by plank, and its foundations dug up and ransacked for hidden

8710	treasure—and not by boys, but men—pretty grave, unromantic men, too,

8711	some of them. Wherever Tom and Huck appeared they were courted, admired,

8712	stared at. The boys were not able to remember that their remarks had

8713	possessed weight before; but now their sayings were treasured and

8714	repeated; everything they did seemed somehow to be regarded as

8715	remarkable; they had evidently lost the power of doing and saying

8716	commonplace things; moreover, their past history was raked up and

8717	discovered to bear marks of conspicuous originality. The village paper

8718	published biographical sketches of the boys.

8719	

8720	The Widow Douglas put Huck’s money out at six per cent., and Judge

8721	Thatcher did the same with Tom’s at Aunt Polly’s request. Each lad had

8722	an income, now, that was simply prodigious—a dollar for every weekday in

8723	the year and half of the Sundays. It was just what the minister got—no,

8724	it was what he was promised—he generally couldn’t collect it. A dollar

8725	and a quarter a week would board, lodge, and school a boy in those old

8726	simple days—and clothe him and wash him, too, for that matter.

8727	

8728	Judge Thatcher had conceived a great opinion of Tom. He said that no

8729	commonplace boy would ever have got his daughter out of the cave. When

8730	Becky told her father, in strict confidence, how Tom had taken her

8731	whipping at school, the Judge was visibly moved; and when she pleaded

8732	grace for the mighty lie which Tom had told in order to shift that

8733	whipping from her shoulders to his own, the Judge said with a fine

8734	outburst that it was a noble, a generous, a magnanimous lie—a lie that

8735	was worthy to hold up its head and march down through history breast to

8736	breast with George Washington’s lauded Truth about the hatchet! Becky

8737	thought her father had never looked so tall and so superb as when he

8738	walked the floor and stamped his foot and said that. She went straight

8739	off and told Tom about it.

8740	

8741	Judge Thatcher hoped to see Tom a great lawyer or a great soldier some

8742	day. He said he meant to look to it that Tom should be admitted to the

8743	National Military Academy and afterward trained in the best law school

8744	in the country, in order that he might be ready for either career or

8745	both.

8746	

8747	Huck Finn’s wealth and the fact that he was now under the Widow Douglas’

8748	protection introduced him into society—no, dragged him into it, hurled

8749	him into it—and his sufferings were almost more than he could bear. The

8750	widow’s servants kept him clean and neat, combed and brushed, and they

8751	bedded him nightly in unsympathetic sheets that had not one little spot

8752	or stain which he could press to his heart and know for a friend. He had

8753	to eat with a knife and fork; he had to use napkin, cup, and plate;

8754	he had to learn his book, he had to go to church; he had to talk so

8755	properly that speech was become insipid in his mouth; whithersoever he

8756	turned, the bars and shackles of civilization shut him in and bound him

8757	hand and foot.

8758	

8759	He bravely bore his miseries three weeks, and then one day turned up

8760	missing. For forty-eight hours the widow hunted for him everywhere in

8761	great distress. The public were profoundly concerned; they searched high

8762	and low, they dragged the river for his body. Early the third morning

8763	Tom Sawyer wisely went poking among some old empty hogsheads down behind

8764	the abandoned slaughter-house, and in one of them he found the refugee.

8765	Huck had slept there; he had just breakfasted upon some stolen odds and

8766	ends of food, and was lying off, now, in comfort, with his pipe. He was

8767	unkempt, uncombed, and clad in the same old ruin of rags that had made

8768	him picturesque in the days when he was free and happy. Tom routed him

8769	out, told him the trouble he had been causing, and urged him to go home.

8770	Huck’s face lost its tranquil content, and took a melancholy cast. He

8771	said:

8772	

8773	“Don’t talk about it, Tom. I’ve tried it, and it don’t work; it don’t

8774	work, Tom. It ain’t for me; I ain’t used to it. The widder’s good to me,

8775	and friendly; but I can’t stand them ways. She makes me get up just

8776	at the same time every morning; she makes me wash, they comb me all

8777	to thunder; she won’t let me sleep in the woodshed; I got to wear them

8778	blamed clothes that just smothers me, Tom; they don’t seem to any air

8779	git through ’em, somehow; and they’re so rotten nice that I can’t

8780	set down, nor lay down, nor roll around anywher’s; I hain’t slid on a

8781	cellar-door for—well, it ’pears to be years; I got to go to church

8782	and sweat and sweat—I hate them ornery sermons! I can’t ketch a fly in

8783	there, I can’t chaw. I got to wear shoes all Sunday. The widder eats by

8784	a bell; she goes to bed by a bell; she gits up by a bell—everything’s so

8785	awful reg’lar a body can’t stand it.”

8786	

8787	“Well, everybody does that way, Huck.”

8788	

8789	“Tom, it don’t make no difference. I ain’t everybody, and I can’t

8790	_stand_ it. It’s awful to be tied up so. And grub comes too easy—I don’t

8791	take no interest in vittles, that way. I got to ask to go a-fishing;

8792	I got to ask to go in a-swimming—dern’d if I hain’t got to ask to do

8793	everything. Well, I’d got to talk so nice it wasn’t no comfort—I’d got

8794	to go up in the attic and rip out awhile, every day, to git a taste

8795	in my mouth, or I’d a died, Tom. The widder wouldn’t let me smoke;

8796	she wouldn’t let me yell, she wouldn’t let me gape, nor stretch, nor

8797	scratch, before folks—” [Then with a spasm of special irritation and

8798	injury]—“And dad fetch it, she prayed all the time! I never see such a

8799	woman! I _had_ to shove, Tom—I just had to. And besides, that school’s

8800	going to open, and I’d a had to go to it—well, I wouldn’t stand _that_,

8801	Tom. Looky-here, Tom, being rich ain’t what it’s cracked up to be. It’s

8802	just worry and worry, and sweat and sweat, and a-wishing you was dead

8803	all the time. Now these clothes suits me, and this bar’l suits me, and

8804	I ain’t ever going to shake ’em any more. Tom, I wouldn’t ever got into

8805	all this trouble if it hadn’t ’a’ ben for that money; now you just take

8806	my sheer of it along with your’n, and gimme a ten-center sometimes—not

8807	many times, becuz I don’t give a dern for a thing ’thout it’s tollable

8808	hard to git—and you go and beg off for me with the widder.”

8809	

8810	“Oh, Huck, you know I can’t do that. ’Tain’t fair; and besides if you’ll

8811	try this thing just a while longer you’ll come to like it.”

8812	

8813	“Like it! Yes—the way I’d like a hot stove if I was to set on it long

8814	enough. No, Tom, I won’t be rich, and I won’t live in them cussed

8815	smothery houses. I like the woods, and the river, and hogsheads, and

8816	I’ll stick to ’em, too. Blame it all! just as we’d got guns, and a cave,

8817	and all just fixed to rob, here this dern foolishness has got to come up

8818	and spile it all!”

8819	

8820	Tom saw his opportunity—

8821	

8822	“Lookyhere, Huck, being rich ain’t going to keep me back from turning

8823	robber.”

8824	

8825	“No! Oh, good-licks; are you in real dead-wood earnest, Tom?”

8826	

8827	“Just as dead earnest as I’m sitting here. But Huck, we can’t let you

8828	into the gang if you ain’t respectable, you know.”

8829	

8830	Huck’s joy was quenched.

8831	

8832	“Can’t let me in, Tom? Didn’t you let me go for a pirate?”

8833	

8834	“Yes, but that’s different. A robber is more high-toned than what a

8835	pirate is—as a general thing. In most countries they’re awful high up in

8836	the nobility—dukes and such.”

8837	

8838	“Now, Tom, hain’t you always ben friendly to me? You wouldn’t shet me

8839	out, would you, Tom? You wouldn’t do that, now, _would_ you, Tom?”

8840	

8841	“Huck, I wouldn’t want to, and I _don’t_ want to—but what would people

8842	say? Why, they’d say, ‘Mph! Tom Sawyer’s Gang! pretty low characters in

8843	it!’ They’d mean you, Huck. You wouldn’t like that, and I wouldn’t.”

8844	

8845	Huck was silent for some time, engaged in a mental struggle. Finally he

8846	said:

8847	

8848	“Well, I’ll go back to the widder for a month and tackle it and see if I

8849	can come to stand it, if you’ll let me b’long to the gang, Tom.”

8850	

8851	“All right, Huck, it’s a whiz! Come along, old chap, and I’ll ask the

8852	widow to let up on you a little, Huck.”

8853	

8854	“Will you, Tom—now will you? That’s good. If she’ll let up on some of

8855	the roughest things, I’ll smoke private and cuss private, and crowd

8856	through or bust. When you going to start the gang and turn robbers?”

8857	

8858	“Oh, right off. We’ll get the boys together and have the initiation

8859	tonight, maybe.”

8860	

8861	“Have the which?”

8862	

8863	“Have the initiation.”

8864	

8865	“What’s that?”

8866	

8867	“It’s to swear to stand by one another, and never tell the gang’s

8868	secrets, even if you’re chopped all to flinders, and kill anybody and

8869	all his family that hurts one of the gang.”

8870	

8871	“That’s gay—that’s mighty gay, Tom, I tell you.”

8872	

8873	“Well, I bet it is. And all that swearing’s got to be done at midnight,

8874	in the lonesomest, awfulest place you can find—a ha’nted house is the

8875	best, but they’re all ripped up now.”

8876	

8877	“Well, midnight’s good, anyway, Tom.”

8878	

8879	“Yes, so it is. And you’ve got to swear on a coffin, and sign it with

8880	blood.”

8881	

8882	“Now, that’s something _like_! Why, it’s a million times bullier than

8883	pirating. I’ll stick to the widder till I rot, Tom; and if I git to be

8884	a reg’lar ripper of a robber, and everybody talking ’bout it, I reckon

8885	she’ll be proud she snaked me in out of the wet.”

8886	

8887	CONCLUSION

8888	

8889	SO endeth this chronicle. It being strictly a history of a _boy_, it

8890	must stop here; the story could not go much further without becoming the

8891	history of a _man_. When one writes a novel about grown people, he knows

8892	exactly where to stop—that is, with a marriage; but when he writes of

8893	juveniles, he must stop where he best can.

8894	

8895	Most of the characters that perform in this book still live, and are

8896	prosperous and happy. Some day it may seem worth while to take up the

8897	story of the younger ones again and see what sort of men and women they

8898	turned out to be; therefore it will be wisest not to reveal any of that

8899	part of their lives at present.

8900	

8901	

8902
